THE BED PAGE.
The Thrush is a little better—not so very much.
“The Thrush” —it is an attractive title. “A
Periodical for the Publication of Original Poetry ”
—it is an attractive idea. But the editor, T.
Mullett Ellis, is a rank British ass, seem-
ingly devoid of critical faculty; and his first
volume collects verse which is insipid, colourless,
and little beside. Yet Henley, Arthur Symons,
Nora Hopper, and others of proved mettle con-
tribute. But in every case they seem to have
passed their weakest verses to the undiscriminat-
ing Thrush, with the result that there is not one
really good thing in all ten numbers to date. The
editor is fully endowed with British arrogance, and
his aim i 3
to foster in £he masses an appreciation of poetic ex-
pression ; to nurture beauty in their souls; to elevate
their ideas ; to exalt them to highmindedness, and to
attune their critical faculty and their mental ear to the
reception of all the loveliness that is expressible in
exquisite Song. .
And he proceeds to fill his magazine with his own
execrable compositions, like these lines from a 32-
verse “ballad” on “The Siege of Mafeking.”
The defenders’ hearts were “ sound,” it seems—
Sound — but alas ! too surely our armour was unbuckled ;
The British cannon—old scrap iron, not fit for shooting
buns.
Eight thousand Boers, with Cronje, outspanned and
grinned and chuckled,
With their ammunition ready and Krupp’s great modern
guns.
The rest is as bad or worse. And The Thrush
was destined “to foster in the masses an appre-
ciation of poetic expression ; to nurture beauty in
their souls,” eta ! —and T. Mullett Ellis “passes
on with pity ” when The Saturday Review jibes
The Thrush looks like a private venture, and at
latest it was pleading desperately for public sup-
port. Sometimes the public is singularly wise.
The Muse Misguided.
h
So! there’s no Laureate of Australia
Who may wear the green crown of the gum,
Though scores in her vast penetralia
Take as kindly to rhyme as to rum !
There are poets who ride through the mulga,
Who camp 'neath the gidgea and gum,
And many a one from the Culg’a
Goes westward a-humping his drum.
There's a bard with each big mob of cattle
That comes from the plains of the West;
There are bards where the dryblowers rattle,
And a bard keeps the “ Travellers’ Rest.”
There’s a poet in every region
Who in verse will set everything down,
And yet there’s not one of the legion
Deemed worthy the eucalypt crown 1
So we drink till we get the blue devils,
Till editors turn and abuse, —
For the fear is borne in our revels,
Some rascal has swallowed the Muse f
E.S.S.
The Sweated Newspaper-Man.
WITHIN the last few years, whilst circu-
lations and advertisements of Sydney
newspapers have steadily risen, the
wages of daily-paper employees on the
big daily papers have steadily fallen. The com-
positor, promised princely remuneration if he
submitted quietly to the linotype, finds an average
of £4 or £4 10s. a week hard to maintain ; whereas
in the old days £6 was easily earned. The clerks
in the business department have suffered also from
general retrenchment. But the journalist is the
chief victim of newspaper sweating. A decade ago
the standard wage for a moderately-skilled Sydney
pressman was £6 a week; and salaries ranged
from that to £B, £lO, £l5, and much higher for
special officials. A reporter valued at less than
£6 on a morning paper was a rarity. Now rates
stand at about this level—
Evening paper staff : From nothing a-week (to an im-
prover), by small, painfully small, gradations to from £7
to £lO or £l2 a-week for an editor. Great plentitude of
boy-reporters getting £1 or £1 10s. a-week.
Morning paper staff: From £1 a-week (to the beginner)
to £7 a-week for the tip-top men of the staff ; a few sala-
ries of £8 to £lO for “ officials ” ; up to £2O for an editor.
A typical reporting- staff would be paid thus : Two at £7,
two at £6, two at £5, two at £4 10s., ten more at from £1
to £3 10s.
And there is a strong tendency on most
morning and evening papers to continue to reduce
the number of fairly-salaried men. One paper
which lost three decentlv-paid men within a few
months replaced them all with boys. In fact, the
production of the “ boy reporter ” is now a matter
of business on some of the daily papers. Messen-
ger boys are engaged at from 7s. Gd. to 10s. a
week to carry copy, &c. ; and any showing signs
of smartness are taught shorthand, then drafted
as clerks into one of the business departments.
After a spell there, they are turned on to “the
staff,” beginning with £1 a week, with the pro-
spect (but not the certainty) of small annual
increases.
It would be a fair generalisation to say that the
wage of a senior reporter (required to write short-
hand, 120 words a minute ; to understand enough
of the English language to write a decent paragraph
or report; to dress decently ; to mingle with rich
people so as to “ keep up the reputation of the
office ”; to live so close to the city that he can
walk home after midnight; to be proof always
against the temptation of a proffered “tenner”;
to exercise a skilled judgment as to the compara-
tive value of what he hears at a meeting) is on an
average £4 10s. a week in Sydney—something
iess in Adelaide and Brisbane ; something more in
Melbourne, where the journalist’s wage is the
highest in Australia.
As to the hours of labour, they are almost in-
finite. The sub-editor of one “great daily,”
asked to state for the information of the pro-
prietary what were the hours worked by the
various reporters, gave in each instance the reply :
“ To come to duty at 10 a.m. and work until 3
a.m., if required.” That was somewhat more
picturesque than exact, but it gave a fair indica-
tion of the length of a day’s work on a morning
newspaper. A reporter is liable at any time to
be kept at work from 10 a.m. to 3 a.m., and even
later during the Parliamentary session ; and his
average hours on duty are 80 a week. This then
is the recompense for skill, education, exercise of
judgment, clean linen, general presentableness,
absolute honesty and fairness —90 shillings for 80
hours’ work !—one shilling, one penny, one half-
penny per hour!
How is it done ? Well, as a matter of fact, it is
not done. The senior reporter of a daily news-
paper should be a good, clear writer; with a wide
general knowledge to enable him to appreciate
the important points of a medical meeting to dis-
cuss consumption, of a bankers’ assembly to talk
finance, of a church gathering to talk scandal, of
a Judge’s summing-up and its legal points ; with
a sure, clear judgment to know what to leave out
and what to put in; with a sense of personal
responsibility, of honour, and of dignity to
carry him through temptations of drink, of
bribery, of social influence. In addition, he
should be well read in current events and
current literature, and know enough to ignore
the Latin quotation or the French sentence
of an affected speaker. But whereas a decade
ago he generally filled nearly all these conditions,
now he generally does n’t. Some of the old, a
few of the new school, still commanding fairly
decent wages, maintain the old tradition. But
most of the new race of smartish youths—
ill-paid, over-worked, with never enough leisure
to read if they had the inclination-fail to be
skilled journalists in several respects, and in Aus-
tralia the status of journalism as a profession
steadily deteriorates. The law of supply and
demand, sometimes slow in operation, is generally
safe and sure, and gradually the daily newspapers
are getting for the average Is. an hour, or less,
that they pay as a sweating wage, just about the
sort of man they deserve. Skilled journalists may
be noticed on all sides abandoning their profession
for the civil service, for trade, for anything ; and
their places are'taken by men of callow brains and
boyish judgment, who submit to be sweated on a
daily newspaper just as they would submit to be
sweated in a wool-store, because their measure of
ability gives no chance of relief. There are still,
of course, many excellent men on the various
papers, but their number is fast diminishing, and
will continue to diminish whilst present conditions
last.
In driving away their good men, the Sydney
daily papers are really draining away their own
life-blood. The result is seen in papers gradually
growing bigger and bigger—their sheets increasing
as the capacity of the staff to put the day’s news
in a bright, terse form decreases; in contents
daily growing flatter and flatter; in the Steadily
increasing crop of inaccuracies, banalities, stupid
blunders, glaring mistakes of policy. What
money is saved in staff salaries is more
than absorbed in increased paper-bills, in
libel-costs, and in other leakages. Yet the
foolish process of starving the brains of a
paper, and spending the savings on its feet, is
persisted in—company-managed organs leading
the way, those proprietaries where the chief men
have been themselves practical journalists follow-
ing reluctantly. The results are, for the moment,
injurious enough. When the time comes for
some enterprising man of money to start in each
of the capitals papers whose chief line of policy
will be to pick the eyes out of the existing press
—to get the smartest men available and pay
the highest wages possible, then the foolish-
ness of the cheap-jack policy will be fully
apparent.

Ibsen’s dramas, formerly 2s. 6d. and 3s. 6d., are
reduced to Is. 6d. and 2s. 6d. in the new English
edition which Walter Scott is publishing. And
the new edition is thoroughly revised and freshly
printed. Probably it will remain Wilhapi Archer s
best title to fame that he was Ibsen’s English
apostle.
Say, what is Art ? The bubble on the wine f
Bloom on the plum ?
Worm in the bud ?
The limelight emphasis on Earth’s divine ?
Crust of the crumb?
Juice of the cud ?
Spectacles ’twixt dim eye and Nature’s page?
Wide of the mark each time, replies the Sage.
Say rather : eye-piece of kaleidoscope.
Wind on the spark.
Wing of the seed.
A finger-post of fire i’ the murky grope?
Th’ ascendant star in Beauty’s horoscope ?
Song of the lark ?
Somethwig indeed
Of joy there is in Art, but more of ruth :
Love’s starveling groping for the breast of Truth.

H.Gr.: As to the Australian Race. My ex-
perience teaches me that the child born in Australia is
either an Australian in every sense of the word, or else.an
aggravated type of Britisher. The biggest Jingo I ever
met was an Australian born of a Russian father and a
German mother. The most characteristic Australian
larrikin in Melbourne bears a French name and is the off-
spring of a Parisian (of the most stagey French type) and
a Norwegian woman. Of course, lam not speaking of the
German colonies in S.A. and Q., where the children rarely
see anybody but Germans.
“Jobber” : As to Lindsay’s way of drawing a
saddle on the ground, note that Mahony makes the same
blunder with the full-page illustration of “ Any Chance
of a Feed Boss?” in Bulletin Xmas number. Any man
who would destroy a saddle by throwing it down on its
flaps, instead of putting it on end, would soon be hunted
from a decent station.

BRIGHT’S DISEASE
For the full understanding of the curative action
of Warner’s Safe Cure, it is necessary to bear in
mind the processes of nature by which human life
is maintained. Reduced to their essential prin-
ciples, these processes are simply the wasting and
renewal of the tissues of the body.
It is absolutely necessary to life that the body
should waste away, and that its substance should
be renewed by food, water, and fresh air. Night
and day this process of waste and replaceman goes
on unceasingly. Every time a man draws breath
his body gains in weight by the oxygen, etc., taken
from the air, and every time he breathes out it
loses weight by the carbonic acid and moisture
given forth.
Insensibly, too, the body is losing weight all the
time. You can prove this by placing your hand
an a cold piece of polished glass, when you will
Bee that moisture given forth by the skin has con-
densed. Were the body to be entirely covered
with a coat of waterproof varnish, death would
quickly result.
Apart from the skin and lungs, the organs em-
ployed in this process of w iste are the kidneys and
liver , and the maintcn nice of those organs in
thorough working order is the grand secret of good
health.
The Kidneys. —The function which the kidneys
perform is to filter the blood and otherwise
separate from it the surplus water and the various
impurities which it contains. These impurities
are urea, uric acid, and mineral matter, which,
with water, constitute the urine.
When the kidneys have done their work, the
purified blood is delivered to the veins, and is
returned to the heart, having in its course
traversed the body and become laden with a fresh
supply of waste matter. The heart again delivers
the blood to the kidneys, which once more filter
it and extract the impurities. This process is
continuous.
The Livek. —The part the liver plays in the
process of waste is chiefly the manufacturing from
the blood the liquid called “Bile,” which is
delivered by the liver into the intestines, and acts
as a natural cathartic.
The liver and kidneys are very sensitive organs.
Sudden chills, fever, alcoholic intemperance, and
tropical heat are all liable to congest the liver and
reduce it to an abnormal condition. The kidneys
are exceedingly susceptible to inflammation. An
ordinary feverish cold, which closes the pores and
hinders the skin in performing its share in the
removal of the waste products of the body, throws
a burden of extra work upon the kidneys which
they may be unable to perform. Influenza, or
any kind of fever, taxes the kidneys unduly, and
may cause them to break down under the strain.
Any inflammation of the kidneys is very apt to
affect the liver, and vice versa.
Should the liver or kidneys, or both, become
unable to fulfil their purpose thoroughly, then the
blood becomes laden with urinary and biliary
poisons, which are carried to every part of the
body.
Sometimes these poisons become deposited in
the joints or tissues, and cause Rheumatism,
Lumbago, Sciatica, or Gout. In other cases the
blood, being contaminated, is in an unfit condition
to absorb nutriment, which causes Anaemia,
Neuralgia, digestive troubles, and Debility. In
other cases the supply of bile to the intestines
being inadequate, Constipation, with its attendant
evils result; in others, Biliousness. In others
again the impurity of the blood develops boils and
skin eruptions. Sometimes the uric acid is
deposited in the kidneys and produces Stone,
Gravel, and urinary and bladder troubles.
The reason, then, that Warner’s Safe Cure cures
so many apparently diverse disorders is that they
all proceed from one cause, namely—the inability
of the kidneys or liver to do their work properly,
and so rid the blood of these poisons in the
manner provided by nature.
Warner’s Safe Cure has complete mastery over
functional and chronic liver and kidney derange-
ments. and by restoring those vital organs to
health and activity, does away with the cause of
these several complaints. When the kidneys and
liver are efficiently carrying out the wonderful
scheme of work allotted to them by nature, none
of the disorders here enumerated can exist.

relating to Warner’s Safe Cure and the diseases it
cures will be sent post free to any address on
application to H. 11. Warner and Co., Limited,
Australasian Branch, 528 to 532 Little Lonsdale

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Epilogue.
We fear not death. We have no tears
For perished hopes. We dread the cold,
On-coming, uneventful years
When even Love himself grows old.
Gnarled Age is near, a grisly sphinx
With stony, ruthless eyes of hate.
(What was her name, that little minx
Who wrecked my life, in Eighty-eight ?)
We care no whit for garish Fame,
The crowd’s applause can’t trouble us ;
The “fit and few” are much the same,
Their praise and censure frivolous.
We’ve lost our heritage of Youth :
That knowledge stings. . (Her eyes were blue:
That other one, the gentle Ruth,
Who broke my heart, in Ninety-two.)
We’re old and hipped and out-of date,
Our wind is scant, our hair is thin ;
And Prudence guards at every gate
Domains we used to wander in.
We’re cheerful as unfeathered crows
In wormless deserts dead-alive . . .
(Now, was it Dorothy, or Rose,
That killed my faith, in Ninety-five ?)
Within the garden of my heart
The trees are leafless all and dead,
And Memory, sitting pale apart,
Weeps for old joys forever fled. . . .
There is a girl across the way,
A lass with eyes that plead and sue,
Who .. . hang it! I will call to-day :
My pulse leaps still. Here’s luck, Nought-two!
T.
FRANK MORTON.
Myra’s Eyes.
He turned his mind from paintings rare
And carven things with strange old looks,
And came from out the musty lair
And witching dust of ancient books
To look on love in noon-time light,
And brown-gold tints of fading day,
And soft and saintly shades of night
That limned her tenderly in grey.
In which of these she was most fair
He could not say; but, did he call
Another lady to compare,
Then Myra was most fair in all.
Her heavy hair was black, her breast
As white as moonlit lily glows.
The pale exotic’s grace, the zest,
The smouldering ardour of the rose
Were blent in her. Her eyes’ deep goal
Such vague enchantments held that he
Was mazed in them. They stirred his soul
Like some old Eastern mystery.
Their purple shadow harboured sleep,
He peered in them as one who braves
The dizzy dangers of the steep,
Or darksome pools in ocean caves ;
And ever strove, by night and day,
With fond devotion to surprise
The wonders that in secret lay
Behind the dusk of Myra’s eyes.
He read within them wisdom, all
The magic of the East, and she
Could never speak a word too small
To kernel some philosophy.
They wed ; and he for one year round
Explored his dream-built wonderland,
Then said, in awe, that he had found
The thing he could not understand.
But came a hopeless day when he
Looked on those orbs in bitter stress,
And in their misty deeps did see
A drear and sterile emptiness.
And then he laughed : “Most learned and wise !
Hast with a soulless creature toyed,
And through the windows of her eyes
Looked three long years into the void ! ”
Y.
EDWARD DYSON.
Faith, Love, and Death.
Grey dawn, and lucent star, that slowly paled
Beyond the utmost flight of fervent prayers,
When boyhood’s heart looked up to heaven
through tears
Of joy, to see the glory of God unveiled :
High noon, and bridal earth, whose footsteps
failed
For very love —when passionate hopes and fears
Dazzled the flowers, made music in the ears,
And through the tranced wood their splendor
trailed:
Calm eventide—afar the lonely West
Dreams of the wondrous day, and dreaming, lies
With silent lips, and softly-closed eyes,
Lapped in the shadow of eternal rest:
Childhood, and youth, and age, for each a prize ;
Faith, love, and death—l know not which is best.
N.S.W.
DOWELL O'REILLY.
On a Melbourne school examination :
“What are the Pacific Islands?” Youthful
cynic: “England and Ireland.”

“How McDougal Topped the Score’ —
“My Mate Bill”—“The Billiard Marker’s

“Alex. M.” writes :
While not entirely sharing the elocutionary enthusiasm
of “ J E ” (B. 30/11/’01), I think that your foot-note some-
what misapprehends him. Elocution, in its proper sense,
has nothing- to do with eloquence. It is concerned merely
with the production of vocal sounds as sounds,
and without any regard to collocation of words.
Therefore, though I heartily agree with you that
we have already in Australia vastly too much
“illoquence” and public palaver generally, I see
no reason for supposing—good enunciation, articulation,
and vowel-tone being admittedly desirable for speech in
its ordinary applications—that scholastic attention to
these matters would involve any considerable addition to
the ranks of Blatherskite. Indeed, my own experience
is that English and Spanish, at least, are most accurately
pronounced by men of unobtrusively comprehensive
intellect and analytical cast, whereas the man who runs
to “ oratory ” is mostly a synthesist, and a bad one at
that—even'when his flourishes do not represent (as in
sundry notable local instances) mere gorgeously-arranged
intellectual indigence.
After which The B. begs to remark again that it
does not care a rap what enunciation, articulation
or vowel-tone a man uses to say “ Pass the salt ”
—so long as he can shut up gracefully : and it
has never heard of a man learning to shut up by
lessons in elocution.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia and Maori land.
In consequence* of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
SI, with 2s. 3d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
O wing to the imposition of\\d. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease of in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage—tyd. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight- now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed'to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J F.
Archibald.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
" Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns m length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient

any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid ‘for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, hut unfitted for direct reproduction
.— i,e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will he paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 21, 1901.
More Troops for South Africa.
A CABLEGRAM published on Thursday of last
week reports a new phase of the Ketchener
campaign in South Africa. Hitherto we
have heard much of the burning of houses
and crops, and the wholesale destruction of
private property—of the demolition of the
belongings of non-combatants, the reduc-
tion of widows and orphans to penury because
the British soldiers laid in ashes those of
their belongings which they couldn’t carry
away, and the other features of a campaign
compared to which that of Mehemet and
his son, Ibrahim the Damned, in Greece,
doesn’t look quite so bad as it used to do.
But still it was alleged that there was a
redeeming feature in the case. When the
Boer woman had been reduced, in an hour
or so, from, comfort to beggary, and her
household treasures had been looted and her
cherished possessions burnt, there was still
the concentration camp—the awful invention
of Weyler, the Spanish butcher of Cuba —as a
place of alleged refuge for her and her
daughters. There the food was bad and
scanty, the shelter insufficient, the surround-
ings foul and unwholesome, and the death-
rate worse than in the worst days of the
Plague—but still there was a semblance of
shelter. Thu cablegram (the Jingo papers’
cablegram) of last Thursday states that even
this is now being denied. It sets forth that
the army of humane and chivalrous Britain
now pursues the cheaper policy of burning
the houses, outhouses, and crops, the furni-
ture, clothing, bedding, and all other
supplies that can’t be carried away, and
leaving the women and children to perish in
the blackened wilderness. The nights are
bitterly cold even at this season on that
tableland, from 3000 to 6000 feet above sea-
level : it was in this region that the well-
fed Australian soldier almost mutinied at
being only allowed two blankets. It is the
wet season ; there is no fuel ; the very
occasional passing of a Boer com-
mando, which itself can find no food in
that desolated land, is the only chance of
assistance. It is a horrible story, and
Europe has heard nothing much worse of
any European race since Tilly captured
Magdeburg, in the days of very long ago.
Even Paskievich, whom no one suspected
of mercy or charity, treated non-combatants
more kindly in Poland—though he probably
didn’t intend doing so.
* * * •
This is the sort of literature that comes
now from the seat of war—literature that
reads curiously when we remember that it is
written by a British soldier (from Australia)
about the new British war-methods, and is
published with tacit approval by the papers
of an English-speaking community. No
word of reprobation accompanies it—not
even a line to .state that a grieved editor
trusts it may not be true, or that, at worst,
it is only an isolated instance :
Ah ! here’s open country at last. Johnny Bohr won’t
follow us here, for we are superior to him in point of
numbers, so we halt for half-an-hour, and burn a stray
farmhouse as some slight relief to our feelings. It is now
raining steadily—a rain that is like melted snow—and the
unfortunate women and children, whom we found in the
farmhouse, are left behind in the wet, without blankets
or shelter of any kind. Forward once again into the rain
and darkness ; it’s much too cold for us to ride, so we
sling our rifles to our saddles and trudge forward on foot.
It was so wet and so bitterly cold that even
sturdy, well-clothed men, with food and a
prospect of shelter ahead of them, could not
sit in their saddles without freezing. And
the British soldiery deliberately threw
children out into that fearful night to perish
in the rain, without provisions or blankets
or any place of refuge. And this, according
to the cable intelligence, is not an isolated
instance, but the regular mode of “ warfare ”
now adopted by Butcher Ketchener s bat-
talions—with no serious protest from the
bulk of the British nation !
* * * •
And the reply of the callous Jingo is that
these things are necessary to make the Boers
knock under, and if they absolutely sub-
mitted such things wouldn’t happen. Which
is true to the letter ? It always is true in
such cases. And Alva wouldn’t have made
his name an execration to all time in the
Netherlands if the Dutch had submitted—
it was religious freedom instead of
political freedom that was at issue there,
but the position was exactly the same. And
Mehemet and his son Ibrahim wouldn’t
have set all Greece on fire if the Greeks
had been properly submissive all along.
And Paskievich wouldn’t have desolated
Poland if the Poles had been obe-
dient to their conquerors. And the
Inquisition wouldn't have burnt anybody
if everybody had knocked under ; and the
Austrian rule in Italy would probably have
been gentle enough, and Weyler wouldn’t
have gone butchering in Cuba, and Cortez
wouldn’t have tortured the Aztec emperor
on his bed of live coals, and Mary of
England wouldn’t have fried anybody at the
stake—if the parties concerned had sub-
mitted. Even Tilly, whose dwarf figure
and huge drooping green feather have cast a
baleful shadow across 300 years of history,
only sacked Magdeburg because the Magde-
burgers wouldn’t give way and concede the
point at issue ; and Cromwell’s appalling
massacres of men, women and children in
Ireland rested on the same good and reliable
foundation; and there was an equally
honorable and Christian excuse for the holo-
caust of St. Bartholomew’s Day. Even
the brigand wouldn’t kill his prisoner if that
unreasoning person would hand over what is
required. In the case of the brigand’s
prisoner it is the ransom ; in the Boer’s case
it is the country—the Rand, with all its
stupendously magnificent probabilities. “ I
predict,” wrote one of the world’s greatest
mining experts, six years ago, “that by
1910 the Rand will be turning out over
£50,000,000 of gold annually.”
* * • *
The beginning of the South African war is
now a matter of ancient history. Even if
the Boers commenced the war without im-
mediate justification—a matter which is
open to much dispute—they had the excuse
that Britain had (apart, from the officially-
winked-at Jamieson raid) twice made war
on them, and had taken from them Natal
and the Cape Colony. At the worst, the
Boers were seeking to recover what had till
quite recently been their own—just as
Garibaldi and Cavour did when they
strove to recover Lombardy and Venice
from the Austrians, and as the Greeks
did, and the Poles, and many other
people spoken kindly of in history.
The penalty of unsuccessful war, no matter
what may be the merits of the case, is gener-
ally the payment of an indemnity and the
cession of part of the vanquished State’s
territory. If the Germans had resolved to
annex all France in 1871, or all Austria in
1866, they would also have had to deluge
the land with blood for years before they
crushed out the last embers of resistance,
and they would probably have degener-
ated into utter savagery in the course of
the struggle. So would Britain if it had
attempted to seize all France, or Spain,
when it ended its last successful campaign
against those countries. The demand for
the surrender of everything, as the price of
something so very ordinary as an unsuccess-
ful campaign, must drive a brave people to
the last extremity of resistance. It is a
demand which no white man’s State has
made upon any other white man’s State
since the execrated partition of Poland and the
atrocious doings ofNAPOLEONI. a demand as
outrageously beyond precedent according to
the ideas of the times, as was Alva’s demand
that the Netherlanders should give up their
faith according to the mode of thinking in
his day.
• * * *
And even if it is for the world’s benefit,
which is more than doubtful, that the Boer
States should be annexed, is not Britain an
alleged Christian country—a follower of the
Man of Peace —a bitter and inveterate spreader
of that Gospel which forbids the world to do
evil that good may come ? Truly, it is time
for England to burn the Bible publicly by the
hands of the common hangman, and go back
to the worship of Thor and Odin, so that
its faith may square with its foreign policy,
and its creed and its Ketchener’s gallows
won’t look so hopelessly incongruous. It is
time for it to sell its churches for drinking
saloons, and its bishops as a job lot in a
declmingmarket, and to take any price that is
offering for the national drama in which the
British hero declares that whoever lays his
hand on a woman, except in kindness, is un-
worthy to bear the English name. The
country which used to boast of its admira-
tion for a gallant enemy, and which sen-
tences men to confiscation of their fortunes
and life long banishment, and sometimes to
execution, for doing what Sir Richard
Grenville did, and the defenders of
Lucknow, and the Light Brigade,
and almost every other hero in its
Pantheon —keeping up their end of an ap-
parently hopeless fight—has no use for
bishops. The country which grovelled in
adoration before the Hungarian Kossuth
and the Italian Garibaldi for attempt-
ing to recover their native lands from a
foreign ruler, and hangs the Cape
Boer who does likewise, has no need for
churches. The nation which has got down to
making war on women and children, and
killing them by the slow torture of cold and
hunger and exposure, wants a different
creed and a ijew kind of national drama. In
these respects it acts very much as do other
nations in like conditions—only it strikes a
higher moral attitude than most of its
contemporaries, and sends out more mission-
aries than a dozen ordinary States, and it is
a cold day when Britain has to plead in its
defence that it is no worse than the
Spaniard, and the drill - sergeant-ridden
German, and the American in whose country
men are still burned alive by lynch law with
public opinion behind it. Nobody even
pretends that the women and children
and infants whom Ketchener’s brutish
policy is killing off wholesale are known to
have relatives carrying arms—supposing
th"t would be any excuse in the case of a
Christian people which professes that it
doesn’t war on women and babies. The
women who were believed to have male
relatives in the field were already provided
for by taking them prisoners and putting
them and their children on reduced rations
—as explained by the British Government in
the House of Commons. The campaign has
degenerated into a wholesale looting, burn-
ing and ejection—after the manner of the
Hun of over 1000 years ago, who left no
grass behind him.
* * * *
And Australia is invited by a crawlsome
Minister to send another 5000 men to help at
burning the Boer woman’s house—there
are practically none save women and
children left in these farm-houses now
—and casting her out with her daughters
and her infant in arms to perish in the
bitter nights on the Transvaal tableland.
There is no longer even a semblance of the
silly pretence that “the Empire is in
danger ” —though the suggestion that it was
ever in danger from two isolated inland
communities whose population was less than
that of Sydney or Melbourne was the
grossest and most foolish insult ever directed
at the Empire. And Australia hasn’t even
the bad excuse that it is led astray by a
hunger after territory, for it won’t get the
annexed territories. It’s only possible ex-
planation will be that it likes the work of
burning houses and reducing helpless non-
combatants to misery or death —or else that
it contains a large number of people who are
willing to do any kind of hangman’s
undertaking for 4s. 6d. or ss. Bd.
a day, and a larger number who are
willing that they should do it. As for
those who are trying to engineer the under-
taking, they don’t propose to go and expose
their well-cared-for skins to the chance of a
Boer bullet, nor do they propose to pay —
the bill will merely make another addition
to the deficit, which is constantly being
handed down to posterity. They will
annex what glory they can gbt, and any title
that comes their way, and go to church
regularly, and still put their faith in a gospel
which says that we should not do evil that
good may come. And if there is a Here-
after—but, on second thoughts, it will be a
great deal better for many Australian poli-
ticians if there is no Hereafter. It may be
very long and very lonesome if there is
nothing ahead but countless ages of lying
still, and flat, and silent under the loam,
but even that should be better than meeting
the long array of the ghosts of little children
who died homeless in the cold and rain up on
the veldt, while the farm-burner rode away
to burn out more helpless mothers “ for the
Glory of the Empire.”
Australian Trades Dis-Unionism.
Dear Bulletin.— Your remark (19/10/’01), “Pro-
vided the unions don’t lose their heads,” etc..
touches the weak spot in Australian trade-union-
ism. As too many cooks are said to spoil the
broth, so too manv heads spoil Unionism. Take
N.S.W. First, there is the Sydney Trades Coun-
cil, then the Eight-Hour Union, then the A.W.U.
(which embraces the bushworkers of N.S.W.,
Vic., and S.A.), the Federated Seamen’s Union,
the Newcastle Colliers’ Federation, the Broken
Hill Miners and Trades Council, the South Coast
miners, the mountain miners, and each and every
one of the isolated, unaffiliated, or unfederated
unions in town or country. Each of these bodies,
with the exception of the A.W.U. and F.S.U., is a
purely local government, having no fixed connec-
tion with inter-State unions or federations or
council or what not.
• * * *
Victoria is in the same state. It has a Mel-
bourne Trades Council, its branches of the
A.W.U., and its Miners’ and other go-alone
Unions. Queensland has no Trades Council or
other Central Union body in the metropolis. All
the little one-horse Unions go alone, with the ex-
ception of two or three affiliated with the Aus-
tralian Labor Federation, which embraces the
Bushworkers, several Miners’ Unions and one or
two coastal combinations. The Seaman’s Union,
of course, exists, and the Engineers, which is a
world-wide, and not a purely Australian affair, as
also is the Amalgamated Carpenters.
* * * *
South Australia has a Trades Council, a branch
A.W.U., a branch Seamens’, Amalgamated Car-
penters’, Engineers’ and other Unions. Wes-
tralia has its Perth and Goldfields Trades
Councils, Miners, and Workers’ Unions, and all
the other more or less united or dis-united unions
and combined or disconnected combinations.
And this is Australian Unionism ! The whole
business, so far as the great motto, “Union is
Strength, ” is concerned, is a farce.
* * • *
Union seamen work alongside blackleg wharf-
laborers at Brisbane. Union colliers and lumpers
and trimmers load coal at Newcastle, to be un-
loaded by non-unionists at Albany, where the
local Union men are on strike. If Broken Hill or
Charters Towers mines require free labor to
combat a strike they can get it from Ballarat or
Moonta ; or vice versa. A miner can blackleg at
Newcastle and work Union at Wollongong ; and,
after scabbing at Wollongong, he can go Union at
Broken Hill. A.W.U. men, so the Queensland
men say, take Union tickets in N.S.W. and scab
in Queensland. The A.W.U. men of New South
Wales accuse the Queensland men of doing ditto.
• * • *
There- is talk at present of a Federation
of Wharf-Laborers, another of bakers, and o_
other trades, upon lines similar to the Sea'
men’s Federation. These isolated Trade Fede-
rations are certainly a step towards the right
thing ; but until all the miners, and bush-workers,
andothertradesthroughoutthe Commonwealth are
enrolled as individual Federations under a grand
Commonwealth Federated Trades-Unionism, as a
social or political power they will remain practically
ineffective. Every one of these little Unions has
a head, and a federation with a common industrial,
social, and political policy is the only way to pre-
vent their numberless heads clashing—getting
broken, and lost.
* * » *
There is nothing utopian about the idea ; nothing
more than there was in the National Federal idea.
Already, the Political Labor movement —which
stands apart from the Industrial movement, though
there is no reason why it should do so —has had
its Federal Congress, and drawn up and defined
its platform and attitude. The platform is prac-
tically to give Trades-Union principles the force
of statute law. But no Federal Trades-Union
backs that platform up. The wrangling—or at
least the tacit agreement among the various trade
bodies not to come to a common agreement,
even on the plainest question of wages, deprives
the Federal Political Labor movement of that
support.
* * * », 4
Some of the foolish Unions —who apparently
don’t want to see cheap labor, long hours, sweat-
ing, &c., abolished, and fair wages, &c., substituted
by statute law —eschew politics. More, many of
the rankest Freeraiders, Fairtectionists, Free-
tectionists, and other Conservatives _ flourish in
Trade-Union ranks. Some Unions, in fact, are
strictly old-fashioned and Conservative, and are
mere benefit societies. Other Unions and union-
ists (principally the Bush, Miners’, and Maritime
Unions) are Democratic to the backbone.
• • • *
And so, what can be said of Australian Union
ism ? Such a thing doesn’t exist —and more’s the
pity ! —X.O.
[Machinery does exist, has existed for some
years, for a general Federation of Australian
Labor Unions ; but a constitution apparently all
right in theory didn’t work well in practice. As-
to the political side of the Labor movement, many
of the Unions were frightened out of that by the
efforts of some schemers to “jump” the claim
and make it an appendage of the Freetrade party.
Most of what “X. 0.” says can be summed up
thus: Trades Unionists, like other people, are
human, and are prevented by various jealousies
from always pulling together; but, when a big
occasion does arise, they generally act with splen-
did brotherhood. —Ed. B.]
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The “Permanent and Reproductive”
Tomb.
Among the items described as “permanent and
reproductive works ” to be paid for out of loans in
New South Wales this year is one which reads
“ Memorial for soldiers who fell in the South
African war, £20,000.” This appears specially
under the “ permanent and reproductive ” head-
ing. It represents about the last possible depth
of degradation to which even a conscience-
less Ministry can descend, and Parliament
and the people share the degradation. The men
who charge a tombstone to posterity as a perma-
nent AND REPRODUCTIVE undertaking are
possessed of a brassy insolence that would do credit
to a book-hawker, and a want of any decent politi-
cal conscience which might shame a burglar in the
higher walks of his profession. And to what a
sordid depth has descended the Jingo enthusiasm
of a year or two ago ! It is only about that length
of time since most of Australia was waving an
intoxicated brolly and holding forth at the top of
a thick voice about the “sacrifices” it was pre-
pared to make for the Empire, and New South
Wales was shouting with the best of them. What
unutterable scorn did these wild and bounding
merchants and importers and shareholders pour
on the head of the person who wasn’t prepared,
like them, to make unlimited “ sacrifices ” for the
empire, and how anxious they were to shed their
last drop of blood in the cause, and to guarantee
that the cohentingenter, when he came back from
serving the empire, would find his billet waiting
for him, and all the rest ? And then what a fall


ensued! Queensland paid the wages of its
contingent by the simple process of adding
them to the deficit, and borrowing money to
pay the deficit, and leaving posterity to pay
the borrowed money ! Victoria got over the
difficulty by cutting down its Old Age Pensions
and temporarily suspending the Act which pro-
vides for the repayment of the old deficit—thereby
leaving posterity to make up the difference as
usual. New South Wales added a lot of its war
expenditure to the old deficit, and covered the
rest by suspending a large portion of the pay-
ments in reduction of the said deficit, so, both
ways, it left posterity to pay the bill. And now
it is proposed that posterity should pay even for
the big slab of stone with which certain loyal
political bounders intend to celebrate the war
they didn’t go to and didn’t pay for. Isn’t there
a shred of honor, or conscience, or decency, or
honesty, or even the vestige of a sense of
humor, in the dull-witted N.S.W. Ministry,
which thus proposes to nobly sacrifice its
lOTT in the cause of Imperialism, and to stick
up its permanent and reproductive tombstone
for all the world to laugh at, with the pawn-
ticket adhering conspicuously to the side of it?
The Bulletin reckons that it is the best loyalist
and the best Imperialist and the best Jingo in
Australia, after all. It loathes the whole war
business, but, since there has been a war, it is
willing to see extra taxation put on, and to pay its
share of that extra taxation, in order to liquidate
the bill at once, so that this generation may settle
up for its own folly. It is willing even to pay its
share of the ridiculous monument if the rest of
N.S.W. will do likewise, seeing that it appears to
be decided that the silly object must be erected—
to keep alive a subject that a wiser community
would gladly forget. And it can’t find one Jingo
of them all who is willing to part with a copper
for either of these purposes.

Lunatics, Criminals—and Others.
Two inquiries held, the other day, in Victoria dis-
close the utter incompetence of the administration
of important departments by the Government of
the day. The first related to the allegation of ill-
treatment of a patient in the Metropolitan Lunatic
Asylum at Kew. Premier Peacock appointed
the three visiting medicos a Board to investigate.
The charge was not sustained, but, having decided
that point, this unusual Board went much further
than was intended, and got considerable inform-
ation for its trouble. Nearly all the witnesses—
from Inspector-General downwards—agreed as to
the shameful state of affairs at the asylum.
Built originally to accommodate 500 patients, it
now holds 865. For these and the *l3O atten-
dants there are a medical superintendent and
two assistant medicos, one a temporary officer.
No head nurse ha,d been appointed for 18
months, and the acting nurse is not certificated.
There were no trained nurses in the hospital
wards for 15 months at a stretch. Nobody had
ever seen any air or water cushions used, and
there are no baths on wheels nor any plunge-
baths in the hospital. There are no means of
warming or cooling a room. There are two airing
yards not in use at all, while the one used
is overcrowded, the reason for this piece of
foolishness being allegedly, the parsimony of
the Government in not making certain necessary
alterations. In one ward the board found 23
patients sleeping, six of whom had beds on the
floor because there was no room for bedsteads.
The single rooms were practically unventilated,
while many of the closets required immediate
repair. Out of the 46 nurses there was not one
with a recognised certificate. Last month there
were no less than 26 cases of bruises, &c., due, the
superintendent opined, to inexperienced nurses.
The while these inquiring persons were learning a
thing or two an inquest—on a patient who
had suicided at the Yarra Bend Asylum
across the river—brought out the information
that that “hospital for mental invalids 99 was also
overcrowded to such an extent that refractory
and newly-received patients were placed in a
ward together—apparently with a view to show-
ing the latter to what a state they might attain
with care and close attention. The receiving
warder here naively observed that he thought it
might be better if these two classes were placed in
separate wards, but at present it couldn’t be done
for want of room. This is the state of affairs at
the big metropolitan asylums—what it is like at
the out-of-the-way country institutions Heaven
only knows. (Sunbury asylum has no water
supply, save from tanks, so it can be
imagined how its patients fare for baths.)
All these matters have been reported on time and
again. The superintendent and the visiting doc-
tors have repeatedly asked for improvements ; the
Master-in-Lunacy urged a reorganisation of the
whole system on scientific lines. Judge Williams
investigated matters privately, and then wrote to
the papers charging the Government (and the
public) with callous neglect, if not wilful cruelty.
Even the common, honorary beak has urged the
establishment of a receiving- house. The depart-
mental pigeon-holes are packed with such docu-
ments, and now the fool Department is pained
and surprised at the fuss that is being made.
The only surprising thing about the matter is that
Victoria has any lunatics at all—that the un-
fortunate wretches don’t die off within a week of
their admission. The second investigation also
concerned the Chief Secretary’s department. A
number of prisoners at Geelong gaol have
attempted suicide lately—quite an unusual
number. One of these gentlemen is now doing a
term in irons —the reward of failure; another
succeeded the other day. He was the doctor’s
“assistant”—the department not providing a dis-
penser for this, the hospital-gaol of the State—
and he digested a mixture of belladonna, arsenic,
opium and strychnine—with the anticipated re-
sult. Another assaulted his own head with a
pannikin so strenuously that his gaolers for a
while had doubts whether they would be able to
keep him. -He is now in a straight-jacket, await-
ing the attentions of the visiting beaks. There
was no inquest held on the dispensing suicide, a
magisterial inquirer merely questioned the war-
ders and doctor, and entered a verdict that de-
cased departed through taking too many chemi-
cals at once. Then Inspector-General Evans came
down, looked round and returned, and casually
told an interested public that where there were so
many invalids in confinement there was bound to
be “a little grumbling,” and “there was nothing
out of the ordinary as far as the general system of
control is concerned.” The value of Evans’in-
spection and control may be gauged by the fact
that a sharp knife was found concealed on a
prisoner even while he (Evans) was at the
gaol, but he knew nothing of the matter
till he saw it in next morning’s papers.
Take it all round, the Victorian “ system ” seems
to have almost got back to the gruesome horror of
Norfolk'lsland in the early days. Minister Tren-
WfTH, when refusing a commission of inquiry to a
deputation, the other day, stated he wanted
definite charges and practical suggestions. As
regards the asylums, perhaps Trenwith doesn’t
consider a proposal for building decent up-to-date
structures and supplying them with a sufficient
staff a practical suggestion.

Protectionist Reid, and Protectionist
M'Millan, and Protectionist Pumpkin
Smith.
By this time, there is hardly one N.S.W. Free-
trader in the House of Reps, who hasn’t arot off
the mouldy lie that “industries in N.S.W. are
allowed to take their natural course, and are not
coddled or brought up on a feeding bottle.”
Everybody who knows anything of the subject is
quite well aware that a number of the largest and
most prosperous of cornstalk industries have been
protected for many years, that no N. S. W. Foreign-
trade Ministry could be found with enough faith
in its so-called opinions to abolish that Protection
and let “ the bracing air of commercial freedom”
in on these industries, and that N.S.W. didn’t
protect them for the sake of revenue, but sacrificed
revenue in order to protect them. The charge
against the Retd-M'Millan-Pumpkin Smith
push, which permitted such heavy Protection
to sugar, beer, tobacco, cigars, newspapers, and a
number of other things, is that it is telling a flat
and ungentlemanly falsehood when it says that it
believes Freetrade is the policy which makes the
country prosperous ; or else that while in office it
was a public enemy and a mean traitor de-
liberately trying to injure the country; or else
that it was a shifty and undecided coward without
the of its opinions, or without enough in-
telligence to know for certain what its opinions
were. By not putting the excise on tobacco and
beer up to the level of the customs duty it lost a
lot of revenue, and it lost a lot more by not placing
a countervailing excise on sugar, and by not
levying the same postage on local as on inter-State
newspapers—in fact, the amount of income it
sacrified so as to prevent the cold bracing blessings
of Freetrade blowing on these industries was
something alarming. The appointment of a Par-
liamentary Committee to find out, if possible,
why Reid and M'Millan and their doleful
Pumpkin Smith didn’t follow their own principles
when they had the chance, would furnish an inte-
resting billet for some undeserving person.

Maoriland’s Old liion.
Never, judging by appearances, should the
Seddon Government have been stronger than
last session. The organised Opposition had dis-
appeared ; the ablest Oppositionist had left the
province; the Ministerial majority was larger
than ever. Yet the session was almost
barren of useful results. Measure after measure
asked for by the people was brought in by the
Government, was lukewarmly discussed, and was
finally shelved or dropped. Thus the Referendum
Bill was rejected by the Upper House without
a single protest.from the once militant Seddon ;
the Fair Rentßillandthe State Fire Insurance Bill
were abandoned ; the Licensing Bill was brought
down so near the end of the session as to make its
introduction a mockery ; and the long-promised
County Bill, though printed, was not even intro-
duced. The only noticeable measure passed wasthe
Libel Bill, giving extensive powers to “jug”
pressmen. Every day of the session meant a loss
of prestige to the Government. The flabbiness of its
attitude on Federation was pitiable. The discus-
sion on the Pacific Islands question showed that
Seddon, with wild ideas of “Hempire” in his
head, had incurred liabilities and responsibilities
which he had never foreseen, and which neither
the House nor the province seemed anxious to
take over. The debates on the York and the
Ranfurly expenditure were a most damaging ex-
posure of the seamy side of the recent grovel.
Richard Seddon, in his declining years, has
apparently lost his initiative. He has done big
things, but the men who supplied him with many
of his great ideas are no longer here, and his stock
of inspiration has apparently run out. So, being
accustomed to do big things, he has tried latterly
to be a greater Jingo and Imperial groveller
than any one else, and has fallen back on
the threadbare device of floating big loans,
which have been a comparative failure, and,
after being one of the greatest successes in
Australasian politics it is quite possible that he
may yet end by being one of its most conspicuous
failures Yet in Maoriland, despite all its reforms
and all its progress, there is still a bigger work
left to do than any Seddon has done, and the
largest opportunity of his life is still before him.
The old lion has no need to spend his declining
years running after mice ; there is still an animal
of his own size to tackle. Maoriland has a debt of
some £50,000,000 —-about £63 per inhabitant. The
man who will find a way of dispensing with fur-
ther loans, and of gradually reducing the existing
debt, and who will set in motion a scheme for
converting the foreign debt into a local liability,
would have a large enough contract on hand to
make him the most famous statesman this
country has ever heard of. Seddon, in his
later years, pottering with small things in
which he has lost all serious interest, trying
vainly to think of a big war-cry with which to
rouse the popular enthusiasm as of old, flapping
desperately and uselessly at a big calico banner,
and scheming to annex islands full of useless
niggers, and to have a “foreign policy”—pretend-
ing to be still the same resourceful politician as of
old, with almost all his opponents dead and his
multitudinous friends lukewarm and bound to him
mostly by force of habit—it all suggests the old
humorist trying to behumorous again, orthe ancient
monarch of the forest mooning over the bones that
he accumulated in his fighting days. Seddon isn’t
so very old after all, but he is mouldering for the
want of a big idea, and a big opponent, and a big
fight—also a big advertisement. He would get all
four if he formulated a scheme for dispensing with
loans, and payjng off the loans that Maoriland has
already incurred. And there is nothing impos-
sible, or even improbable, about the idea. It
would certainly involve a big effort to get out of
an unwholesome old groove, but the Seddon
Government, in its better days, used rather to
like getting out of old grooves.
Trial by* Docker.
N.S. W. minor Judge Docker, at recent Quarter
Sessions, sentenced al3-years-old boy to six months’
gaol and a whipping for common assault on a girl.
I'he jury found the lad guilty of common assault
(with a strong recommendation to mercy), but
Judge Docker found him guilty of a more serious
offence, and proceeded to administer punishment
in accordance with his own verdict, not the jury’s.
It would have only been to a small degree more
improper if, after the jury had said absolutely
‘ not guilty,” the Judge had persisted in sending
the accused to gaol, for, as it was, he punished
him—and clearly stated that he punished him—
for an offence of which he was not found guilty.
According to the newspaper report—
His Honor could not understand how any iurv could
have returned the verdict they did on the evidence before
them. On the evidence of the girl it was clear that more
than a common assault had been committed. The law
provided that anyone who committed an assault on a
female might be whipped, and the present was a case
where, he considered, a thrashing would be the'most
salutary form of punishment. He then sentenced the
prisoner to six months’ imprisonment with hard labor,
and to receive 25 lashes. Were he to deal with the boy
as a first offender, the young men who sat in court might
think that they could commit sim' r offences and get off
free The whipping would be a lesson to them as well as
to the prisoner.
This proposal of Mr. Docker to make an accused
person whipping-boy for the benefit of other
young men in Court, has been, fortunately
promptly nipped in the bud by the N.S.W. Pre-
mier, who has countermanded the flogging order
pending inquiries. But something more is neces-
saiy than that strong rebuke to Judge Docker
over this particular case. His contempt of juries
has been so frequent and flagrant that it cannot be
longer ignored. This incident is but one of many.
Its particularly scandalous nature is, however
peculiarly well-fitted to give the prompting to
stern action. The jury found the accused guilty
of common assault, with a strong recommendation
to mercy, and a regret that “ the case should ever
have been brought into Court at all.” That was
their judgment on the facts, and they, and they
alone, had the right to judge the facts. Mr.
Docker brushed aside their verdict with con-
tempt, and substituted one of his own with what
he deemed appropriate punishment. The Govern-
ment, it seems, must choose now between trial by
jury and trial by Docker. One or other must be
abolished ; they are mutually incompatible.

Melbourne Marine Board has been complaining
of the dangerous way in which coal-ships are
/ArcTw \ to ,e oa^e< 3 at Sydney and Newcastle
(N.S.W.). The practice in question is mostly
limited to foreign-going vessels in the English
trade, proceeding by both Cape and Canal routes.
Owing to the high price of coal at Aden and Cape
Town, the object is to carry supplies to last in the
first instance until reaching Port Said. Fuel
is cheaper at Port Said than at Aden, for
ships discharging coal at the former port
pay no Canal dues. On the Cape route the
usual coaling ports are Las Palmas or Ten-
eriffe, in the Canaries—-about 38 days steam
from the last coaling-port in Australia (generally
Albany), and, as very few steamers have sufficient
bunker-capacity to last them for this period, it
becomes necessary to provide what are known as
“reserve bunkers.” These, if under hatches,
make a considerable inroad on cargo-space; there-
fore the decks are used for extra coal-storage,
sometimes up to 400 tons, and often in such a way
that, in a gale, water shipped forward has no
means of egress. The coal on deck thus, by
becoming sodden, much increases its weight and
endangers the vessel’s stability, besides being
liable to shift dangerously with every motion of the
vessel. The master is generally powerless in the
matter, owners’ orders being on no account to coal
at Capetown or Aden. So the poor skipper takes
his chance, praying only for fine weather until
there shall be room enough in his bunkers to let
him shift his deck-load. If deck-stowage is abso-
lutely necessary, the quantity should be limited
to, say, 100 or 150 tons, egress being provided for
storm-water shipped ; and the penalty for non-
compliance should be recoverable, not from the
master, but from the agent or owner responsible
for the deck-stowage.

“H.S.T.” discourses on the Boer butcher’s-bill:—
British War-Secretary Broderick’s recent statement
that “11,000 Boers have been killed, wounded, or have
left the country,” is so vague as to be meaningless. Then
he gives the number taken prisoners, and the statements,
taken together, are misleading. For instance, a Boer,
after recovering from his wounds, becomes a prisoner,
and thus figures in both categories. Also, the fact that
the actual number of slain is not separately given is sig-
nificant. The method of estimating the number killed in
this S’African business is a howling farce, anyhow. After
an engagement, sundry wandering Kaffirs find their way
into the British camps, and a nigger, who often cannot
count up to 10, is questioned, and his unsupported
say-so accepted as reliable. On one occasion par-
ticularly (when the present writer, through the
bolting of his horse, found himself nearer to
the enemy than any man in the British column)
the fighting was confined to one hill, and, after the Boers
scooted, this valiant warrior rushed the position alone
(he didn’t have his bayonet, which was in camp, serving
as a tent-peg), but he didn’t find any downed Dutchmen.
Yet, afterwards, in a dispatch to the War Office, it was
stated that, in this particular scrap, 32 Boers were killed.
I couldn’t make it out until Chamberlain (our dusky
waggon-driver) explained. Pointing to another nigger,
he said, “Duke of York over there. Him terrible liar.
•Him come in camp that time an’tell big boss plenty killed.
Get plenty scoff. Never see fight at all. He awful ter-
rible great liar.” That’s how the British “ Intelligence ”
Department gets much of its information. And you’re a
“ blanky pro-Boer” if you don’t believe it.

“0.P.” to The Bulletin about the opium-habit:
Hume Cook and the others who obtained the 10s.
increase of duty on opium have done a much larger thing
for Australia than some people might suppose. It is not
generally known that the opium-habit has got firm hold
in many parts of the Australian bush as well as in the
cities. Practically the whole of Queensland and portions
of N.S. Wales are affected, and it’s quite common in
those parts to meet travellers of all sorts mooning along
with their “ home-made ” lamps and pipes. The signs of
the curse are evident also in many of the townships—
especially the remote ones. Present writer, some years
ago, went into a Chinese den in Oharleville (Q.),' and
found 16 bunks built around one room, and two white
men on each—one smoking opium and the other “ toast-
ing” for him, turn about. They were a miscellaneous lot
—jockeys, bush-workers, clerks, drapers, and so on ;
some shattered skeletons with the sallow features and
leaden eye of the Chinaman, and others obviously in the
earlier stages of the habit—but all, without doubt, doomed.
On many sheep and cattle-stations, too, in both States
mentioned the drug is working silenttragedies. The mana-
ger or his wife often engages a Chinese cook, or persuades
Chinese dealers to camp at the back door, simply in order
to get opium on the quiet. A Chinaman cook once told
the present writer that the manager’s wife often came to
his room at night for a draw at the fascinating “ bubbler ”
—the Chinaman’s pipe ! Think, too, what the power to
withhold would make possible in such a case ! Think,
also, of the predicament into which the curse drives its
devotees, an inability to work and earn money, com-
bined with the necessity of finding the wherewithal to
procure opium. There are many cases, especially in
Queensland, in which white men do a little work for
Chinamen in exchange for opium, but, having no energy
left wherewith to earn a living, simply beg what they
can and crawl about in rags. There are worse phases of
the matter than these, but they can’t be mentioned here;
and as what I have set forth is mere casual discovery, the
trouble may be ten times greater than is suspected.

Thus the Westralian Worker :
The Bulletin asks: “ Would the miner who wants
cheap German and Chow and Jap. commodities growl if
the artisans asked for cheap minerals mined by Coolies,
or Japanese, or some other 4Jd. a day pick-wielder ?”
The miner doesn’t want cheap German, Chow, and Jap.
commodities. He simply says : If you protect one kind
of industry, why not another ? Why should you make
mining unprofitable in order to make jam-making profit-
able? Why should the miner out in the Never-Never
country be compelled to starve in order that a Melbourne
sweater may get bigger prices for his sweated goods ?
The miner doesn’t want black labor to grow his food or
make his clothes ; he has been the most persistent advo-
cate of white labor in Australia. But he objects to starve
in order that fat exploiters may feast. His motto is : A
white Australia, white wages, and fair play to all in-
dustries.
Verily the Freetrade-Labor paper is a weird
anomaly. The miner doesn’t want cheap Hindoo,
Chow and Jap. commodities. He wants ’em
made by white Australian workers working for
the same miserable wages as the Hindoo, Chow
and Jap. workers sweat. Which is a big dis-
aV 1( ir 10n the slightest difference. The
Melbourne sweater won’t be a sweater if the
working classes stand true to themselves. Give
the manufacturer his protection against the out-
side world, and regulate the wages he shall pay
and the hours his toilers shall work, and the
workers will have sufficient cash to spend to make
mining out in the Never-Never become profitable.
Will the Westralian Worker, or any other Free-
trade-Labor paper please state how we are to have
white wages, and fair play to all industries ”
unless we keep out cheap labor goods by a protec-
tive tariff. The day that is shown possible The
Bulletin will recant absolutely.
When the proposal was made, in N.S.W. Parlt.
more than two years ago, to send a colientingent to
neJp'Jimgland to crush the Boers, Prime Minister
Barton made use of the argument, to squelch
opposition, that it was too late to discuss the
rights or wrongs of the struggle: hostilities had
J ' ; 'o u 'h a j was the duty of all good citizens to
stand by the mother country, whatever
m’ght be individual opinion as to the merits of the
war. -tms argument, or appeal, was successful.
ISow, as the tide of Jingoism begins to turn in
ltmgland, the Chamberlain party appeals to
• U *mT l - n ser| ti mei 't regarding the war as its
justification ! At a recent meeting of merchants
and other wealthy people in Liverpool, a strong-
hold of Conservatism and rampant Imperialism,
the president, in his address, stated that if it were
not for the sympathy and support of the colonies,
they might almost begin to believe the war was an
unjust one.” Lord Salisbury has since said prac-
tically the same thing. In fact all British Jingoism
is now replying to the anti-war party by references
to the enthusiastic colonial approval of the war.
1 lius it now happens that Australians, who were
urged to stand by the dear old mother coun-
try in the war, whether right or wrong, merely
because it had begun, are now asked to carry the
burden of being the makers of the war, as being
the people whose profound conviction of the jus-
tice pf the S’African raid is ample justification of
the British Government’s policy, and full reason
why the enterprise should be persisted in to the
bitter end. This is our reward for accepting the
Bartonian appeal!

The Barton Ministry has gone into recess with-
out taking the sense of Parliament on the timidly-
mooted proposal co send 5000 more men to
S Africa, and this should be a definite pledge
to, the electors that no action can be taken behind
the backs of their representatives. But can Mr.
Barton be trusted in this matter ? There is a
general conspiracy to bamboozle Australia, against
her better judgment, into sending away another
array (Sydney D.T. gives as one asinine reason
for this step that the German press has been
making nasty remarks). The Empire no longer
calls; it merely nudges ; but a nudge or a gentle
kick under the table is as good as a call to a title-
hunting politician, and a treachery to national
interests which timidity would have made im-
possible with Parliament sitting may be at-
tempted during recess. N.S. Wales feeling on
the matter is shown clearly by the stony silence
with which Premier See has received Mr. J. S.
Horsfall’s frenzied appeals to send out more
men to son-in-law Rupert Carrington. Mr.
Horsfall makes it clear that the British authori-
ties want to get the men, but don’t like to ask
openly (he avers that Ketchener himself dictated
or approved of son-in-law Rupert’s impas-
sioned cable). A private and confidential
communication, whilst Parliament is out of
session, to the Federal Ministry seems to be
the most likely thing in the world. Should the
Premier play the Jingo game, then it should
definitely kill the Barton tradition on this
continent. If the Commonwealth is to enter 01
its first war, let it be by the express sanction
of its Parliament after full consideration. That
sanction The Bulletin believes, would not be
granted, or, if granted, it would be after such
debate as would show Jingoism the trend of true
Australian opinion.

“ Pukaki ” writes in reply :
Re Kiwi’s diatribe (B. 30/11/’01) against the Seddon.
McKenzie land system. A big land settlement policy
can’t be run on the lines of “land-value” faddists of
various denominations. “Kiwi’s” admission that the
system has given satisfaction to all classes is surely the
best proof that Seddon and McKenzie, when they framed
the scheme, were acting not like theorisers,' but like
statesmen. “ You can’t fool all the people all the time.”
“Kiwi” complains that the big estates aren’t being
broken up fast enough At all events, they are paying
graduated tax, and, in many instances, absentee-tax, too.
“ Kiwi ” is out of it, by the way, when he declares that
selectors of resumed estates can acquire the freehold.
They can only take up the land on “ lease in perpetuity ”
(999 years). As to “ gambling ” in the leases, the Govt,
has just issued new regulations under the Land for
Settlements Act, by which preference is to be given to
family men and farmers ; and selectors are not to be al-
lowed to transfer for five years. Probably other means
of checking “gambling ” will be found when wanted.
The Foreign Importers’ party had a pretty bad
fall, in the House of Representatives, the other
night. It had been holding forth about the Clyde
(N.S.W.) engineering works as an'example of how
great local industries might spring up under Free-
trade. Whereupon Kingston sent for particulars,
and the manager replied stating that the original
concern (Hudson Brothers) had burst up
in 1896, almost immediately after the Reid
Freetrade tariff came in force ; that the factory
remained in a comatose state till 1898; that
then a new co. bought it solely because it
believed that Federation and a Protectionist
tariff were assured ; and that, pending the
advent of the Protectionist tariff, it had lived on
Government contracts (seven-eighths of its busi-
ness was Government contracts). Then there was
such a silence among the M‘Millan party that
one could have heard the footsteps of a butterfly
half-a-mile off, till a Freetrader got up to say in a
flurried voice that-the matter was really one of no
importance.

Winston Churchill on the war :
Week after week the end of the war has been ex-
pected, week has been added to week and month to
month till now we come upon the third year of waste
and sorrow, and the Ministers who ought to be cease-
lessly planning for the future, seem to drift helplessly as
in a dream. The people who asked the Government to
employ the iron hand and declare the Boers outlaws were
very short-sighted, for what could keep the Boer forces
obedient to their commanders better than the assurance
of perpetual banishment, or even of death, if they fell
into our hands? There is at the present time a most
perilous apathy. The war seems to be regarded as
chronic. Public attention is often diverted from it
Even such things as the racing of pleasure yachts turn
the minds of thousands from it. There is the' loss of life,
and there is also the wealth of the nation draining away
—enough to buy every month four of the largest battle-
ships in the world. There is India. Nearly 30,000 men,
detained beyond their contract with the State, im-
pitinntly await relief in India. Every day increases the
strain upon our military organisation and the embarrass-
ment of our finances. The loyal districts of South Africa
sicken in the grip of martial law ; there is the gulf of
hatred between Boer and Briton growing wider, and every
day devastation and ruin rules over wider areas.

Some years ago, a Polish gentleman, with
a name that sounds like a naked friend being
slapped with a wet towel, wrote a great novel,
and a woefully bad drama built thereupon was
staged at Her Majesty’s on Saturday. The stage
“Quo Vadis” is full of divergences from the
book, and they are all for the worse. An allegedly
comic bald general and his shrewish wife, neither
of whom is in the original story, take up a lot of
valuable space. The great banquet—that brilliant
function at which the palace of the Caesars was
full of the nobility of Rome, and the nobility was
full of liquor, and had slid on to the floor—is
represented by a table, round which about a dozen
persons are gathered. On it are many flowers, a
few cups, and what look like two pineapples.
And into this gorgeous scene, through the guarded
gates and into the guarded halls, there bursts
a sinister - looking brown Christian with a
thunderstorm in his pocket; and this person
straddles his legs far apart, and waves his uncut
mane at the best and most intoxicated Roman
society, and accuses Caesar with impunity of mur-
der, tyranny, incivility, unpunctuality, butchery,
impiety and aggravation. And then the usual
cheap and useless miracle happens—the wasted
miracle that is badly wanted later on when it
doesn’t come—for the lights go out, and lightning
plunges all over the promises like an illuminated
bookmaker trying to get away from his creditors.
* * m m
The play is full of curious and useless in-
novations. Eunice gets 50 lashes instead of 25,
which seems an unreasonable amount of flogging
for such a slender girl. Chilonides, the Greek in-
former, enters the story mainly that he may be
found listening to nothing in particular with
his hairy and neglected ear on Aulus’s doorstep.
Croton takes another large ruffian with him when
he goes to steal Lygia, and Ursus kills two of them
instead of one. The glittering Petronius doesn’t
suicide at a banquet among his friends, but waits
until Tigellinus and his soldiers come to arrest
him. Tigellinus has evidently brought six deaf
soldiers, for they stand gloomily against the wall
despite the most urgent orders to seize and drag
away their prisoner, and Petronius reads a good
deal of literature to them, and uses expressions
about Caesar calculated to cause a breach of the
peace. Then he and Eunice poison themselves at
leisure, and sit around at great length on the floor.
Nero’s persecution of the Christians comes before
the burning of Rome instead of after, which
stands the sense and intention of things on its
head, for the persecution is supposed to be based
on the allegation that the Christians had set a
light to the city of seven hills and fourteen
bad smells. And the dramatist sends Lygia back
to prison after Ursus has rescued her from the
stuffed cow with the starched tail; and Vinicius
is sent there also, and they escape by changing
clothes with Petronius and Eunice after the
fashion of the Irish drama. The two latter are
left in the prison, which takes fire, and then the
cold and imperturbable Petronius hollers for help,
and pounds on the door, like an ordinary person
with a thirst trying to get into an old Roman
hotel on Sunday, so that he may take 3d. out of
the tail of his toga and spend it on beer. Why
all these things are it is hard to say.
A good deal of fine old palace and much
picturesque clothing pervade “Quo Vadis.”
Petronius himself is so like Pete in the “ Manx-
man ” that he leaves behind no impression worth
defining. Ambrose Manning’s Nero is a fine study,
and among the male characters he stands out like
the big tree on One Tree Plain. His Nero is widely
different from Caleb Porter’s, yet it is equally
good. Basil Gill supplies a gloomy, but rather
forceful, Vinicius. Among the ladies, Miss
Lillah M'Carthy and Miss Edyth Latimer are an
early Christian and a late Pagan that are beautiful
to look upon and clever to boot. A number of
miscellaneous Christians, apostles, deacons, elders,
presbyters, martyrs and sundries call for no special
mention. Miss Belmore’s Poppoea only awakes a
sorrowful longing to see Nelly Mortyne back in
the part; and the brothers Majeroni, as Ursus
and Tigellinus, are at least respectable. The
Wilson Barrett season ends on Saturday —“ Ham-
let,” “Othello” and “Virginius” filling in the
last three nights.
*# * *
A. Jefferson’s drama, “ The World’s Ver-
dict,” has a short run at Sydney Lyceum till
“ Greed for Gold ” arrives to preach a moral lesson
of some sort on Boxing Night. The- “Verdict”
was staged some little while back, so it is only
necessary to mention that the villain is married
two deep, and that the usual irrelevant maniac
obligingly tries to dispose of one wife by means of
a home-made guillotine which he has fabricated in
some dark, damp place, evidently without police
interference. This is the juncture when the dress
circle’s lower jaw is supposed to drop on its tariff-
smitten starched collar, and when the gallery
man’s ditto gets a closer grip on his pepper-
mint in the tension of the moment. The
will, which, as usual, Stirling Whyte is killed
for making in act one, turns up secure in
act five, as might be expected.. Ethel Buckley
and Bert Bailey take the parts of two faithful
menials, Bert Bailey’s character suiting him
exactly. H. O. Willard is, as always, an attrac-
tive villain with a graceful habit of turning on hi
which argues deep study of the Family
If icrtALD ; while Miss Helen Fergus is a villainess
of excellent capability, with many changes of
wardrobe.

Charles Arnold opens Sydney Palace on
26th December with “ Hans the Boatman ” —the
first appearance of the big dog drama for nine
years, and also Hans’s first appearance at 35., 25.,
Is. Last week Arnold’s manager was advertising
for a lot of “ kiddies ” and a “ good natural baby ”
aged about 12 months to be the infant in the play.
No wooden, artificial, or unnatural baby need
apply. _ Prior to Arnold’s appearance a musical
entertainment, “The Arcadian Nights,” is to
occupy the Palace on three occasions only
(Saturday, Monday and Christmas night).
Miss Maud Celinski will play and Miss
Florence Mack will sing in these pro-
ceedings. Holloway and a melodrama, “For the
Sake of a Woman,” are due at Sydney Cri. on
Boxing Night. Also, the Queen’s Hall opens on
Saturday (21st) with “Our Army,”described as a
“grand military entertainment,” and “Little
Red Riding Hood.”
