Sydney Empire company still places itself
against a background of roses in the first part.
Hettie Warden creates some considerable interest
by her brisk “Rough and Tumble Boys”; while
Miss Amy Blackie’s fine voice is heard to advan-
tage in “ Kate O’Donohue.” But Amy does not
coiffure herself to advantage, and her f roc king is
out of date. Miss Ida Rosslyn is a practised actress
and makes the most of herself and her voice.
The cricket-match with which the first part con-
cludes shows that the Empire company has
studied the great subjects of the day, and on that
account may be commended. The second part is
brightened by the Elmore Sisters, the “some-
what otherwise comedian,” Fred. Rivenliall, and
Will Stevens, in a Female Impersonation of a
violent and nightmareish description. The Empire
is going to have a pantomime if it can be crowded
into the limited space. “Robinson Crusoe”
bursts on astonished Sydney on Boxing night,
as * * *
The only new face at Sydney Tivoli is the
elderly one of Mr. S. Holdsworth, an American
ballad-singer whose items are illustrated with
chromatic outrages in the shape of magic-lantern
slides. Mr. Holdsworth is a very-ordinary singer
indeed, and Mr. Rickards might have got as good
without going further than Manly ; but the turn
takes with the public. For the rest, the show is
very much the same as before, the first part still
being blessed by the humorous figure of the stout,
bald gentleman in gorgeous clothes solemnly fold-
ing to his breadbasket the arms of two young per-
sons, according, presumably, to the customs of
Venice.
* * * *-
The last week of the Italian Opera co. at
Her Majesty’s (Melb.) has added “Fedora,”
by Giordano, to its list of good deeds. Probably
this will be bracketed with “La Boheme,” in
general estimation, and anyway Melbourne will
remember it as a new experience that ought to
have happened earlier. The four acts of Sardou’s
tragedy are reduced to three in the operatic
version by the simple device of changing the
second-act scene from the house of Countess
Olga to the home of Fedora, where her
lover, after confessing that he killed Count
Vladimir, comes back by appointment to the
lady’s toils when the other guests have departed.
Some of the space between these two interviews
is filled in—what time Fedora sits writing a note
to the Russian police officer—with an orchestral
number not unlike the intermezzo in “ Cavalleria
Rusticana.” For the rest, the libretto is an
extraot of meat from Sardou’s story, and anyone
who knows “Fedora” in English can translate
the Italian opera at sight.
• * * 9
Giordano’s music is in “ La Boheme ”
style, but he hasn’t bound himself to a scheme of
fragmentary melody. There are airs that linger ;
songs with well-defined beginnings and endings to
them. The orchestration strikes one as having,
perhaps, a more durable charm than “ La
Boheme ” effects, and the question as to whether
any of these up-to-date operas will live to be old-
fashioned is not worth discussing. At present
they are a very pretty fashion. Signorina
Bassich is an artistic Princess Fedora, and,
as far as her medium-sized voice and meek
Mimi-like air will permit, is impressive.
But on Saturday the orchestra seemed to
put her into the shade at moments when
her voice should have been brilliant. Dani,
as Ipanoff, sang his best sometimes. Signorina
Vaccari was a gay and sprightly Olga, and Lom-
bardi, as the coachman in the first act, got a
chance to display his fine basso. To-night
(Thursday) the last performance of “Fedora” is
due, and on Friday the season ends with a mixed
bill. After which a number of people will begin
to think they missed something in not going
oftener to the opera when the chances offered.
* * *
“San Toy,” a musical comedy which is
expected to smash the “Florodora” record and
strike the new Governor pink when he beholds it,
is the Xmas attraction at Her Majesty’s. The
Xmas of J. C. Williamson is a movable feast,
and this year, same as last, he will put it ahead
of the almanac. “ San Toy ” is to get born
into this hemisphere on Saturday the 21st,
and Miss Florence Young will be there
at the time. The reappearance of Mks
Young, after three years’ residence in Pans and
other foreign parts, looks highly refreshing on
paper. Also, Mr. Claude Bantock, and Mr
Maurice Dudley will be introduced to Melbourne
as members of J. O. Williamson’s Comic Opera
co., whilst Mr. Spencer Barry is staging the
gorgeous production. After turning those e
names over in its mind, The Bulletin wouldn t
be surprised to learn that the limelight man is
Cholmondeley Vavasour, or something with de
Vaux in it.

The amazing (l War of Wealth will rage
at Me lb. Royal until Tuesday night, when bank-
manager Baker will take his chairman of directors
captive for the last time. Meanwhile, Bland Ilolt
is cracking his stockwhip to satisfactory business.
Bland’s character in the ‘‘ War of Wealth has a
fairly dishonest appearance at the outset, and his
Yankee-Australian language grows wearisome,
but he saves Baker’s bank with a waggon load of
money, and is seemingly the hero of the drama
after all. “The Span of Life” follows the
“ War ” on Boxing night.

At the Bijou, the variety turns around as
before, and the baggy pants and broken discourse
of the Musical Burglar continue to charm good

** * *
Something new in the Opera House pro-
gramme is Gallando, who chucks lumps of soft
clay at a board and models faces with his fingers
at the rate of about 90sec. per face. The spry
Gallando has an undoubted talent for quick
modelling, and he only nee.ds a little help from
the imaginations of his audience to enable him to
produce a pale mud Kruger, or any other well-
known character within a given period. On
Saturday, he made George Reid the subject for
his last attempt, and George was one of his
failures, but when the artist has got a firm mental
grip of that misguided politician’s countenance,
be will probably push the clay pudding into some
likeness thereof. Leggy Pryde is singing new
songs and pattering other patters than at first,
and the Craggs are always fresh.
*# * *
The Clown Ballet and numerous altera-
tions in the bill are maintaining Fitzgeralds’
Circus and the ravenous menagerie during the
“off” time that comes before Xmas. Some
educated dogs have been added to the Fitz
animal kingdom. Also the Dunbar who rejoices
in the name of Geraldine is holding on to a distant
bar by 10 toes, and dropping a hundred feet into a
net.

Adelaide Royal has been closed (for the
first time under lessee Pollock's management) all
this month, and the companies that stayed away
are to be congratulated on avoiding the heat-
wave. The Tivoli, without opposition, has put in
a strong time, regardless of weather, with Dante’s
widow, Will Whitburn, and De Wynne. The
latest turn is direct from London, Colby and
Way. The latter is a clever dancer, with a “La
I’oupee ” style ; Colby is a useful ventriloquist,
with some really new jokes.

The Australasian Temperance and General Life
Assurance Society shows a very much-needed improve-
ment in its report for year to 30th September, 1901:
Ordinary Life Department.
Claims, Added to Total
Income. Expenses. &c. Funds. Funds.
1894 £38,304 £7,906 £11,987 £18,411 £170,678
1895 38,120 8,134 13,103 16,883\ 1(V7
Depreciation, £19,915, written off J ’
1896 39,707 10,885 13,349 15,473 183,119
1897 42,787 11,888 15,310 15.589 198,708
1898 46,646 13,816 15,813 17,017 215,725
1899 49,777 17,245 29,033 12,499 228,224
1900 53,572 19,760 25,409 8,403 236,627
1901 61,872 19,909 24,725 17,238 253,865
The income shows the largest increase up to date, and
the amount added to the fund is also the largest. Fur-
ther, the expense rate, after showing a lamentable increase
for a number of years, has begun to move downwards a
little. The following are the expenses of the T. and G.’s
ordinary department since 1894 : —
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1894 .. £20 12 10 per cent. 1898 .. £29 12 4 per cent.
1895 .. 21 6 9 „ 1899 .. 34 12 11
1896.. 27 8 2 „ 1900.. 36 17 8 ,,
1897 .. 27 15 7 „ 1901 .. 32 3 7
The expense rate is still ridiculously high, and no other
office of any respectable pretensions shows such an outlay.
Still the expenditure of £32 3s. 7d. per cent, is an im-
provement in the case of the Temperance and General—
which only goes to further demonstrate what a lament-
able state it had got into in previous years.
The interest earnings for the year were £11,062,
equal to £4 10s 3d. per cent, on the average funds. This
is distinctly better than the figure for 1900 (£4 6s. Id.
per cent.), though it is not equal to previous years. It is
just about the ordinary rate earned nowadays, and the
T. and G. requires to earn something quite extraordinary
to compensate for its most disastrous expense rate.
The Temperance and General’s Industrial
Department also shows a large increase of business, but

There are some weird possibilities about this kind of
business. In seven years the income of the T. and G.’s
Industrial Department has multiplied by four, with,
naturally, an increase of policy liabilities ; and the funds
have diminished by nearly £14,000. In the last eight
years the expense rate has been on the following ratio :
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1894 £32 13s. 9d. per cent. 1898 £93 13s. 2d. per cent.
1895 34 8s. 3d. „ 1899 84 19s. 2d. „
1896 47 5s. 3d. „ 1900 68 3s. 4d.
1897 112 16s. 2d. „ 1901 67 0s. 3d. „ .
These figures ought to be a warning to the foolish and
ignorant class which keeps industrial assurance offices
going. Even last year, when things were better than
thev had been since 1896, the person who paid £1 for
industrial assurance in the Temperance and General got
on the average 6s. 7d. worth of insurance for his money,
and the other 13s. sd. went in expenses. If a savings
bank invited the public to put its money m with the
promise that it would faithfully return 6s. 7d. in the £
with interest, what sort of chance would it have t Yet that
is what the industrial assurance business often amounts
to on the average when the expenses are paid. The 1.
and G.’s industrial department realises about £4 10s. 2d.
per cent, interest this year on its average funds, as com-
pared with about £4 ss. 7d. per cent, in 1900, so there is
an improvement here as well as in the expense rate, but
the whole T. and G. concern requires a lot of further
repair to make it an attractive institution.
» • ♦ *
It looks as if this was likely to be a bad year
with the N.S.W. railways and trams. Expenses are
growing faster than revenue, though revenue is in-
creasing handsomely, and for the first quarter (July-
August-September) net earnings were £36,395 less than
for the corresponding quarter last year. The drop was
from £333,980 in 1900 to £297,585 in 1901. Interest has
to come out of these net earnings, and the interest bill
has also largely increased during the year. Going back
over 16 years, from the 12 months ended June, 1886, to
the 12 months ended June, 1901, we get these curious re-
sults : In 1886 and 1887 Victoria had the most profitable
railway system in the Commonwealth : South Australia
had the best one in 1888, 1889, 1890, 1891, 1892 and 1894 ;
N.S.W. showed the best results in 1893 ; and the Westra-
lian system has been on top in the seven years, 1895 to
1901. But N.S.W. has held the second place with great-
persistence. It was first one year out of the 16, and
second no less than 13. Victoria held first place two
years, and second place two years. S.A. had first place
for six years, and second place one year. Westralia held
first place seven years consecutively but never second
place.

The Fourth Victoria Permanent Building
Society (Melbourne), one of the rare institutions which
met all liabilities right through the reconstruction days,
continues to improve, the earnings for year to 31st Octo-
ber, 1901, being the best for many years. Starting from
the period when the old reserves (£7821) and £28,114 of
capital were written off, affairs have gone thus :
**■ Written Re-
Profits. Dividends. off. serves.

1896 .. 3521 2 per cent. =£1917 .. 4315

The profits represent £7 Is. 6d per cent, on capital, which
is a remarkably good rate in the building society business
nowadays. Deposits and advances on mortgages are
both increasing, and the Fourth Victoria appears to
have good prospects ahead of it if the management con-
tinues to be as careful as it is now. At present the
society’s own business premises are mortgaged for
£24,750, and it requires particularly to keep its dividends
low until it saves up sufficient to clear off this liability.
With about £30,000 reserve, and no lien on its building,
and no heavy interest bill to pay, the Fourth Victoria
would be in an enviable situation, and it should aim at
getting there will all speed.

Labor-member Law has long had a notice of
motion mouldering on the N.S.W. Assembly notice-
paper to the effect that Sydney gas-works should
be made a municipal property, in order to
“liberate our people from the painful necessity
of continuing to be supporters of a monopoly
which annually declares enormous dividends, and puts
thousands away to their reserve fundyearlj - .” About the
big dividends (15 per cent.) Mr. Law is right enough, but
seeing that the reserves are £26,000 less than they were
10 years ago, and no addition has been made to them for
a very long lime, the well-meaning M.L.A., in the latter
part of his discourse, is merely damaging an otherwise
good case by sheer ignorance.

of public works to be paid for out of loans this year. Of
course, there will be a supplementary programme later

or part of the interest on their cost, or which can have the
benefit of the doubt, while the other £808,074 is made up
of works which won’t pay a single copper, and are not
expected to. These consist of little jobs and repairs that

The Hannans Oroya Gold Mining Co., Kal-
goorlie, have lately placed an order with the Austral
Otis Company, South Melbourne, for a double drum first
motion winding engine. The cylinders are 18 inch
diameter by 36 inch stroke, and are fitted with balanced
piston valves. The drums are Bft. in diameter and fitted
with indicators. The engine is completely controlled
from the driver’s platform. In all respects is similar to
the engines working so satisfactorily at the Golden Horse-
shoe Mine, Boulder, Long Tunnel, Victoria, and the Oobar
Chesney Mine, N.S.W.*

Vanning Tables.—The very newest and what
gives exceedingly fair promise of superseding all other
tables is the machine known as the Wesley-Baird Vanning
Table. The table is of South Australian invention, and
has given excellent results on Broken Hill ores and ores
containing pyrites, carbonate of copper and tin. Like all
tables, the best work is done on “ fines,” and more espe-
cially when the product is properly classified. The-
Central Mining and Tramway Co., of 424 Little Collins-
street, Melbourne, and 40 Hunter-street, Sydney, are the
sole agents in the Eastern States. The table has a capa-
city up to 30 tons per day.*

The Broken Hill Proprietary intends erecting
kilns for the desulphurising of slimes ; Block 10 will most
likely start the installation of an electric plant on the
mine early in the year to replace the present steam
power, and Sulphide Corporation contemplates some
dew plant. The South, as soon as the new plant (now
nearing completion) is ready, will output over 3000 tons
a week. Litigation is threatened over the Huntington
and Carmichael desulphurising processes.

Govt. Batteries : A deputation from Bathurst
asked the N.S.W. Mines Minister, a few days ago to
erect a public battery at Clear Creek. He admitted’the
necessity for a battery, recognised the mining 'industry
as one the Government should foster, and promised to do
his best to have a battery erected. Before going any
further, he might find out how Govt, batteries have
panned out in other States.

munity which was blessed with honesty or common
sense would charge them to revenue accordingly. There
are many interesting items among these alleged “ Per-
manent and Reproductive Works”: Wharves and
jetties to replace old ones (never paid for)
that have worn out, hospitals, consumptive
home, the lighting of harbor beacons, public baths,
monument to dead contingenters, court houses, police
courts, benevolent asylums, quarantine station, art
gallery, prisons, additions to Parliament House, the
dredging of rivers (which will need, to be dredged
regularly as long as commerce travels on them), purchase
of Government steamer (to replace old vessel, now worn
out, which was formerly bought with loan-money, on the
ground that it was permanent and reproductive 1), repairs
to old dredges (never paid for), and all the usual sundries.
N.S.W. already owes nearly £13,000,000 for unproductive
works that should have been charged to revenue, and the
figure promises to be well on towards £14,000,000 by next
June. Every year the Government describes gaols and the
like as “permanent and reproductive works”—and yet
the Speaker will fall like a landslide on any hon. member
who casts doubts on any other hon. member’s veracity.
The Victorian loan expenditure on “Perman-
ent and Reproductive Works ” for this financial year is
set down at £532,300. This is only one-fifth of the N.S.W.
expenditure, but the ghastly fact is that, barring one
possible item of £50,000, it is all unproductive, and
should all be fairly charged to revenue. Victoria has
been the least dishonest of the States in its loan dealings
up to now, but unless it hangs its Peacock it seems likely
to degenerate. “Maintenance of main roads, £15,000”
(maintena/nce, be it observed—just ordinary daily repairs
‘that the ordinary road-mender does with the ordinary
pick and shovel) is about the most amazing item that has
been seen in a loan schedule up to date. ,

The Adelaide Life Assurance and Guarantee
do. (which does no new business nowadays, and is simply
waiting for its last policy-holder to die or drop out)
doesn’t seem to be much of a speculation for its policy-
holders, but for the shareholders it is certainly good
■enough. These latter individuals apparently annex all
‘the interest earned by the policy-holders’ funds, and
.things go after this fashion :
Claims 15 per
Income and Ex cent - Total
Income. gurren . Ex- Divi .
ders. Penses. dend
4898 .. £3225 £2065 £765 £l5OO £52,313
■4899 .. 2683 878 612 15001 „
Written off, £lOOO / 61 ’ 106
'I9CO .. 3892 350 519 1500 52,629
1901 .. 2871 2543 519 1500 50,938
'The funds are made up of shareholders’ capital, £10,000";
sreserves, £5097 ; and life assurance fund, £35,841. But
the co. is in this remarkable position, that the whole of
its life policies only amount to £28,200, so if all the policy-
holders dropped down dead this afternoon there would
-still be £7641 of the life assurance fund left for the share-
holders to divide. This and the other reserves would
■enable the management to hand back £2 ss. 6d. for every
£1 of capital, and after getting 15 per cent, dividends for
many years, this isn’t at all bad. Moreover, as there is
-even now nearly £7OO a year premium income the surplus
■is still increasing. This method of winding up is one of
■the curious possibilities of the proprietary life assurance
business. When a proprietary co. leaves off business it is
to its interest that the old policy-holders will take their
•surrender values and get out with all possible speed, so
that it can close up and divide the assets and save ex-
penses, and to induce them to stay in by any reckless
(liberality would be sheer foolishness.

Soon after the Canadian Independent Order of
Foresters started life assurance business in Australia,
(Premier Peacock, of Victoria, was invited to test its bona-
,'fides by having an audit made of its accounts (in Canada).
(Peacock declined the responsibility in these beautifully
■non-committal terms, which the institution is now adver-
tising all over Australia as a certificate of solvency :
“ The Government has evidence in its possession that
the business record of the Independent Order of Foresters
In Canada, where its head-office is situated, has been a
perfectly honorable one ; and that it is licensed to do the
(business of life assurance in many States of the United
States of America; and that, after examination and inspec-
tion, these licenses issued annually have been renewed ;
■and because the Government also has evidence that the
institution is incorporated by a special Act of the Parlia-
ment of Canada, that it is regularly licensed by the life
assurance department of the Government of Canada to do
(the business of life assurance in the Dominion, and that
iit is under Government inspection in Canada. The
‘Government also has evidence that it is licensed by the
(Board of Trade of Great Britain to do the business of life
Insurance there, and that its quinquennial valuation of
:assets was duly accepted by that body.”
Which might be put thus :
(1) The 1.0. F. has a perfectly honorable record in
•Canada, inasmuch as i t has paid all claims up to date. Every
•office that is alive has done that, whether solvent or in-
-aolvent; when an office can’t pay claims it dies. The
■fact doesn’t prove that the funds are equal to the lia-
'bilities.
• (2) Ist is licensed to do business in the United States like
any other assessment office. The U.S. is full of the
‘wrecks of dead assessment offices, but it still lioenses the
-survivors.
(3) It is incorporated by a special Act in Canada, which
.'Act compels it to declare itself as an assessment office—
t.e., one which reserves the right to make calls on its
! policy-holders over and above the premium which they
• originally agree to pay, or which reserves the right to
■charge increased premiums. Its special Act is not a cer-
tificate of its special solvency, but is rather in the nature
•'of a special precaution against its being mistaken for the
‘usual kind of life office.
(4) It is licensed in Great Britain, and its valuation of
“assets is accepted there. There is no law in Great Britain
‘under which the State can refuse to accept any assurance
■ office’s valuation. Even the valuation of the Yorkshire
(Provident is accepted, and it has been in business for 30
‘years, has a premium income of over £12,000, and large
inabilities under its policies, and has not only no assur-
ance fund at all, tout has lost most of its capital.

The once great Union Mortgage and Agency
f Oo. of Australasia, and its brother and off-shoot, the
'Australian Estates and Mortgage Co., continue to have
! hard luck. This joint concern was originally the Union
'Mortgage and Agency Co. pure and simple. Then when
I bad times came, the brilliant London management con-
‘eeived the idea of selling most of the properties to the
rnewly-formed Australian Estates and Mortgage Co. As
(the old co. held practically all the shares in the new co.,
‘ this left things just as they were except that there was
1 the cost of the extra management and extra bookkeeping
rto be considered. Later on, it was proposed to sell the
‘ remaining scraps of the old co. to the new co. in order to
save expense of management, which would just make
lit the same old co. under a new name, and now a
“great and cumbersome amalgamation scheme of this kind
is in progress. This is the recent record of this truly
remarkable double-barrelled institution, the two com-
panies being lumped together for convenience :
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
June, 1892. .£52,465 .. 6 per cent. =£55,980 ..£152,070
~ 1893.. 18,367.. Preference3p.c. 19,508.. 150,929
„ 1894.. 11,457 .. .. 162,386
Dec., 1894.. 6,487 .. .. 168,873
„ 1895.. 26,781 .. .. 195,654
Loss.
„ 1896.. 999.. .. 194,655
„ 1897.. 6,536 .. Written off .. 100,000.. 89,119
Deficit.
„ 1898.. 25,642 .. N .. 75,000.. 11,523
i, 1899.. 47,592 .. .. 59,115
i, 1900.. 188,416 .. .. 247,531
These two remarkable concerns have a nominal
joint capital of £2,258,660, but of this one co. holds the
other co.*s shares to the amount of £1,229,930, so the real
joint capital is £1,028,730. Nearly a quarter of this has
disappeared. _ Last year, the ordinary income of the
two companies (apart from profits made by repur-
chase of depreciated debentures) was £111,096, and as the
interest on the debenture-liabilities alone absorbed
£153,532, saying nothing of working expenses of
all kinds, the situation looks about as bad as it could be.
The one gleam of real humor in the business happened in
1896, when one co. made a small profit and the other a
considerable loss, the two combined making a net loss of
£999. Whereupon the first co. paid a dividend to the
second co., and pointed to the profit it had made before
paying the dividend, and the other pointed to the profit
it had made after receiving the dividend, so that they
both looked well, though the joint loss of £999 was there
all the time.
* * ■ *
The Broken Hill outlook is still gloomier.
Lead has fallen further, going down to t'lo 10s. A weak
market just before Xmas is, however, the common thing ;
but, though there may be a temporary recovery after
the holidays, there is nothing to indicate that bottom has
been reached. In fact, the last published lead statistics
are unfavorable. Meantime, the B. H. miners are more or
less considering the co.’s request to them to accept lower
wages. The employees directly interested have handed
the matter over to the whole body of workmen. The argu-
ment is that if the men in the small mines give way, B.H.
Prop, and Sulphide men will soon be asked to accept too,
so that all are equally interested. But there is this big
difference—the big co.s’ men are not in' immediate peril
of losing their work by deciding against reduction,.while
the smaller co.s’ men are. If it comes to fighting, the
men have nothing to fight with, and they know it ; but
most of them take up this position—“ It’s a wholly inade-
quate wage you ask us to accept, and we will not consent
to accept it. If, however, you force it upon us—well,
that’s another matter.”
* » * *
No worse report than that of Block 14 for the
half-year ending Sept.' 30th has been issued by any
Broken Hill mining co. Between March 31 and Sept. 30
the co. made a dead loss of £8279, and also reduced its
surplus of liquid assets from £19,041 to £9484, so that it
is over £17,000 worse off than it was at the end of March.
Moreover, nothing is written off for depreciation, so even
above figures don’t represent the real loss. During the
six months 1600 tons more of lst-grade carbonates were
treated than for the Oct.-March half-year, and 20,000 tons
less of 2nd-grade carbonates and sulphides. With only a
surplus of £9484, as shown, in hand, the financial position
of the co. cannot be regarded as satisfactory, and, if dras-
tic economy is not adopted, the mine had best, like some
others, close down till the lead-market improves. With a
view to meeting the situation, the directors have reduced
the salaries of the Broken Hill officers by 10 per cent.,
which means a saving of £3 ss. 10£d. per week.

Mt. Bulga, N.S.W.: Recent developments at
200 ft. level satisfactory. The large quartz intrusion has
been displaced by solid copper pyrites, averaging 10 per
cent. That is the yarn, anyhow.
• * • *
Dapto Smelting Works : This co. has gone into
reconstruction in London by the issue of 150,000 7 per
cent, cumulative preference shares of £1 each; an
analysis of the financial condition shows that, when every-
thing is provided for, the co. will only have about £ll,OOO
to go on with. It happens to be indebted to Dalgety and
Co. to the extent of £105,000. A disastrous past is ad-
mitted, and, of course, a brilliant future assured. Now
declared that the economic position of the site is better
than any other smelting-works in Australia. That is not
correct. Writer has always said that it occupied a bad
site, and he has never had reason to alter his opinion.
Also, it is averred that there is an abundance of
suitable smelting ore obtainable for a large and profitable
business. Then why is it that the plant has never been
kept in full blast for any length of time ? The works are
valued at £300,009, and it is estimated that by treating
300 tons of ore per day the net earnings show a profit of
about £BO,OOO a year, but where is this ore to come from ?
The Bulletin can only sadly shake its head.

Copper continues to fall, and is likely to see
£5O a ton by the time this gets into print. Cabled that
shares in the Amalgamated Copper Co., alias the Ameri-
can “combine,” have fallen from £l3O to £65, that this
has had the extent of depreciating the holding of Thomas
Lawson to the effect of £2,000,000 !—that “ bears ” were
forcing him to sell, and, by this means, Rothschilds and
Rockfeller would be able to control the world’s copper-
market. This cable is somewhat indefinite. Why should
they go for Lawson ? Are they trying to ruin him as the
Vanderbilts many years back tried to ruin Jay
Gould (and would have succeeded but for American
banks coming to his rescue—not that the banks
wished to save Gould, but did it to prevent a panic).
Prices of shares in Australian copper-mines have not
depreciated in proportion to the fall in the metal. Writer
has a cable from London to say that prospects of
immediate improvement are unfavorable. Dr. Reid, a
director of the Cobar Copper Syndicate, fears that the
collapse of the French Copper Syndicate ten years ago will
be repeated ; if so, it will be a bad thing for Cobar. In the
big mine the gold-contents of the copper have been going
down. At one time the gold was 4oz. in a ton of copper,
and now it is only 2oz. Most of the big mines will continue
to work copper at £5O a ton, but only one or two of them
will be able to pay more than wages and working expenses
at that figure. Throughout Australia copper-mining
will be in the same position as the silver-lead mining
industry at Broken Hill. Already Blayney mine has
closed down, throwing 200 men out of work; just
at the time, too, when the mine is looking re-
markably well and might be made to pay with two
furnaces. It is hard luck on the Lloyd’s copper-
mine at Burraga, where they have been for over a
year preparing to more than double their output; and
the collapse occurs just as they are ready to start their
concentrating works. Lloyd’s is a rich, permanent, and
high-grade mine, but with expensive London and
local management, and copper at £5O per ton
divs. will be like angels’ visits. Mt. Lyell can’t
possibly pay with copper at £5O a ton, and with the
loss of its chief customer, North Mt. Lyell. Strange, too,
that these shares should keep so firm, unless they’have
some big developments in the mine’s lower level, of which
the public have heard little or nothing. What hope is
there for Chillagoe under existing circumstances ? Share
6d - ln London and Is. 9d. in Australia. Bad new
travels more slowly than good, and, no doubt, when the
m -n\ ar u VeS .i n London from Australia, shareholders there
horrified with the detailed account of things at
Chillagoe. Mt. Garnet is turning out a large quantity of
rich matte, but there can be no profit at the present
price of copper. The market will have the effect of
shutting up a number of small copper shows, especially in
u u*u n °k pay to work unless copper were
much higher in price. These could be enumerated, but
it is not worth while. If copper stays at or under £5O a
ton, thousands of copper miners will be thrown out of
work sooner or later.

Golden Link, W.A., has arranged for the treat-
mentinFeb. of a 1500-ton parcel at Hannan’s Star mill.
Ibis news probably means the ushering-in of the Golden
« m >. " f the regular monthly producers of the
neid. If it has taken this mine five years to obtain 1500
tons of ore, how is it possible to keep up a regular return,
tor it is well known that it is not the result of recent
mining operations but a laboriously-compiled accumula-
tion. Having what was at first regarded as a splen-
did position (between the Great Boulder and the
Lake View) the Golden Link started work with
an enormous working capital. It has never been
short of money, and must have spent to date up-
wards of £250,000. It has sunk a shaft 1100 ft., put
out crosscuts and levels in every direction, and, while it
has a little ore, there is nothing to warrant the price of
the shares or the things said about it. It certainly
deserves a better fate, and but for London money it
would have shut down long ago and waited till one of the
adjoining mines proved gold up to its boundary. With-
out ever having a crushing, much less a div., this mine
has a market value of nearly half-a-million, while the
bouth New Moon and Clarence mines of Bendigo, each of
which has years of ore in sight, and is paying nearly 100
per cent, on its capital, have only a little more than half
ot the Golden Link market valuation between them
Just think of it!!
* • * •
Boulder No. 1: The battery on this mine is
being fitted with a plant to treat sulphide by the Riecken
process of gold-extraction worked successfully at the
South Kalgurli. There is no doubt that if the expensive
and enormous sulphide plants on the Great Boulder, Per-
severance, and other mines had not been erected this
process would be in common use at Kalgoorlie. It is
cheaper and more effective than filter-pressing and
ordinary cyaniding. The extraction is if anything
greater, and the bullion is got in a cleaner form. All
slimes are not suitable for filter-pressing, as other mines
outside of Kalgoorlie have found out to their cost, parti-
cularly at the Waihi, where the presses have had to be
thrown out.
r * • • *
The big mines of W.A. : Perseverance, 10,743
tons, 14,1470 z record yield. If the bottom levels in
this mine were as good as from the surface down to 400 ft.
the present price of the shares, £9 155., would be justified.
The bulk of the output comes from a run of gold near
the Lake View boundary. It is nearly 300 ft. long on the
surface, narrows to «oft. at the 500ft.'level, and seems to
have gone right out in the levels below. The second lode,
known as the Perseverance, looks like living to a greater
depth, and may save the mine from disaster when the
other is worked out, which it is likely to be, at present rate
of production, within the next 12 months, unless another
golden zone is found at greater depth. These big ore
lenses of the Lake View and Perseverance do not seem to
carry down unlike the ore in the Boulder and Ivanhoe,
where it runs more in the form of chutes.
* • * «
Lake View Consols: 10,104 tons, 14,4120 z.
These shares went to £7, but have started to recede, and
are bound to get to £5 before they see £lO, which they will
probably never reach again. Wired to the eastern dailies
that the yearly report will show that the ore reserves
have largely increased and there is now £650,000 in sight.
Allowing for expenses that would not pay £2 a share
in divs. So much development work has been done
without opening up any new ore bodies that there is good
ground to fear nearly everything left in the mine has
been exposed, and something extraordinary will have to
happen to stave off another collapse in the gold yield and
in the share market.
• • • •
Golden Horseshoe : 8758 tons, 15,3270 z: This
mine is a sealed book, although it has leaked out that in
the bottom level the ore, although rich, has a tendency
to string out —indicating that the mine will not maintain
its richness to a great depth.
* * * *
Associated: 3538 tons, 32900 z. It is expected
that tonnage and yield will be doubled within the next
few months, but the mine must be worked more cheaply
than any other on the field to pay divs. from sulphide ore
worth under an oz. to the ton. Shares, 445.—£1,100,000
for such a mine ! Imagine such a valuation for any
Australian-owned mine that had been working at a loss
for two years!
* * * *
Kalgurli (1505 tons, 18690 z.) will have no return
this month, and probably none next. It will take that
time to make the new installation to deal with a bigger
tonnage. About time that this mine paid a div if it’s
ever going to do so. Shares, £3 12s. 6d. Nearly half-a-
million for a mine that has never returned the share-
holders a shilling, but that is a common inconsistency on
the London mining market, and for which there is no
accounting.
* * * *
South Kalgurli (1920 tons, 1900 oz.): Still no
div., and another evidence that there is no profit in loz
sulphide ore unless worked on a bigger scale than this
mine is able to do.
* * * *
Ivanhoe: 10,527 tons, 11,0870 z. An improve-
ment in yield from a largely increased tonnage, but not
so great as anticipated. It would be surprising if it were.
Lake View South : 2122 tons of tailings
cyanided for 5230z5. These tailings did not come out of
the mine, and therefore the gold morally belongs to other
people, butjthey have Buckley’s chance of getting it.
Boulder Main Reef : 1864 tons, 21020 z. Next
year will probably see this mine worked out.
* * m a
Brownhill: 52860z5. for about an equal tonnage
of sulphide ore and sands. It was expected that this
mine would have exhausted its ore-reserves months ago,
but development proved they were more extensive than
supposed even by the management. It will be remarkable
if the present monthly output is kept up beyond Feb. or
March next. This co. is said to be making another move
to amalgamate with another mine—this time with the
Oroya, in which it is said to have already acquired a
good number of shares. Last year it failed in an attempt
to buy the northern Oroya block. It has a hig plant, for
which it will soon have no use, and, as the Oroya is
believed to be a mine without a plant, some good might
come of amalgamation. Last quotation is £3 7s. 6d., and
a big drop is expected shortly. Oroya shares on bore-
hole developments went to 565., and are back to 45s
What’s up ?

Great Fingall Consolidated : 3620 tons, 65360 z.
This, it is reckoned, will be increased to at least 10,000 oz.
per month as soon as the new plant gets going. It is
probably the biggest mine in W.A. Shares £l3 7s. 6d.,
and, no matter how great the slump in other directions
they maintain their price. It has no sulphide problem to
solve, but runs a free-milling quartz reef, averaging 15ft.
thick, and opened up in several levels 700 ft. in len»th
With 10,000 oz. a month, it can make as much profit as
any other Kalgoorlie mine with 15,0000 z., and, if next lift
opensup as well as those above, shares will see £2O.
* * • *
Great Boulder: Over 13,0000 z. for the month.
A substantial improvement, but a much greater has
taken place in the mine at 1200 ft. level. This mine has
more ore m sight than any other two on the field, and
will probably maintain its present price—-£l—when
several of the others have fallen 50 per cent. It has a
long life assured. As aloor 12 per cent, mining invest-
ment these shares are as safe as anything in Australia,
and safer than anything else on the field.

Taiisman (M.L.): Clean-up for last month
averaged 275. 6d. a ton, Is. 6d. more than previous month.
This could not be got without sorting the ore, and it will
barely pay at that. And yet the mine has a market
valuation of upwards of half-a-million. Many other
M.L. mines floated in London some few years ago had an
equal, and in some cases, a greater valuation. Komata
reefs were once over £5; present buyers 6d., sellers
Is. 6d. Hauraki, 6d. sellers. They were once as many
pounds. Waihi Grand Junction stay at 30s. because of
proximity to the great Waihi, although it has spent a
lot of money without finding pay ore, and it has been
proved beyond a doubt that the Waihi has got all the
gold in its own ground—an area of 500 acres. New
Zealand Grown, 10s. This mine is a small div.-payer, and
there is a great inconsistency between it and the Talis-
man, the shares of which, based on the share capital, are
tour times a,s high, although it has never paid a div. and
nas got a mighty poor show of ever paying one. A few
months work, and the Talisman will go the way of nearly
all the others, the distinguishing and lasting exception
being the Waihi, which has the largest plant in Aus-
tralasia, and if it does not become the greatest producer
it has a life probably only equalled by Mt. Morgan.

King Conrad, N.S.W.: The concentrators may
or may not be in operation at the end of the year. A lot
ot ore a,waits treatment, and, as there are large supplies
of ore there should be a regular output of concentrates
when the plant really does make a start. Not likely to
pay at current price of its metals.

oi 41 u 0 ' * rust funds in its charge, and of these
x&,U5J,382 has been borrowed by the Treasurer to make
ends meet. This is apart from those funds that are
permanently invested in State securities. If the trust
funds were planted out of the Treasurer’s reach, so that
he couldn’t borrow them at every emergency, the pay of
State officials would be about as erratic as the salary
of a Bashi-Bazouk.

Gibraltar mine, N.S.W., is on tribute now, 60
miners being employed. The co. is, however, extending
operations on the Williams line of reef, where it hopes to
get good stone from the recently-acquired Caledonian and
Woods properties.

Surely it can’t be true that N.S.W. Judge
Docker seriously intended that the 13-year-old boy,
Gavenlock, should receive 25 lashes from the cat-
o’-nine-tails ! To anyone who has ever seen the
lash used, the thing is incredible. If, however,
it happens to be true, then Mr. Ernest Brougham
Docker should at once be fired off the bench and
allowed to amuse himself with amateur photo-
graphy and wagging his jaw at church synods.
* * * *
In an interview on his Divorce Bill, pub-
lished in the Arous, Senator Dobson has much
unnecessary religious doodleflap, but he hits the
nail fair on the head when he characterises
the proposed excision of the three years’
desertion clauses as leaving a condition of
affairs which “weak human nature may not be
able to withstand.” This is a point for the
Church party to remember. If a deserted man
or woman desires to re-marry, and is not per-
mitted to do so, he or she will simply marry
without marriage. Even a big gun of the Church
knows that while a man remains strong and
healthy his religion is very small potatoes beside
his girl, and the same applies to the woman.

* » * *
The recurrence of the bubonic scare has
brought to light in Melbourne the pleasing infor-
mation that thousands of bags of sugar are con-
tinuously stored in the local wharf sheds; that
the rats sample these and spread the contents
abroad, and when the buyer of a hundred bags or
so removes his lot, he always carefully collects the
sweepings and fills up the broken bags.
♦ * * *
Attorney-General Wise says he has a Bill
in hand “dealing with” the glaring blue and
green advertisements which disfigure Sydney har-
bor, and The Bulletin trusts that it deals with
them in a ferocious spirit. There is no adequate
remedy save utter annihilation. The growth of
unkempt Sydney is destroying the harbor beauties
surely enough, still its progress is comparatively
slow. But the great spider-legged advt. about
the defunct pill for dead people overruns the
beauty-spots in a night, and the globule and the
porous plaster glare on the astonished wayfarer in
the morning. No man deserves more unanimous
hanging, as a public enemy, than the sordid fiend
who steals the people’s recreation ground and
blots out all its loveliness in order that he may
tell them lies about his four-and-sixpenny pants
or his huge, thick hobnailed bluchers at half-a-
crown. He is a thief, which is bad ; and he is a
soulless Goth, which is far worse. The short
remedy is this : No placard, advt., calico sign, or
hoarding to be allowed on the shores of the har-
bor, or in sight of the harbor. The harbor
should bo, as far as the presence of population
will admit, a place of peace and calm and loveli-
ness—and nobody can be at peace when a fresh
bill calls to him at every headland with a blue and
red yell 24ft. high and 3Gft. long, asking him if
his stomach is out of order, and urging him to
boil somebody’s lager mackintosh for biliousness
with the name blown in the bottle.

The Victorian border town of Bchuca
seems to be about the first place to actually erect
a statue commemorating the late Queen. It is a
£4O affair and represents Cyparissus—who wept
himself into a cypress at having killed one of
Apollo’s stags. The relevance of this ancient
gentleman to Victoria is a dark and beautiful
mystery which is thrown in for the £4O without
extra charge. Anyone, now, with a cheap second-
hand statue of Psyche or the Venus di Medici
might drop in on Dawvid Syme to see if it couldn’t
be palmed off for his Queen Victoria memorial.
* * ¥ *
Melbourne’s beautiful new avenue, chris-
tened by the little Duke, and over the mainten-
ance of which the City Council and the Public
Works Department are still wrangling, has of
late been elevated into a pony-trainmg ground,
several of the unregistered pony-fakirs doing
their morning gallops there. One, invited to ex-
cuse himself, said he didn’t think he was doing
any harm, and it wasn’t everywhere he could get
a nice, straight two-mile tan track. A hint for
the needy Council: turn the concern into a bike,
foot-and-horse-racing track, rope-in Govt. House
hill, charge a shilling a head, and, with a meeting
every day, down go the city rates.
* * * *
Another fire at that hideous tinder-box,
George-st. asylum, Parramatta. One of the out-
offices was consumed, and an inquiry was held to
account for the outbreak. Verdict, as usual—
nobody to blame, and no reason assigned. The
fire just happened along. The presiding magis-
trate was very severe on the apathy of Govt, in
allowing the asylum to remain standing, and shud-
deringly referred to the consequences if the fire
had broken out in the three-decker building a few
rods off. Out of eleven millions expenditure no
money can be spared to house the 700 decrepit
inmates. Govt, can spend £20,000 loan-funds to
commemorate the men who died for Cohen at
ss. Bd. a day ; not to speak of £4OO for paintings
or photos, of the Rocks, and a few hundreds here
and there for Heaven know 3 what, but nothing in
order to prevent 700 old people from being
roasted. What an odor of burning flesh will
ascend at Parramatta one of these days !
* • * *
Chief Justice Darley, in speaking at the
recent N.S.W. Discharged Prisoners’ Association
meeting, assured his hearers that the separate
and silent treatment had long been abolished. As
a matter of fact, it still exists, and in many cases
to a period of 12 months. Its effects are naturally
brutalising and not reformatory.
* » * *
About twice a month S.M. Herald
devotes a couple of columns to chronicling the
doings of the 41 (less one gone bung) ridiculous
suburban councils around Sydney. Some of the
reports are printed in extenso ; hereunder is a
sample of the history-making solemnly published
by the Herald :
Alexandra.—The Zetland branch of the Simultaneous
Mission wrote thanking the council for the use of the
Town Hall and park, and forwarded 10s. for cost of gas
consumed. The letter was received, and on the motion
of Alderman Brown it was decided to hand the 10s. over
to the charwoman to compensate her for the extra labor
entailed during the mission.
* » * *
The directors of Sydney Hospital have at
last abolished the system of admitting paying
patients. In future the institution will be used
strictly for what it was intended, the relief of the
sick poor in the Metropolitan area.
“Mary S.” : The woman who possesses
an office in town is a great mark for dead-beats
and small swindlers. I was interviewed, the
other day, by a distressed male, who, with half
his face blotted out with a red handkerchief, con-
fided to me, irrelevantly, that his life had been
ruined by his sister-in-law, and that he was on
terms of intimacy with all the nobility of Sydney
—showing their alleged old clothes on him as
testimonials of the same. He then produced a
portrait of one of his aristocratic relations,
which he said he had consented to raffle
for his own benefit. Could he put my
name on the list ? —a shilling was the
price. Some weeks later, he came in
great grief because the list of names had
been lost; so he was going round asking
everyone to give another shilling and to
write their names again, just to prove
that he hadn’t tried to impose on them.
Another day I was summoned by the
knock of a sorrowful working-man, who
stated that he had been working “right
in front of this’ere’ouse, ” and his lunch
had been stolen out of his basket. 4 ‘ It
seems to me that a lydy wouldn’t let a
poor ’ard-working man go without his
dinner.” The writer (whose own lunch
never exceeds 6d.) donated a shilling,
and he said, “Yus, he could make it
do.” Ten minutes later another sorrow-
ful working-man brought a claim for
vanished lunch, and, when this demand
was refused, wondered (but with gentle-
ness) if present scribess considered herself
a “lydy.”
The M.C.C. got into hot water over the
band at the cricket-match. Its agent contracted
with N.S.W. Permanent Artillery Band for the
series of Sydney matches at £0 a day—a rate far
lower than a private musicians’ band could accept,
and which works out at two or three shillings per
man per day. Minister John Forrest, at the in-
stigation of W. H. Hughes, M.P., very properly
stepped in and cancelled this sweating contract,
and the greedy club had to re-engage the per-
formers at market rates. Apart from the unfair-
ness of the business in engaging a band —the best
in the city —which can tender at, a cut rate by
reason of Govt, support, the club looked rather
mean at haggling over a fiver or so, when the
grandstand could not find elbow-room for the band,
and the attendance represented nearly £2OOO.
Another aspect of the question. The Friday
on which the band was booked to play at the test
match was also the day for the bandsmen to perform
in Hyde Park before the public, who pay them for
that purpose ; but, as is the case in almost every
instance when the band’s public and private
engagements clash, the public have been left to
divert themselves elsewhere while their band went
out on the “make.” What a farce to pay
these men to perform a public duty, and then
allow them to neglect that duty that they may
add to their income by “blacklegging” at the
expense of outside bandsmen ! If the men are
not paid sufficiently for them to live without
private engagements, then Govt, should increase
their pay.
* * * *
Sydney City Council, in answer to popular
clamor, has appointed a local man as city organist,
and, with the usual Australian contempt for the
Australian article, reduces the salary from £5OO
to £3OO. It is only reasonable to suppose that
the Australian populace which insisted on a local
man will stop away in crowds from his recitals.
* • • *
At last annual meeting of Bulli (N.S.W.)
Cottage Hospital, Dr. Sturt (one of the Board)
then apparently fine and healthy, jocularly called
attention to the fatal number of 13 persons being
present. “ I wonder, now, which one of the 13
here will die within a year ? ” A few weeks after,
the speaker was dead (his favorite horse also
dropped dead on the same day). Speaking of the
fatal 13, writer of this paragraph once attended a
dinner of 13 Bohemians all told. To propitiate
the gods a dummy figure was used. It was left to
George Lambert, artist (now in Paris), to keep up
a conversation with the “silent woman ”on his
right. It was sufficient propitiation. That was
two years ago. The 13 Bohemians are still alive—
but some are married.
* • * ■’ *
A Melbourne small boy has given the
police some healthy exercise over a story about
how he saw two men kill another in broad day-
light close to Moonee Ponds Creek, put his body
in a bag, and carry the bag to a cart which two
other men had in waiting. The vehicle was
drawn by a white horse (this is a point in the
boy’s favor, as a white horse is a possible animal
—if he had said a bright blue horse there would
have been an improbable air about the narrative),
and it departed at a gallop towards Footscray
with its ghastly burden. The police have got
their feet very wet and their boots deteriorated
fishing in the creek for a clue, and hunting along
the banks for bloodstains, wheel-tracks and foot-
prints, and have looked inquiringly over many
adjacent back fences, but so far nothing has come
of it.
HISTORY REPEATED.
My lady keeps upon her shelf
A comb—a tortoise-shell affair ;
Thus history repeats itself—
The tortoise following the hair !

Adelaide’s new Mayor has had his allow-
ance fixed at £lOOO, and in giving thanks made the
significant remark that every penny would be
spent on the citizens; his last term of office is
reckoned to have cost him quite three times as
much. Ex-Mayor Ware had shocking luck with
a farewell garden-party. Just before the time
fixed the only rain of the month began, and lasted
till dark, drenching all whom it did not keep
away. The whole Corporation ostentatiously
stayed away.
Ex-Mayor Ware aforesaid has a fine moral
courage. He has been generally accused of mean-
ness in office, and his corporation publicly took
him to task, afterwards refusing the usual vote of
thanks for his services as head of the council. Yet
he has said never a word in reply, neither admit-
ting, denying, or explaining anything. Just kept
quite silent, and went on as if nothing had been
said, or what had been said didn’t matter. There
is a certain strength of character about this.
• • • *
At opening of Sydney Stock Exchange
this week stock-broker Palmer let what is now a
fairly well-known cat once again out of the bag.
“It had been objected,” he said, “that
the S’African war was undertaken for
the sake of the capital invested by England
in the Transvaal. If so, what then? There
could be no worthier object, for capital repre-
sented the life-blood of the community.” The B.
is pleased to record this candid confession. Pro-
vided that it is admitted that the war against the
Boers is a, war on behalf of British Capitalism,
the question as to the desirability of fighting for
British Capitalism (which is mostly represented
by foreigners) can be safely left to be argued at
another time.
“Nina Jeane” inquires whether this
throws any light on the scarcity
of the domestic servant:
Last week, in a Sydney hotel,
which considers itself of the
better class, but where there
is no “ useful ” or yard-
man kept, I found a girl
who started work at <
4.30 a.m. on week-
days and 5.30 a.m.
on Sundays.
Every morning
(including Sunday)
she had to scrub
out and wash down
the two bars, pub-
lic and private,
after which she
cleaned the boots of
the family and a limited
number of boarders,
and made missus tea
and toast to sustain
her till rising-
time and break-
fast. Then she
had “ to assist
with the cook-
ing, do all the
scullery-work, act
ns chambermaid and
housemaid, and do
the whole of the
laundry - work of
the establish-
ment, which
meant ironing
every night of
lhe week. Also,
while ironing she
had to mind
“the kid ” of the
house while its
proper nurse as-
sisted missus in
the bar. If the in-
fant was asleep she could go
to bed at ten, but if that
infant was awake she had
to stay up to tell it about
the little pigs going to
market till nursey and
mama had finished
beer - pumping. For
all this she received
11s. 6d. per week.
She was from the
country, and now
she has gone back
determined to do
anything in the
future rather than /
accept domestic
service in Sydney.
This particular
grog - emporium
never engages any but
country girls, to be
paid fortnightly, and if they attempt to leave with-
out giving a fortnight’s notice they are told that
they thereby forfeit all wages due. So the slave is
secured for a month, when she disappears right out of the
profession of “ slavey ” for ever.
* * * *
A Victorian damsel, a few days ago,
charged her father with a horrible offence, and,
though he was undefended, a simple cross-
examination showed that “there was another,”
and that the interesting young person had, to her
grandmother, blamed this other for her “ trouble.”
The father was promptly acquitted. Nothing, of
course, happened to the girl. They play the game
with loaded dice.
* * m m
A blushing damsel faced a Melbourne
Bench, the other day, on a charge of being an
unregistered baby-keeper. She was a single girl,
and was invited to explain why she had repre-
sented herself as married and had adopted a casual
infant. Her lawyer assured the Bench that she
got the baby to convert hard-hearted relatives,
who had intervened between herself and her
young man, to the belief that that young man
must forthwith “make her an honest woman.”
On Saturday at N.S. W Society of Artists’
rooms were exhibited half-a-dozen pictures, mostly
S ortraits, by Mr. H. J. Thaddeus, a painter from
Ingland, or rather Ireland. The chief of these
canvases, “La Obedienza,” an historical picture,
shows much weakness in the treatment of the
’ °bes and background. But the central figure,
(lab of Pope Leo XII., has plenty of life and
character. The Pope is given the face of an
eagle with a sense of humor: masterful, good-
humoredly cynical; a clever Pope must look like
that. Just as good in its way is the countenance
of the Pope’s nephew, a plump, jolly, soldier
figure. Several other portraits by the same
painter were shown—those of Speaker Gully, of the
Duke of Cambridge, and of Lady Howard
De Walden were the most notable. There is
no doubt as to Mr. Thaddeus’ talent for
portraiture, and perhaps his much inferior
treatment of fabrics and accessories is a device to
concentrate the interest on the faces that he pour-
trays. It is comforting to see that the work of
our Australian Longs taff is in no wise eclipsed
when put beside these picked pieces of an English
portrait-painter. On Saturday Cardinal Moran
was among those who saw Mr. Thaddeus’pictures.
Perhaps the fine Pope painting will then be kept
in Australia as an altar-piece, for which it is
emininently suitable.

Benevolent Asylum lately and enjoyed itself
thoroughly. The committee and committee-men
and women of other institutions gathered and were
thoughtfully accommodated on chairs while the
poor old derelicts were put through their paces.
Those able to walk (including the blind and the
halt) passed in parade before Bumbledom. There
was no earthly reason for this degrading spectacle.
Bumbledom calls it the Annual Muster and pre-

Love and loot. A returned cohentingenter
was recently sued in Maoriland for breach of
E remise. It came out that the engagement ring
ad been looted from a S’African farm.
N.S.W. Board of Health is “considering’
the filthy habit of butchers wrapping up their
wares in stale newspaper. This unclean trick has
had support in various influential quarters— the
sale of “ waste is quite an item in the profits of
the blanket dailies, and at least one N.S.W.
politician used to add to his allowance by
the sale to a butcher of Govt. Gazettes
and other official publications, supplied ,
to him m almost any quantity on de-
mand. But it is hard to understand
how any decent community could
tolerate it for long. Clean, white
paper, too, is so cheap that there is
no excuse for enclosing the raw joint
in ancient “rags.” Just think of
taking home a pound of mystery-sausages
wrapped in the much-clawed report of a
divorce-case.

Concerning Victoria’s new Governor :
On January Ist, 1901, a.d., the poor little glory of
Australian State governorship departed this life. New
Governors are being shipped to fill the local billets, mili-
tary men of more or less “ distinction,” with a strut and
a mannah, hut the multitude will set no store by these
unnecessary new-ehums. They are beside the mark.
’Opetoun is the mark. A gratifying result of Federa-
tion in Melbourne was visible last week when State-Gov.
Clarke came to hand, at St. Kilda pier, and was driven to
town forswearing-in purposes along thesame route that had
been taken by the awful Brassey some years ago. Brassey
of yore scored a great triumphal progress as compared with
Clarke’s journey to the Exhibition Building. Brassey
was treated as an event by crowds in the streets and
effusive loyalists on doorsteps and balconies. Clarke
went for a mere circumstance. Few people troubled to
put themselves in the way of seeing him pass, and, ex-
cept at those points where the idlers amounted to a
cluster, there was never a cheer. The “ typical Austra-
lian welcoming ” of a new State Governor from England
is going to be silent for the future as regards citizens in
the street. Probably a revival of “demonstration” will
mark the drive-past of native Australian Governors when
Time produces them.
A big effort was made to work up a show at the
Exhibition Building, but some hundreds of tickets for
reserved seats—out of the 4000 odd issued—seem to have
fallen on stony ground. The invitees stayed away in
such large numbers that back-seat holders came forward
to fill the gaps. Some grew tired of waiting for the
mislaid potentate and slunk away, leaving the “ missus ”
to sit out the function. The intense Britishness of it
all was rather irritating to the unofficial portion of
the audience. Municipal councillors came along by the
dozen in knee-breeches and long, warm robes, as though
they were perspiring together to excite ridicule. A cold
climate and a sense of old tradition are excuses for this
civic masquerade in the land of its origin, but as Mel-
bourne doesn’t trace a line of aldermen back to the
Crusades, the mummers who assess the rates from the
corner of Collins-street ought to be restrained from wear-
ing robes when the glass registers three figures in the
shade.
The display of old-world trappings was painfully
general. Lieut.-Gov. Madden sat in splendour to watch
his sun going down, and Barton wore an aspect of con-
scious gilt, which won' no pity for him, seeing that
Peacock contrived to get through his part of the job in a
thin frock coat and a loose, jaunty style. Why
this striving after British effect? What has
even Australian “ loyalty ” in its official bear-
ings got to do with Windsor uniform? Twelve
hundred school-girls provided the one true touch of
Australian nature, not by their sweet young warble of
“ God Save ” and “ Rule Britannia,” but by their free-
and-easy bearing as they marched to the organ-gallery.
The gew-gaws and formalities of grovel looked altogether
foreign to the community which had raised those 1200
women-children, so different from the timid maidens of
Gov. Clarke’s country. There was not one meek and lowly
curtsey-dropper in the whole crowd. They were game to
rush upon the dais and pinch his Excellency’s leg, if the
conductor had given them the word.
Sir George Sydenham Clarke—The Bulletin must
call him Syd. and his wife Lady Gov. Clarke, to distin-
guish them from their local namesakes —is much the
same cut of military person as the little Major-General or
Colonel Somebody or other who played Commandant of
the Vic. Forces ten years ago. He was a trim, bald-
headed, bantammy personage who soon passed out of
memory because he had no feature worth recolleoting.
Gov. Syd. Clarke bears a general resemblance to that
unremarkable has-been. A short K.C.M.G., with a
neat little nose, married to a tall, stately dame
whose aquiline feature spells character, and whose
•daughter takes after papa. Lady Gov. Clarke is
the most leading-lady Melbourne has ever met since
“ dear Lady Loch ” filled the bill. She came ashore in an
airy bonnet arrangement that symphonised with her fine,
upstanding silver hair. Threads of black among the
silvery whiteness of hair and bonnet ; graceful deport-
ment, slender waist for her age, and an eye that sums up
the situation. The limelight will be on my Lady Silver-
hair every time she takes the social stage.

CRICKET-MATCH.
HIM: “By Jove, that chap’s a great catch,”
HER: “ Indeed , he isn’t ! He’s only a junior in
the same office as my brother.”
How the law is swallowing up the dead
Tyson’s millions ! The Tyson legatees were mostly
people who knew very little about wealth till the
old cattle king’s pile came their way, and their
luck in wandering into scrapes out of which they
can only be extricated by four solicitors and eight
barristers is something awful. What with the
best girl, the law, the church and the property
agent, the hot sun is shining on Tyson’s snowball,
and it is spreading out to fertilise the earth.
* * * *
Some members of N.S.W. Law Institute
intend to challenge, before that body, attorney T.
M. Slattery’s assertion that it is a “common prac-
tice ” for solicitors to represent vendor and pur-
chaser in a property transaction, with the implied
duty to seek, on behalf of the former, as high a
price as possible : on behalf of the latter, as low a
price as possible.
* * * *
The Bulletin's term “ cohentingents,’
applied to Australia’s S’African troops, originated,
of course, in this paper’s disgust at finding
Australian pluck and muscle enlisted in the
service of the cold-blooded liars and exploiters of
London Stock Exchange. It was one Levy, an
ex-Australian, and his friends (several Cohens) who
first suggested a body of Australian bush men for
service in the golden country. But London Jewish
Chronicle innocently asserts that the “cohen-
tingent jest ” generally current in Australia is a
striking testimony to the prominent part borne
in the S’African war not only by English and
S’African Jews, but by their brethren in all parts
of the Empire. “ Cohentiugent ” certainly wasn’t
invented to mark the number of Jews who went
to the wax'. It was invented to mark the number
of Jews who stayed away from the war and egged
other people to go. This paper pointed out that
long ago one Jew died for the Gentiles, and that
now, to pay for that death, the Gentiles were
dying for the Jews at the rate of 60 per cent,
compound interest extending over 1900 years.
* * * *
Some Bathurst (N.S.W.) Jay Pees, an-
noyed at Attorney-General Wise cheating them
out of a sectarian shindy by his dignified rebuke
of Police-Magistrate O’Neill’s wild hurroo about
“ the godless public schools,” called a meeting to
further discuss the matter. Mr. O’Neill’s report
to bis Department on the incident was circulated
at the meeting. Some extracts :
Already the whisper is abroad, “ O’Neill will be shifted
over this,” but O’Neill, conscious of no wrong-doing on
his own part, and strong in the belief that he will suffer
no injustice at the hands of the Department he hasfiong
and faithfully served, stands erect with folded arms,
answering bigotry with the eloquence of silent contempt.
From a mother’s lips, in the far-away past, I received my
life motto : “ Always act the man,” and to-day she looks
down upon me from her place in heaven, when I say
the man does not exist who can point to a single incident
in my history that is darkened by the shadow of sec-
tarianism.
Now, could anyone have the heart to take a man
with such a gift of “ iloquence” seriously ?

The other day the cable announced that
the British Secretary for War had reprimanded a
British officer (the commander of the Connaught
Rangers) for falsely accusing the Boer commandant
Fouche of the murder of British prisoners. The
bogus “Boer atrocity” must be beginning to
smell pretty high.
