A WOMAN’S LETTER.
*, _ . Sydney, December 17, 1901.
Mt Dear Arini, —
In spite of all that our horse-poets have sung,
the cricket bat must be dearer to the people of
this State than the horse. Forty thousand people
at lasfcSaturday’s test match; close upon £5OOO gate-
money taken ! The Englishmen said that the
sight-from the centre of the field was the most
r : mark able of its kind they had ever seen. They
really enjoy the barracking, advice, and criticism
given from the shilling enclosure, and find them-
selves listening and waiting for the jokers’ half-
truths. Little paper bags df dust, which those in
the back seats oarry to hurl at others who obstruct
the view by standing up, also have a charm for
“them Englishmen” when well-aimed. I learn
that the Australian cricketers have nick-names,
more or less appropriate. When you hear a crioketer
cioaking of “Father and Mother and Gertie,”
you think of a nice family group, until you learn
that Darling is “Father,” Kelly “Mother,” and
H. Trumble “ Gertie.”
To secure a seat in the Ladies’ Reserve one had
to be there by 10 o’clock. Lunch-baskets were
plentiful. Those who had brought supplies tried
to keep the seats of those who had not, whilst the
latter-struggled into the tea-room. A large per-
centage of the women didn’t really know the
wicket from the bowling-crease, or the pitch
from that other pitch that can’t be touched
with safety. Fm speaking of the crush on the
lawn. Non-cricketesses on the Stand sustained
themselves by studying the toques in front of
them. One could see little but millinery in such a
crush.
Froeking at the match was gorgeous, but not
tiff the play was over, and the silken trains and
the oceans of'flounces were billowing across the
dusty road to cabs, carriages, ’busses and trams,
could they be fairly seen. It was a flood of frocks
of all shapes, shades, and grades of beauty—white
predominating—but they never took form long
enough to make any distinct impression. One
dainty creation swung a moment on the step of
its owner’s carriage—a pointed overskirt, open-
vested corsage of the palest of sage-green cloth :
three‘lines of the finest of black velvet ran round
the outlines, and the skirt was slashed with cerise
pink; -the ehiffou that gathered round the
neck and billowed up from the open corsage
was pink, with a toque to match. The wearer
just stood on the step long enough to reveal that
from the feathertip of her toque to the tan-shod
foot with its embroidered hose, she was perfect.
The dust and grime of the sweltering afternoon
had passed her bv. Floral muslins were every-
where. A girl swept by in something that looked
like superfine Assam silk, flounced by itself and
set off with a turquoise-studded belt, the joints of
which were petals of a lily; her biscuit-colored
hat was lost in a wilderness of chiffon and roses.
Three girls, hanging en the arms of johnnies who
looked like animated window-figures from their
pink cheeks to their varnished boots, were in fawn
silk. When the sun shone on it the fawn turned
red, and in the shade it glowed like a tarnished
emerald, and gave the fluffy trinity a chameleonic
air.
Lord Hopetouh, attended by Captain Walling-
ton and Major Philson, was present on Friday
and Saturday, coming early and staying late, and
reading the evening papers in the pauses of the
game. He strolled through the members’ reserve
with George Reid (the latter in an ugly little
German-bun hat that was calculated to for ever
discredit the M‘Million's importing policy), and
M.L, A. Jessop's loud voice oalled for a cheer for
“ the best man in Australia.” Then some cheered
the Governor and some the Dry-Dog.
Rosehill race meeting was largely advertised as
Ladies’ Day. Perhaps that is why men went
there in train-loads, and women stayed away. A
few unswerving feminine horse-lovers, who would
probably rather go to a race meeting at Mil-
parinka than see King Edward himself at the
wicket and his heir bowling, were present. But
the keen, gusty wind that blew over the roses and
the hills had few coiffures to ruffle. Half
the “Florodora ” sextette wore out the afternoon
on the lawn and in the paddock ; one of them in
a pretty green linen, another in a “simple white
muslin,” tucked and lace-trimmed into costliness.
In a second-class carriage (the firsts were rushed
by men, it being ladies’ day, you know), coming
home, a middle-aged widow, who had the look of
having done hard work in the Never Never, gave
her reasons for not re-marrying. “Me husband
was as good a man as ever lived, and a woman
doesn’t draw two big prizes in a sweep—at least
it ain’t likely. Tf I’d a bad husband I’d have tried
a second one.” Lightning never does strike twice
in the same place.
Our new Mayoress, Mrs. Tom Hughes, second
daughter of the late Dr. Gilhooley, is tall, fair,
brown-haired, and young. She is a smart dresser,
with a good figure, and in her unmarried days
was by way of being an artist. A member of the
St. Cecilia choir, she is a singer and player of
skill, and a philanthropist of note. St. Margaret’s
Maternity Home is one of her pet charities. Mrs.
Hughes has two sons. Her sister is also married
to a Hughes—two brothers married two sisters.
The position of Mayoress has always struck
me as a singularly unpleasant one. In her
short term of office Mayoress Graham acquired
a worried look, a business-like voice, and a
hurried walk. She was always rushing from one
charity meeting to another, or presiding at a circle
to raise funds to send the latest infantile prodigy
on the piano to Europe, or opening a bazaar in a
hot, dusty suburb, or being At Home at the
Town Hall to a horde of persons who said (as they
say of everybody in turn) that she was very un-
fitted for her position if she wasn’t able to keep
np the conversation with them at the point where
it was left off at the last At Home.
Exoerpt from Mrs. Doherty’s reminiscences of
places where she had in her youth officiated as
“ general ” :—“ It was all right at Mr. Barandy’s;
everything up to the knocker; no ’ot Sunday
dinner, they bein’ very religious. And the boss
wasn’t a bit stuck up, as ’is ’obby was making red-
’ot love to servant-girls.”
As Shakespeare has it “the readiness is aIL”
At a recent picnie, a rising young Sydney dentist
had the carving of a finely-skewered turkey, the
skewers of which sternly resisted extraction until
at last Mr. Pultusky, with a professional wave of
the hand, drew from his pocket a pair of forceps,
and, telling the turkey to “keep still, Miss, and
I won’t hurt you,” had the skewers out in an
instant.
In one of the larger West Coast towns (Tas-
mania), where everybody knows everybody and
mostly everyone lives in two-roomed cottages, a
lady, a recent arrival, was bemoaning the faot
that there were absolutely no class distinctions.
“In fact,’’she went on hopelessly, “there have
been women calling upon me who admit having
milked cows and other things ” —probably ducks.
Consider the inappropriateness of things as
sometimes carried out in Australian country parts.
At a certain township a grand conoert was held in
aid of the local cemetery. Three comio songs and
a comic duet were among the items. Possibly
the comic contributors lost sight of their objective,
else their items must have sounded tragic. At
another entertainment, the recitation “ Moloney’s
Cat ” was followed by the singing of the Doxology.
And the queer association of ideas that some
people have ! Heard a widow planning to raise
funds to provide a headstone for her husband’s
grave and to get her youngest daughter a set of
artificial teeth.
A little girl friend of mine recently showed me
her essay on the commandment, “ Honor thy
father and thy mother,” wherein she set forth
that children should pray for the “ warfare ” of
their parents. She also states that the “lang-
wige ” somp children, especially the elder ones,
use is “ dispeotful,” and gives, as a sample, the
five words, in semi-phonetic spelling, that are
always handed on a slip of paper to the Bench
when a blushing policeman comes into court to
give evidenoe, which “ can’t be spoken, your
Honor.” In giving synonyms for a list of names
she described wedlock as “a cheap kind of pad-
lock that you can buy for sixpence when it’s
rusty.”
Why don’t the New South Wales Railway
Commissioners reserve special tram - cars for
women ? The indiscriminate tram is a
horror. A woman with an expensive, beautiful
dress gets in and takes the seat just vacated by
the man who has been asphalting the pavement,
her nearest neighbor a Chinaman with vegetable
stains on his rags and smelling like a dirty cellar.
Both have as much right to their seats in the car
as the lady, and perhaps the smoker is full, but
her expensive dress is ruined. It is the man who
stands on the car platform and expectorates all
over the space near the doorway that piles up the
horror. Awhile ago I drew a conductor’s atten-
tion to such a man. The car—an electric one—was
not a smoker, and he was alone on the platform and
had the roadway on either side of him for a cus-
pidor. His amazement at being requested to
“ stop that or get off ” was comic, and he got off,
but the state of the platform was sickening.
It seems to be the custom for German-bred
people and nuns to kiss on both cheeks. On a
reception-day at a convent you’ll see the old-girl
pupils and their mothers receiving the two-cheek
peck with unfailing regularity on arrival and de-
parture. When the Duke of York got back to
London after his tour his father “ embraced him
warmly and kissed him on both cheeks,” which
salute, the reporters called “a hearcy British
greeting.” This “British” greeting you mav
see any week on the out or incoming Teutonic
liners. It may be a kingly custom ; more likely it
was just the German blood showing.
Mdlle. Maud Celinski begins a series of musical
fantasies entitled “The Arcadian Nights” at Syd-
ney Palace Theatre on Saturday next. Her pro-
framme, bearing the names of illustrious poets to
e “illustrated,” looks very attractive.
The death in Florence (Italy) of Mr. Robert
Prendergast, the well-known brewer, will send
his wife back to Sydney, where until two years
ago she was the leader of R.O. society, and noted
for her charities and wonderfully youthful figure.
Mrs. Prendergast is a sister of Sir Nicholas
Fitzgerald, of Melb. She has four sons. The
eldest is assayer on the Mt. Morgan mine, the
second is on one of the Prendergast stations, and
the other two.are at school in England.
Miss Hilda Mulligan, a fragile-looking, fair
little 19-years-old soprano, gave her first profes-
sional recital before the Professional Musicians’
Association last week. The room was too small
to allow her voice to be heard to advantage, but
its promise is uncommon, and, when funds are
forthcoming, she will go the way of other song-
birds for study. Miss Mulligan is a grand-niece
of Vincent Wallace, of “Maritana” fame; and
if history repeats itself, has got the right sort of
name to work a boom with.
Among the passengers who left for the laud of
wooden nutmegs and medical and religious fakes
by the Ventura on Tuesday was a palmist person
who is sent to America by subscription to make
further study of Christian Science under Mrs.
Eddy, the lady with the enormous banking
account. A palmist is just about the proper
person—that is, if no crystal-gazer is available
—to study under Mrs. Eddy. Mr. and Mrs.
Bernard Wise went to Maoriland by the same
steamer. While Mrs. Wise looks round the
Sounds and things, the K.C. will study the work-
ing of the M.L. Arbitration Act, and won’t give
himself any recreation at all.
Mary Eddy, announced by Sydney D.T. as the
“ founder and discoverer of Christian Science ’
but not very enthusiastically described by an
Australian lady-worshipper of her cult, who went
on a pilgrimage to the apostle—is said to be worth
about £1,500,000. Exploiting a good idea—which
she didn’t discover, but which is older than
Christianity and nearly as old as Thought—Mrs.
Eddy surrounded it with the hokus-pokus mum-
mery which seems essential to a new experiment
in the religious line, and so makes magnificent
money. There are quite a number of Christian
Scientists in Sydney, but since the report above-
mentioned of one enthusiast who personally visited
the high-priestess, they don’t subscribe nearly
so much to Mrs Eddy’s fortune.
Sydney palmists are at present exhibiting pic-
tures of General Buffer’s hand, and pointing out —
now J—the lines which indicated that, “through
indiscreet loquacity,” he would fall from high
office. How marvellously accurate these past
futurists are—after the fact!
A belated tale of the Census to hand: The
squatter was filling in the paper, and one of his
female servants had given her age as 34 ; he hinted
that he wouldn’t object to a few years subtracted,
but She persisted, and finally he urged that
a misstatement of her years would stand in the
way of her old-age pension. “That’s another view,”
she replied ; “put down what I am—s 3.” Concern-
ing old-age pensions. The authorities, in making
inquiries, have brought out some funny points.
Young men of 30 have married ladies of 70 or
over, and the latter are now applying for pensions,
having settled property on their young husbands.
This anecdotelet is recommended to notice of
the Societies of Art in our midst: Two women
were looking in a shop-window at a picture to be
disposed of by art union. “What a lovely pic-
ture !” said one. “ What is it called ?” “ Called 1”
said the other, laboriously reading the particulars
attached, “ Oh, it’s an art ‘ unknown ’ ! ”
If any politician wants to discover a royal road
to political eminence, let him go round among the
oheap streets and lanes, and interview the aged
and broken women who live on their old-age pen-
sions and the proceeds of a little washing. He
could collect enough pathetio fact to wreck any
Ministry that would even hint at reducing the
present miserable allowance by a fraction of a
farthing.
Concerning dogs: I know a lovable, middle-
aged collie, owned by a first-class family. He has
a curious fad. A year or so ago he picked from a
dustheap an old 21b. jam tin. It was bashed and
riddled with holes, but he brought it home, and
has never allowed it out of his sight since. He
plays with it all day, and takes it to bed with him
at night. Once someone threw it a parish away,
but collie tracked it down and recovered it. He
met other jam tins in his search, quite new ones,
that had once held marmalade, witn pretty labels
stiff adhering, but he was not seduced from his
first and battered love.
This is the 20th season of the Sydney Lieder-
tafel, and it is an institution that all music-lovers
would do well to join. The annual subscription
is only £1 Is., for which trifling sum the subscriber
receives three tickets for each of three grand con-
certs, and one gentleman’s ticket and one lady’s
ticket for each smoke - concert —which is the
cheapest supply of good music that I know of.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The State Parliament and its feminine de-
tachment were just in the humor to beam on an
imported Governor after several hours of the
breezy ozone on the Hygeia, with luncheon in-
cluded. But after past experiences of-the swagger
of the Brasseys, the amateurish stiffness of the
Maddens, and the aloofness of the Countess, they
were nearly paralysed with delight at finding the
Clarkes so very affable—say a little too affable to
last. The bows and wreathed smiles extended
by the new arrivals to the hand-shakers, and
vice versa, made the straight, stiff aide-de-camp in
the rear smile over and over again. He contrived
for a long time to keep his countenance, but,
finding the gushing shakes increasing in warmth
and vigor, he let his laugh go, and defied the
Fates. Mrs. F. C. Mason, who always takes
things quietly, and Mrs. Peacock, who does ditto,
presented posies to the Lady and Miss Clarke, and
made little fuss about it. It was the oleagin-
ous stream of M.’s.P. and their fair attachments
that made the first reception of the Clarkes the
most gushing and effusive affair in all the world.
The State law-makers were so delighted to find
an imported Somebody to wring their hands
warmly and look proud and pleased to meet them
that they nearly fell on his neck and wept.
“Like most men of diminutive stature, His
new Ex. has a habit of calling himself to attention
and standing right up to his inches every now and
then. At these times he spreads his shoulders
and straightens his back tiff he looks almost tall.
Miss Clarke reminds one just a little of Mrs.
Freeman Thomas—of the same height, the same
build, the same complexion, something of the
same vivacity. Miss Clarke is a factor for our
girls to take into account. She has more
attractiveness than was dreamt of by those
who absorbed accounts of her accomplishments,
her musical talent, her ‘ model daughter ’ attain-
ments. But the Governor’s one ewe lamb is fresh
and frisky—despite all this weight of learning and
seminary gilt. One is prepared to be interested.
‘Yes, they had such a delightful voyage, and were
so anxious to get here; and are sure they will be
so happy here, and they know such a lot about
Australia,’ etc., etc.
“ One snap-shotter in skirts turned her, camera’s
eye on the Hygeia at St. Kilda pier, and gave the
astonished Clarkes an idea of Woman’s position
at the front here. The Pier, you must know, was
cleared of the crowd. Only the blue-jackets were
left, and several military offioers with much gilt
about their inflated chests. The camera-fiends
formed a separate detachment, and this one
vigilant female fronted the lot, with her instru-
ment of torture. The hand that adjusts ths
camera rules the fashionable world.
“ Up at the Exhibition Building, Sassiety dames
frizzled in ill-humor and prickly-heat during the
long hours of waiting. Treasurer Turner’s wife
alone sat smiling all through the ordeal, undaunted
and alert. Her hubby, on the other hand, let his
drowsy head droop upon his gilt-edged Ul l^° rm
and forgot the World, the Flesh, and the
Governor. John Forrest, a colossal figure in gut,
grew more like a beet-root every moment, but
cracked jokes and drew many a laugh from nis
tired neighbors. Fringes drooped, faces grew
moist, fans fell limp from apathetic fingers, ana
womankind sat inert, tired of everything. Ad-
jacent chiffons ceased to interest them, complexion
veils at last failed to court conjecture, the long,
sultry hours took the gilt off vanity and made the
whole world kin. In the front of the dais, Judge
Williams’ scarlet robes held their own, and Miss
Muriel Williams scored above all. the frilly
frockers by a black and white creation, as pro-
nounced as the capital head-lines of an American
yellow journal.
“Sassiety made the acquaintance of the new
Exes, at Wednesday’s polo match on the new
Caulfield course, where a syndicate of swagger
sports now booms polo as it was never boomed
before. Some of the smartest rode out on highly-
polished hacks—amongst them Miss Blanche
Watson, of the hunting clan, and Miss ‘ Beaulieu ’
Buckley, just fresh from globe-trotting. . The
Govt. House contingent drove out with their full
ice-cream escort and outriders. The new Gov.,
quietly dressed in tweeds, deepened the previous
day’s impression that he will do very nicely if he
doesn’t incline towards militarism. When intro-
duced to a damsel fair or buckramed dame, Sir
George gives his hat a great sweep through the
atmosphere, bows his bare crown, and falls into an
easy position for a chat. Something of a gallant
he seems.
“Lady Clarke and her daughter might have
been but just returning from their reception, for
they wore the same frocks, the same vivacious air,
and the same smiles as on the previous day.
The party was enclosed by an impromptu
fence of wooden rails. Mrs. Philson left her little
hubby to look after himself, while she talked to
the Clarkes, swinging as she did so on the door
of the carriage. One of the Madden girls, in
billowy blue, hovered close to the party —not yet
used to being herded beyond the V.R. limits with
the vulgar majority. Sister Ruby peregrinated
with a youth. By the way, youths—the
clean, lisping species—are the fashion. No
smart woman who respects her reputation
in the gay world would be seen without one or
two of these callow danglers. One haughty
matron had two of the kind, and also, for a time,
the brand-new aide-de-camp, Captain Browning
(who, in busby and tinware, looks like Charles
Kenningham, doing comic opera ; and, in tweeds,
like a pretty, pouting pet addicted to the society
of his elders). At tea-time, he procured straw-
berries for Miss Muriel Cumming, and carried
lumps of sugar for S’Rupert’s dame. Mrs. Harry
Payne—a Toorak sporting dame, coming to the
fore rapidly—was one of the frilliest of the crowd,
in pink silk foulard, with expensive elaborations
of applique.
“ Mrs. L. C. Mackinnon and Mrs. W. Leonard,
who hosted it at the afternoon tea, where a
supply of ices and creams and wine-cups proved
as inexhaustible as the Tariff amendments, had
the most to say, naturally enough, to our new
Exes. Mrs. Leonard rolled some rich jokes off
her ready Milesian tongue, and sported a par-
ticularly smart costume of butcher-blue linen.
The Doorman Bayles’ dogcart was one of the
trickiest turns-out. The Frank Madden family
filled a brougham as seriously as all the profes-
sional talent might the mourning coach of a rich
patron. Mrs. Walter Cold ham, in bright yellow
muslin, was conspicuous to the eye.
“ Lord Hopetoun lost his jaundiced look after a
few rounds of polo, and later on shed his coat.
The sight of the exalted figure in silk shirt and
vest was a sight that the full-bosomed dames of
the gathering sighed to behold. He reminded
them of a thin paling.
“The Waxellency had a final splutter all to
himself on Monday of last week. During
the most crowded time of the ‘ Block ’
an orderly rode down Collins-street with
a rattle of spurs, blowing a shrill whistle.
Every ear was attracted, and every eye
was turned in his direction. Then all beheld a
carriage in which the Waxellency sat like a very
mighty monarch doing himself proud to give this
last glimpse of a great Representative of Royalty
to the people. A laugli went up and down
Collins-street and all round the Block.
“The Madden entourage marched in to ‘Gor
Save ’ accompaniment for the last time for a long
while, at Mrs. Green’s fancy hop, last Monday.
They went very slowly to their palmy place in the
balcony, as if to spin out the sweet time; whereat
there was a good deal of tittering among the
women, who have been hurt at the Maddens’
profound admiration for themselves.
“At this dance, Miss Florrie Green’s representa-
tion of the enchanting siren, in delicious shell-like
frills of black, heliotrope and silver coruscations,
was a triumph of dexterous posturing. After
her three successive dances of breath-catching
charm she completely collapsed.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
According to a country paper, Jimmy
Governor’s widow has married a half-caste.
The father of N.S.W. Judge Heydon and
solicitor Louis Heydon worked as compositor on
the Sydney Monitor in the forties.
If B. R. Wise climbs the N.S.W. bench,
his judgments ought to have a literary flavor;
and that will be almost a novelty in Australia.
N.S.W. Speaker M‘Court learned type-
setting in the office of Thomas Garrett, afterwards
Lands Minister and candidate for the Speaker’s
chair.
On Nov. 26, N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley
completed his 15th year as C. J. His Honor can
now, at any moment, retire on a pension-of 7-10ths
of his salary.
Mr. W. P. Crick, who announced himself,
a few weeks ago, as a mainstay of N.S.W. Par-
liamentary refreshment-room, is now said to be a
total abstainer.
Archdeacon Langley, nominated to be first
Bishop of Bendigo, is brother of Sydney Arch-
deacon Langley. Both followed secular businesses
throughout early manhood.
Mr. Justice Parker (W.A.) declined to sit
in a recent case, as relatives of his were appearing
for one of the parties. He would not sit in any
case where his kinsmen were counsel.
Lady Barkly, widow of the highest-paid
Governor that any Australian province ever
had, is said to be hard-up in London, her only
income being a £75 Civil List pension.
The Rev. J. J. Kenny, whose name
figured in the Coningham case, may have had his
course of conduct approved at Rome, but his
mission, at all events for some years to come, will
lie in South America.
During N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley’s
year’s leave offabsence, Mr. M. H. Stephen, senior
Pusine Judge, may be'acting C.J.; and Mr. R. D.
Pring an acting Judge, with a permanent appoint-
ment to follow—if B. R. Wise elects to stand out
of the way.
Dr. Joseph Stapleton, found dead at
Wallsend (N.S.W ) the other day, was a brilliant,
though eccentric, doctor, with some reputation as
a surgeon, and a hobby for literary study. He
was said to be one of the best Shakespearean
soholars in Australia.
Memo, for Mayor Hughes : Sydney’s two
latest ex-Mayors (Graham and Matthew Harris)
both lost their Parliamentary seats and their
Mayoralties almost simultaneously. By the way,
Mr. Hughes (not yet 39) is the youngest alderman
who lias reached the chair.
Judge! Walton, the newly - appointed
Judge of the King’s Bench, London, is
brother of storekeeper Walton, of Rock
hampton (Q.). Latter was one of the
first settlers on the Barcoo.
*n 65, Melb. Argus tried the ex-
periment of having a Presbyterian
parson—Jarrett—for its editor. His
reverence only lasted six months : he dis-
tinguished himself by writing a leader in
which he recommended, as a cure for all
the ills of the body politic, a careful
study of THE BOOK.
SOMETHING STRONG.
THE PUSH MONARCH (to intoxicated old gent,
who is vainly trying to rise from the ground):
“’Ere! I say, you silly old gheeser, why
don’t you lean on your bloomin’ breath?”
Among Australasia’s big dividend-re-
ceivers, one name is somehow rarely mentioned —
that of William Hart, who owns the biggest half
of the grand old Tasmania gold-mine. He has
averaged between £4OO and £5OO per week for the
last 22 years—which is very high up amongst
Australia’s records.
Discussing in M.L. House of Reps, the
item “ £l5OO to the Premier for expenses con-
nected with the coronation ” one member referred
to the Premier’s Jubilee trip expenditure as
“some £2O a day, although champagne and all the
other necessaries of life were found by the
Imperial Govt.” The necessities of a Radica
Premier !
Judge Docker (N.S.W.) is a judicial mar-
tinet whose cruel sentences and insolent abuse
of juries are alternated with studies in amateur
photography. Judge Windeyer, when' he wasn’t
hanging a man, delighted to attend and weep at
sentimental amateur theatricals.
One Argue (by name and by nature) has
been lecturing in N.S.W. recently. Some of his
subjects: “ John Norton and Morality ”; “John
Norton and Conscience”; and John Norton in
various other ways. And it is wonderful how the
name of John Norton draws a crowd.
Strange how the prefix “ Sir ” kills Aus-
tralian politicians. “Sir” G. Dibbs, “Sir” A.
Stuart, “Sir” J. Dickson, “Sir” G. Berry,
“Sir” J. Forrest, “Sir” J. Abbott, “Sir” H.
Nelson, and a score of others all seem to have
been slain politically by the charm or bane.
Ex-serrior constable A. L. M. Steele, the
Nemesis of the Kelly gang, recently retired from
the force, but still resides at Wangaratta, Vic.,
within coo-ee of the meeting of the waters of the
Ovens and King. He now cultivates garden
blooms, and is a noted prize-taker at flower-shows.
Australian explorer Eyre, who died the
other day in England aged 86, incurred much
Exeter Hall odium in ’65, when Governor of
Jamaica, by his rigorous suppression of a black
rebellion. Eyre was recalled and never employed
again. Nor was he ever, like the other retired
Governors, knighted. Eyre’s opinion on a White
Australia would have been valuable.
At Ashburton (M.L.), when Premier Sed-
don was parading with the Yorks, some one in the
crowd shouted, “Good old Dick!” whereupon
R.S. smiled and bowed graciously. But when a
beery individual yelled “Goo’ ole Bun-Tuck!”
he frowned and snorted “ something awful.” By
the way, Seddon, who now weighs over 20st., has
of late been establishing in % variety of ways his
claim to be regarded as a purely-oomic personage.
If Rev. W. J. Carr-Smith does not get the
Bishopric of Goulburn, he will shortly warm himself
with- an apron somewhere else. Lord Beauchamp
took him to his bosom, and bragged of him over
the oountry as the future Archbishop of Sydney,
and it is wonderful how closely C.E. clergy and
upper-class laity notice the words of a Governor.
Beauchamp had it in mind for a while to teach
catechism in Carr-Smith’s church, but was
reasoned out of it by more sensible people.
The unfortunate General Hector Mac-
donald-dropped in at Fremantle on Dec. 2 on his
way to England in the Ormuz, and though he had
but five hours at his disposal, underwent a
welcome by the Frpmantle Municipal Council,
another by the Caledonian Society, a luncheon at
Govt. House, and a reception and presentation in
Perth. He returned to his steamer richer by a
possum-rug, a gold-mounted jarrah walking-stick,
and a portentous poetic greeting in Gaelic.
Melbourne Age has had a good succession
of editors, beginning with David Blair (a ship-
mate from England with the late Dr.
Garran, of S.M. Herald), who was
a first-class discursive litterateur.
Ebenezer Syme, a profound
thinker and vigorous writer,
was cut off by death as soon
as he had established his
name. Then the chair
passed to G. P. Smith, a
splendid slang-whanger,
»hose style was founded
on that of Wm. Cob-
bett, of whom Hazlitt
quoted “he had the
back-handed trick bet-
ter than any man in all
Illyria. ” G. P. ’s article
on “ Men PootPes,”
aimed at Captain Stan-
dish and one or two
other Melb. Clu b cele-
brities, attracted so
much attention that it
was posted on card-
board and exhibited in
city shop windows.
James Harrison, who
followed, was a thor-
oughly sound and care-
ful man. The office
gossip always credited
him with having at
least a little to do with
the authorship of the
book on political econ-
omy which bears David
Syme’s name on its
tide-page. There was
not much in the way
of political intrigue
that was not' known
to Geo. Collins Levey,
his successor. A. L. Wind-
sor, who came next, and
reigned 25 years, was a well-
equipped, all-round literary man
who acquired his style from con-
stant study of the Saturday Review in its best
days, and who narrowly escaped being one of the
essayists of his century.
The curtain should now be definitely rung
down on the Baden-Powell farce, with the presen-
tation of that “sword of honor from Australia.”
“Australia,” in this instance, largely represented
the more-or-less unwilling victims who parted
pence in response to the cadging of various young
Evangeline Snookses with penny-collecting cards
—said little missies having had their collecting
industry stimulated by the hope of a public pat
on the back from the Sydney kitchen-gossip organ.
The death of Mrs. Charles Daniel O’Connell,
in England, brings out the fact that the Sydney
Rileys, of £5-bale of drapery fame, are related to
Irish Demosthenes Daniel O’Connell. The lady
just deceased was daughter of Edward Riley, and
her husband was related to Maurice O’Connell.
Old citizens remember the row between Alderman
Harris and Alderman Riley, when the former
charged the latter with saying, one time, he was
of Irish desoent, and later denying his Irish
extraction and claiming to be of an old English
family.
MOTHER'S CARE.
As exampled in sundry inquest and hospital cases of late.
The charge of indolence against Barton,
the Federal Constitution-maker, is, curiously
enough, a repetition of that urered by S.M.
Herald against Wentworth, the N.S.W. State
Constitution-maker, when, in July, 1843, he was
a candidate for the Speakership of the new Leg.
Council: “Of steady and sustained application
we believe him to be quite incapable, and if he be
called to the chair we are satisfied its duties will
be performed in a fitful, slovenly manner dis-
creditable to himself and prejudicial to the public
interest.”
It would not be surprising if N.S.W.
Attorney-General Wise appointed Mr. C. E.
Pilcher, Q.C., to the Chief Justiceship. Mr.
Pilcher probably makes, say, £6OOO or over
annually at the Bar, and his appointment would
mean a distribution of this money among other
wigged leaders, of whom (though this is a side-
issue) B. R. Wise would get the bulk. The
main consideration is that of all names mentioned
for the position Mr. Pilcher’s is the most popular
amongst the Bar men having claims as legatees to
that £6OOO.
“Baldy” Davis, from Bourke, told N.S.W.
Assembly that, during 18 yearn’ pioneering
in the Western Division, he paid £55,000 in
interest. A member interjected—“ You got into
the hands of the Jews.” “No, I didn’t,” said
Baldy. ‘‘ I got into the hands of a good, honest,
Scottish firm.” If Mr. Davis had taken his
troubles to Jewry in the first instance, the end
would have been more speedy in coming, but he
would have probably paid in the aggregate less
interest. These “good, honest, Scottish firms”
know their business.
Fifteen years ago, Thomas Hall, of Christ-
church (M.L.), was sentenced to life-long im-
prisonment for the attempted murder of his wife
by poison. Maoriland law isn’t liberal in the
matter of divorce, but it gives a woman the right
to her freedom if her husband is sentenced to a
term of over seven years for trying to take her
life, and Mrs. Hall appealed for and got her
divorce a few days ago. Away back in 1886
there were many affluent men who would have
gladly married the heroine of the great poisoning
case, but 1886 is long ago.
Mr. T. H. Nesbitt, Sydney’s new town
clerk, is, at any rate, no end of a fine Freemason,
being a member of Tynwald Lodge, No. 1242,
Douglas; W.M. of Spencer Walpole Lodge; a
Deputy Provincial Grand Master; a J.W. ; a
P.P. Senior Grand Warden ; a Royal Arch of A
Mole ; a First Principal; a Past Assistant Grand
Director of Ceremonies; a Mark Master; a
P.P.G.I.G. ; a Rose Croix; and a dozen or so
other things. The Bulletin trusts that some of
these weird honors argue a capacity for dealing
vigorously with street stinks.
King Dick of Seddoiiland has a son, until
a year ago a youth with an adolescent fringe on
his upper lip, the polisher of a stool in a Welling-
ton office. Young Dick had never been a volun-
teer. but his rotund Royal father thought he
would be a fit and proper subject to lead M.L.’s
bone and sinew against the Boer. Getting young
Dick’s commission was an easy matter, and
then “Bobs” begged H.M. Dick, as an especial
favor, for the loan of his gifted son as a staff-
offieer. Now Maoriland has upended itself in
frantic joy on the return of Captain Seddon, and
Dick swells his chest with joy and pride at
“my boy, my boy” as he calls the heavens to
witness that he, too, has given to the Hempire of
his own flesh and blood.
There is a little conspiracy hatching in
Van Dobson Land to confirm Alfred Dobson in
his billet as Agent-General in London at I*6oo a
year, and at the same time appoint a commercial
agent at a good salary to do the work that Alfred
is supposed to do, but doesn’t, because it would
be beneath the dignity of the office. Alfred will
be supposed to shave very carefully every day and
look as nice as possible for the £6OO per annum ;
while the other fellow bustles about and inquires
the price of butter and blue-gum sleepers. To ex-
pect a member of the Van Dobson family to move
round among hide and tallow stores, even for
£6OOO a year, would be preposterous. Anyhow,
the confirmation of any Agent-General in his
office, in view of the probable early abolition of
the States’ A.G.’s, as such, ought to be regarded
with much suspicion.

Miss Rose Scott, Sydney’s Woman
Suffrage leader, recently visited and inspected a
large N.S.W. country gaol, a special departmental
permit having to be obtained to overcome the
regulation against women visiting the men’s
quarters. The prison choir was specially told off
to sing “Te Deums” and the musical “I’m
Saveds ” of Sankey. This delightful official
subtlety was a huge success. The impressionable
lady willingly succumbed to the idea that prison
is a paradise, and stated, in big handwriting, “ I
am much comforted by all I’ve seen.”
Charles Sublet, a hearty old citizen of
Rochester (Vic.), has reason to remember the
eventful dawn of Sunday, Dec. 4, ’54, for he was
on sentry duty, with two others, at the corner of
the Eureka Stockade which was first attacked by
the Imperial troops. One of his companions was
shot dead at the first fire, and Capt. Vignol, who
led the attack, fell, shot in the thigh. The Stock-
ade defences consisted of two parallel logs,
against which slabs were fixed in upright position.
Sublet and his other mate found themselves undlr
the slabs, at the point of attack (for the logs
rolled over with the impetus of the stormers), and
escaped to their tents.
“ Cecil P.” to The Bulletin
Octogenarian R. A. Henderson, original discoverer of
lode tin in N.S.W., is still at Gulf Creek (Emmaville) tin-
field in that Sta e. He came out as a soldier in the 11th
Foot, and formed one of the guard of honor at the open-
ing of the first Australian railway. Some time ago, the
N.S.W. Govt. —mainly owing to work by Opposition
leader Lee—gave him a pension, but since the old-age
scheme has been in force this has ceased. Now, if Govt,
was justified in first granting this, it would be only logical
to continue it—whatever else may accrue from general
legislation. The old chap, who has not entered a town
for nine years, rises at four and tramps, jlone, miles away
over the mountains—driven by the spirit of discovery.
He and his wife are thrifty, kindly, and universally re-
spected.
Those who bungled N.S.W. Superannua-
tion Fund may be indirectly charged with
accelerating the death of Superintendent Read.
He has died in harness at 72, but had he been
able to retire on pension, as he was entitled to do,
and visit the old land, as he desired, he might
have added several years to the score. He was 52
years in the police service, 45 in N.S.W. and six
in England. He was one of the 40 who arrived in
Sydney per ship Bangalore in 1855 to form the
nucleus of the present force. It was cruelty on
the part of the Government to keep him in har-
ness so long. His pension would have equalled
his salary, and by keeping him on £470 a year was
saved.
When Thomas Broughton, ex-Mayor,
ex-M.L.A., celebrated his 91st birthday—and for
the last 40 years he loved to celebrate these occa-
sions—he claimed to have had only one rival in
N.S.W. in the number of such public commemor-
ations. That was the late Chief-Ju stiee Stephen.
Other men have lived longer, and in some, way
celebrated birthday anniversaries from time to
time, but Broughton and Stephen made the occa-
sions practically public festivals. Broughton, the
oldest Sydney Mayor, dies at 92, just as Hughes,
the youngest Mayor, takes the Sydney chair at
the age of 39. Broughton’s birthday celebration
lasted into his marriage celebration, as the days
were to-day and to-morrow. His widow is 80.
Acland Oronliyatekha, charged with indecent
assault on a lady typewriter, at the Melb. office
of the Independent Order of Foresters, is said to
be a full-blooded Mohawk Indian. His father,
who is also the “ father ” .of the 1.0. F., was the
young student chosen—so the story goes—to
deliver an address to the Prince of Wales (now
Kifig), when he visited. Toronto University, long
ago. Having fired off his eloquent address in the
orthodox cap and gown, Oronhyatekha came for-
ward again, this time in the war-paint and
feathers of a Mohawk chieftain, and welcomed the
Prince in his native lingo, subsequently translating
the speech into English. The Prince —so the story
goes—was so impressed by the brainy redskin
that he “shouted” him a trip to England and
placed him at Oxford University under the guar-
dianship of equerry Acland. In due course the
original Oronhyatekha married a Mohawk lady of
royal lineage, and their first-born, the young man
now charged with amorous misconduct, was given
the name of Acland after the personage aforesaid.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Aboriginalities.
to Contributors -In the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
The Bulletin, the other day, printed a
wondrous sample of N.S.W. journalese. But
North Queensland can beat even that. A citizen
there, accused by a contemporary reptile of having
borrowed two shillings, finds room in the other
Deal paper for the milder part of his feelings thus
(after ->eing, as will be noted, severely pared
down by the editor)^
I was vilified with shameless audacity; and that too,
Great Heaven ! while in articulo mortis ! Had the paper
i« question only suspected that my life would he spared,
diend alone dread of the just chastisement which a
superior journalist was sure to inflict—would have saved
my character and reputation from its reckless, murderous
and unwarrantable onslaught. I make no apology, there-
fore, Mr. Editor, for tying this vile, villainous, and vindic-
rive paper up to the triangles of public opinion, and
publicly inflicting on it that figurative flogging, which its
sa\ agery and brutism have so richly merited. I rejoice
excei < ingly in my superior strength and superior intel-
lectuality ; and publicly challenge this literary assassin—
this journalistic vixen—to meet me in the columns of this
journal, and endeavour to defend itself from the odious
stai n of having tried to libel and ruin its neighbor in the dark
The paper is a liar and a slanderer, lacking only courage
to he an assassin. [More violent abuse here deleted.]
The action of the paper is in perfect harmony with its
every action since it commenced its miserable and ill-
fated career. [Some scathing expressions suppressed.] An
odious organ which is a vile satire on every other journal
in Australia. I detest its slimy ways, its meanness, its
unscrupulousness, its odious, glaring artificiality. This
wretched little paper [more virulent language cut out] is
as false as it is feeble, as cowardly as it is hypercritical, as
audacious.and vulgarly presumptuous as it is servile and
cringing.
X now arraigp this loathsome journalistic organism,'
this literary microbe, before the bar of public opinion :
and I do so in the interests of public peace and honest
journalism ; in the name of everything that is pure and
noble and holy ; everything that is bright and beautiful
and good ; in the name of that high heaven that bends
above us—that God we each adore. Finally, in the name
and in the interests of humanity, and in the broad, sacred
interests of liberty and truth.
But the North must be getting civilised, when
■editors plume themselves upon deleting all
“ violent abuse” from correspondent’s letters and
keeping them (as above) within the limits of
courteous debate.

The great Western country of New South

There is a good deal more of the same sort, but the
job of investigation grows wearisome. Roughly
speaking, one can draw a line from' Barringun, on
the Q. border, due south to the Lachlan, and then
say in a broken voice that everywhere west of that
line and west of the Lachlan, save around the
Broken Hill mines, man is a diminishing quan-
tity. Also, in several counties east of the line
above described —in Culgoa, Narran, Cowper,
Gunderbooka, Canbelego, Mouramba, Kennedy,
Dowling, Sturt, Gaira, Waradgery, &c.—he is
also growing fewer and beautifully less. The
area of diminishing population, taken by counties,
amounts to about two-fifths of New South Wales.

“ Farrier ” : Be rat-exterminating crusade.
Rat is stealing along top-edge of lining-board in
butcher’s shop—is biffed, and falls ’twixt weather-
board and lining. Next morning board is removed
to recover the carcase, but the other rats have
‘ ‘ scoffed ” it, leaving only the hide. Now, are rats
habitual cannibals, or do they only “ get outside ”
their dead mates as a sanitary precaution, when
unable otherwise to remove the corpse ? It cer-
tainly wasn’t hunger that compelled them in this
case to “pass in” their “passed out” friend.
* * * *
“ Kobbi ” : At Yeppoon (Q.,) is a Kauaka
who has been 22 years in Australia, and his wife
21 years. They have a family attending school,
and the man points out that to leave the kiddies
in this country (if the Kanaka Bill be passed)
would break up the family, while to take them to
the Islands might mean that they would be killed
as strangers.
* * • *
“ Bilby ” re Great West Land, N.S.W.
(B„ 23/11/’01): —
Can’t make out what “ P.X.” is driving at. A very few
lucky ones certainly made a pile in the early days by
selling out at boom-prices, before people knew what the
country really was, and when £5OO might have been
safely offered for any rabbit caught in the district, but
beyond this all is fair ruin ; and the financial companies
that tried to prop the thing up have all been hit very
hard. The fact that absurd prices were paid for many
stations has not made much difference ,in the long run.
The ruination would have come just the same if they had
got the country for nothing. The present trouble lies not
in the high price paid for the stations, but in the fact
that it is fast becoming impossible to carry any stock at
all. As for any western station-owner keeping up a
palatial city residence—why, his wretched drought and
rabbit-stricken desert of miles of cut scrub, sand-buried
fences, and dried-up tanks would drag him down to
poverty if he were a second Carnegie. Draw a straight
line from Brewarinna to Condobolin, and let the rest of
the map be bounded by the Lachlan and the Darling, and
I guarantee that for the last six years the genuine profits
from the whole of that vast extent of country would be
insufficient to run a mud hovel—to say nothing of a
palace !
» • » *
An aged drover, found in a state of col-
lapse on Bungaree (Vic.) road by a constable, and
speedily taken to hospital, was so grieved over a
couple of old “ billies” the bobby left behind that
the latter seriously thought of going back for
them—till he searched the old chap’s clothing.
Then he changed his mind. In the clothes were
£62 in gold and a deposit-receipt for £llOO. The
ancient died a few hours later, the doctors said, of
absolute starvation. As he had no relatives and
died intestate, the Treasury gets a lift. Another
victim of thrift 1
“H. J. M.” : Be
edible indigenous Aus-
tralian plants. About
two years ago a man,
whose horse threw
and deserted him in
the wild Queensland
South Coast district,
sustained life by eat-
ing fern pith until
found by a search-
party. The pith of
the cabbage-tree is a
good food, and may
be eaten raw —in
which state it tastes
almost like cocoa-
nut ; or boiled or
baked in the ashes, like
damper. The cabbage-like
portion is the part to use ; and
when boiled with corned beef
or bacon is not easy to distin-
guish from white-heart cab-
bage. Roasted under beef or
mutton it is still better, and
is a good substitute for arrow-
root or sago. The marsh-
mallow also makes a good
substitute for cabbage ; and
the buddawong nuts, when
crushed and soaked in run-
ning water for about 10
hours, may with safety be
boiled and eaten. They are very plentiful along
N.S.W. South Coast. The leaves of the pricldy-
pear also, when skinned and boiled, are accept-
able to a hungry or cabbage-sick man.

“ A.P.R.” : “ P.X.” (8., 23/11/01) admits
that the mortgage co.’s have turned out many
squatters in Western N.S.W. ; hut is he aware
that several of the co.’s themselves have nearly
gone “broke’ over western transactions? He
hints that the western settlers don’t deserve con-
sideration, because the de : reciation in land value
and the “ slump in wool ” “isn’t the State’s fault,”
and mentions that one squatter kept two servants,
and another had a fine house—which doesn’t
signify much if these men have involved a fortune
in the West. Would it be the “State’s fault ” if
half our province became a sandy desert, and if
the sturdy pioneers (not bloated-squatters) who are
sweating out the prime of their lives, hoping to
make a Hades habitable (for the good of the State’s
posterity), were allowed to abandon their pro-
perty —ruined? The voice of “P.X.” is that of
the pavement-tripper, not of the man who has
fought the heat, the drought, the rabbits, and the
maddening solitude of the West.

THE LANGUAGE OF THE BUSH.
“ Give us a virtuous lubra !”
“A what?”
“A square gin, phase.”
The now Western Land Board (N.S. W.)
will have to give an early decision covering the
rabbit question. Squatters and selectors are ex-
pected to keep their runs and leases clear of the
post, yet of what use is their trying to do so
while the vermin are allowed to run riot over
travelling-stock routes and Crown lands ? One
Far West homestead lessee complains that,
though for a month recently he poisoned hundreds
of rabbits nightly, they still swarmed in on him
from adjacent Crown lands.
» * » *
At Kilinore (Vic.), was recently fined a
farmer who for years has struggled his hardest
against therabbit pest, but who could, nevertheless,
be punished under the Vermin Destruction Act
for not performing an impossibility. Defendant
said that the struggle with the pest had cost him
two-thirds of his income, and that it was beyond
human power to cope with the rabbits, which, he
added, would be there as thick as ever long after
the Be.,ch and himself were mouldering in their
graves. The Argus correspondent reported the
ease as being a “ very amusing one.” Amusing !
* * * *
Be that long-promised N.S.W. Inspection
of Land-Boilers Bill, which seems as far off as ever,
writes “W.H.T.”: —
In the bush there'still exists a goodly number of old
fire-kettles—many of them actually held together with
fencing-wire. A son of mine, 1 found, was employed
driving one of these, and, in answer to my anxious in-
quiries as to his safety, lie said : “ Oh, the old tank is all
right. When I get a full head of steam on, she leaks all
over; I have counted 16 such safety-valves. The worst
of it is, when she gets hot, the wire round her expands,
and you might as well have nothing to hold her in.” Of
course, the young chap is not a qualified engine-driver—
a man who knew anything of the potentialities of a term
would not have been found in the same paddock with
such a murder-machine. And this matter of unqualified
“ driving” also urgently needs legislative attention.
South Australia’s Land Boilers Bill, which lias
hung fire for over 20 years, has just been thrown
out. Until a bishop is blown up, or a judge badly
scalded, the decayed boiler will apparently remain
a neglected danger and a dreary joke for small
wits.

“6xB” : Grass is now so scarce, and dry
feed so dear up N.W. Q. way, that people can’t
afford to keep horses; and in Kynuna, west of
Winton, goat-teams are used (goats live on bark,
manure, old rags, and newspapers) to cart water.
One turn-out consists of a neat little waggon
mounting two 36gal. casks and drawn by 12 goats.

Not long ago, the petroleum-boring co.’s
men, whilst putting down a bore at Eurie station,
near Walgett, N.S.W., struck, at a depth of
1600 ft., a tree, not in the least petrified, though
slightly decayed, and judged to be sft. to 6ft. in
thickness. Water was reached at 2700 ft.

“Emquad” : I knew an old couple in
New England who owned a most remarkable
cockatoo. He could do anything but work. If
you pointed a gun at him he would stretch out his
wings and yell “Murder!” He was a perfect
imp of mischief, and nothing was safe that he set
his eyes on. He would follow the boss about
when he was transplanting cabbages, and pull
them out as fast as the old man planted them,
and when discovered would fly to the missus to
escape sudden death. He wasn’t ungrateful
either, for when said missus died, cocky refused
food, and simply starved himself to death.

“ C.H.R.” writes from a
great distance : Re kitten-drowning.
“ H.L.” (11/5/01) asks, “Is this ex-
perience unique ? ” No, it isn t at
all. It is a miserable cruelty prao-
tised on dumb animals, especially
cats, by ignorant, unfeeling, hog-
like persons with no brains in par-
ticular, It would be as unreason
able to take a useless horse out in
a boat and drown him, when by
one bullet from a rifle his troubles
would be o’er. Generally the
latter course is pursued, presum-
ably because it would be too much
trouble to drag a moke perhaps a
mile or two to a waterhole. A
blow on the head of cat, dog, or
other animal, when a gun is not
handy, is sufficient, when carefully
administered, to cause death, or,
at all events, insensibility at the
first attempt. Cats have been
known to survive under water ten
minutes, and any person who
would unnecessarily prolong a
dumb animal’s agonies for that
period deserves to be kicked from
Carpentaria to the Bight. There
is nothing unique about “ H.L.’s ”
experience, or about “H.L.” him-
.self. He is simply a person wlio
deserves hanging.

“Cosmos ”: Some Bundaberg (Q.) planters
subsidise a tame parson, who is handy for showing
visitors around and explaining how happy the
hands are. One big plantation issues a Kanaka
prayer-book containing a very exclusive selection
of hymns and texts of the “ Servants, obey your
masters ” and “ future reward ” type. Once heard
the perambulating missionary doing his “ piece.’
“ Boys’ ” huts ; boys seated in bunks ; missionary
at one end. “ Sin allee same snake—
s’pose snake lie ki-ki your finger, you die
finish. S’pose sin ki-ki your heart, you die
—you no die finish— you go alonga big fella
fire —burn alia time. You want to die finish
but God he no let you die. He make you stop
alouga fire cause you been sin. Cli’ he
no like um sin. What for you tellum lie alonga
master? — that sin. What for you gammon sick
alonga master 'l—that sin. What for you steal um
time belonga master “l—that sin. What for you
stealum ki-ki belonga master?” Just here I
came away, sick. At one churchy mill the boss
unctuously prayed for “ those at sea ” —he was
expecting a cargo of niggers ! A man started at a
very “churchy” mill. He waxed eloquent at
prayer-meeting and was promoted. Preached, and
got a further rise. Preached some more, and got a
fancy cuff-and-collar billet. 'Then he had to let go,
and went on a glorious burst. Said that he found
works without prayer of no avail, and so had to
join the mob ; and his rather public explanation
affected the local holiness-market for some little
time. Reminds me that I once worked for a
religious dairyman near Auckland (stronghold of
sweaterdom, bethelism, and juvenile viciousness).
Dirty yard ; dirtier quarters ; hours, starlight to
starlight, including Sundays. Wages, Bs. per
week. Family ate superior tucker at same table.
Started to clean out our “ sty ” one wet Saturday
afternoon, and was told to do it on Sunday. Boss,
leader in local joss-house. Again, religious ships
are 'always mean ships. Churchy shellbacks are
also mean, dishonest, and dirty. Effect or coin-
cidence? I always myself, now, carefully avoid
employing “holy’’men.

Dear Bulletin. — Re paragraphs about
Australian barbarisms in “Safrica.” Can quite
understand same after once seeing a “bullocky ”
(one of whose bullocks had fallen from sheer ex-
haustion —sulks, he said) tie a rope to the beast’s
tongue and attach remainder of team thereto in
order, as the fiend grimly said, “ to coax him up.”
Result, tongue pulled clean out by the roots. —
“Greenhide.”

“Alma”: To say that stones drop more
frequently in mines in the small hours of the
morning is asserting that the law of gravity does
• not apply at 2a. m. just as at, say, 2 p.m.—which
is absurd. [Not necessarily. It may be asserting
that more fruit drops from trees in autumn than
in spring, because there is more to drop ; or it may
be asserting that sun and earth, pulling together,
pull harder than when pulling separately ; or it
may be asserting that inorganio things have a
rhythm of life, like organic things;—ora dozen
other assertions.] Looking back over perhaps 100
serious accidents here (Broken Hill) during the past
half-dozen years, through falls of earth, most, by a
long way, ocourred during the day ; while the
other class of accidents, such as falling down
chutes, or through the careless use of explosives,
or getting caught in machinery, nearly all occurred
during the night-shift, when men cannot be so fit
to work, and a second’s lapse of memory may mean
instant death.

“ Carisbrook ” : In championing the late
J J. Durack, “Spinifex” (8., 13/4/’01) suggests
to me that he is probably a personal connection of
some of the owners of “ indentured ” slaves. I
was myself acquainted with the late Durack, but
as he is “ the late,” I prefer to say nothing about
his treatment of the natives. The contention that
the nigger has a grudge against all white men
because “white man take my country” is pure
bunkum, to be swallowed only by those who
liave not been behind the scenes. I spent years
in the Nor’-West, and I know what I’m talking
about. Can “ Spinifex ” supply a single instance
of a “white fellow” having been strung-up (in
that part of A ustralia) for the murder of a black —
as against the dozens of cases in which niggers
liave been hanged for offences against whites?
* * * •
“ E.O’D.” : A belated “tight place”
item. Self, mate and a few wage-hands were
fencing in the Yic. Mallee. Mate’s young wife
cooking for camp ; water-supply a 400-gal. tank.
After one roasting day, when approaching camp
in the evening, we missed the usual smoke; also
the cook. The first man to approach the tank for
the wherewithal to ablute hastily retired with
blushes and blasphemy, and investigation dis-
closed the lady imprisoned in our reservoir. De-
siring a bath, she had got in per opening on top.
Her body swelled a little by contact with the
water, and she couldn’t get out until she cooled
and shrank after nightfall. Sceptic can test
truth of this by immersing his maiden aunt in
water for a few minutes on a hot day and
observing how she expands.

tarian creatures all drink as the kan-
garoo does.” It is beyond all
question that the kangaroo
fops—as do wallabies,
’possums, and
bears —surely
not flesh-eat-
in g animals.

THE QUEENSLAND BLACK
AS A MINING EXPERT.
CHILLAGOE (loq.): “You catoh ’em plenty copper
in big fellah hole !”
MINER: “ Yes, plenty of copper there, Chillagoe.”
CHILLAGOE: “Me no tink so. Copper big fellah
rogue. You get ’im plenty to-day, then ’im
copper no more to-morrow.”
Five
bushmen
were working
on one contract in
Gisborne (M.L.).
On one and the same
day three of them cut
themselves badly with their
own axes, one was injured by a falling tree, and
the fifth disabled himself by falling from a chop'
ping-rest to the ground.

“ Walgo” : Be longevity of birds, M‘Mahon
Bros., near Coonamble (N.S.W.), have a fine
cockatoo much over 40 years old ; and Mr. A.
Cook, of Walgett, had, not long ago, a magpie
alleged to be 47 when it died.

“M‘Coooy”: For “ Grecngum’s ” infor-
mation (13. 26/8/’99)—snakes will live without food
for 18 months or more. Frogs can also exist for
long periods without food. As for tortoises (I
know personally of a case in which a specimen of
Che'odina longicollis, at Burragorang, N.S W.,
lived on its back, ploughed-under, from June, ’69,
to June, ’7O.

Interesting to Australians who think of
going to London:—“Last October, during 31
days, there were just 62 hours 56 minutes of sun-
shine —an average per day of two hours.
Re the inefficiency of the blockhouse-sys-
tem in S’Africa. The Times urges “ that block-
houses should be placed not more than 400yds.
apart, and that they should be connected by wire
entanglements.” Good old Times ! That would
mean more than four times as many blockhouses,
four times as many men locked up in them, and
four times the expense. Does the Times know
what it means to live for months in a blockhouse
—to be shut up week after week with 15 or 20
others in what is really only a big box, with no
light but what comes through narrow loopholes;
unwilling to open the door for fear of bullets ;
no fresh air, no exercise ? That sort of thing
merely fits men for a lunatic-asylum. The
Times wants more C.l.Y’s. and more Australian
contingents. Well, the Australian who wants to
go to S’Africa to be shut up in a blockhouse is
welcome to it, provided the British Govt, will
supply the asylum he will have to be sent to when
it has done with him.
The Bulletin’s Christmas number has
been a great success. Copies can still be obtained
from the office at 6d. each, accompanied by the
necessary postage.
“The Bulletin Story Book,” a solid, well-
printed cloth-bound volume of over 300 pages;
containing many of the best stories and literary
sketches which have appeared in The Bulletin
during 20 years, is now ready. Price 4s. 6d. ;
post-free from Bulletin office.
Many Australians will gladly hear that
Nicholson, the English “free ear-drum” swin-
dler, has just got 18 months for fraud.

less stamps (of any State) accompany.

The most tiresome of “ poets” are those with “ missions.”
If the Australian poet can word-paint Australian Nature,
or any of the great emotions of the human heart,
his work is poetically complete. Any moral teaching,
economic education, incitement to strenuous purpose,
that may be got from his verses is so much surplusage—»
admirable of course, but in no wise essential to the poetry.

TO THE EDITOR OF THE AGE.
Kir.—ln 'the report that appeared in your
columns of the recent conflict that has been going
on in the bosom of the Victorian Artists’ Society,
and of the final resolutions of the council, that
body, composed of the principal artists in Mel-
bourne, lias been accused of “aiming at crushing
all aspirants in art,” and reminded that “ its
primary object was the encouragement of national
art.” Now, it isnot the “'pirants to are., but, to 1
speak plainly, the apes of art that
.are to be crushed. j

SYDNEY.


Is not civilisation a defiance of natural ten-
dencies? Put a man alone, say, on an island, supplying
him with all the accessories of modern civilisation, and
would he continue the usages of polite society, or would
he revert, in the long run, to savagery ? “ Fingers were
made before forks.” Would the man, for instance, take
to eating his meals with Nature’s utensils, instead of con-
tinuing the use of those provided by Custom? If anyone
with a craving for research would care to make the ex-
periment and will provide the island, we, for our part,
will provide the following: Ivorine Table Knives, at 12s. ;
Dessert Knives, at 10s., (Carvers to match, ss. 6d.) ; Nickel
Silver Table Forks, at 10s. ; and Dessert Forks, at 7s. per
doz. The offer remains open indefinitely. M'Lean Bros,
and Rigg, Ltd., Elizabeth-st., Melb.*

Miss Susie Vaughan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss Vaughan said:—“l believe that ‘Lady
Hunt worth’s Experiment ’ will be the plum of the
Broughs’ programme. Carton, the author, is a
tiny little chap, and his wife a fine, tall woman.
She is Miss Catherine Compton, the actress. The
parts in ‘Lady Hunt worth,’ and ‘Lord and Lady
Algy ’ and ‘ Wheels within Wheels ’ were written
specially for her by her talented husband. She’s
the pivot on which the play turns, and she speaks
those quiet, telling lines with effect. She
doesn’t assist Carton in his play-writing. She
leaves it all to him. Carton writes beautifully. I
love his plays. He brings everything home and
makes it sweet and wholesome. I don’t like the
problem-play. It leaves a disagreeable taste in
the mouth. I love Carton. He is more human.
“Your paper calls me ‘the fat woman,’ but I
don’t care. I cut the notice out and pasted it in
my scrap-book. I have a quiet-looking face, al-
though you wouldn’t think it; and when they
photo, me they have to snap me. If they keep
me too long with ‘ Keep smiling like that' That’s
nice ! ’ my photo, comes out with my teeth
smiling, but my eyes looking serious. So I very
seldom have a photo, taken. I don’t mind the
dressmaker, but I hate the photographer.
I love character business ; and I’m very fond
of my work—if I’m out of it I’m miserable. But
I never go to witness a performance ; never go to
a matinee. And I’ve never spoken a line of
onakespeare
in all my 30
years of stage
life. In Eng-
land we are
always en-
gaged in the
same Hue of
business, al-
though there
may b* other
parts that you
are dying to
play. Thus,
for the past lf>
years, I have
always played
old women,
with not more
than three
breaks. An-1,
after all, it
doesn’t mat-
ter to me. It
brings the
money in, and
I’ve got on
s plendidly.
Miss Prim, of
‘Nita’s First,’
was the be-
ginning of my
playing old
women. My
favorite part
is Lady Janet
M‘Clin took in
‘ The White
Heather.’ I
just love it.
It’s a magnificent part; the sort of part you only
fet once in your life. My part here in ‘ Lady
[untworth’s Experiment ’ is the same as I had
in the London production. I love breezv, bright
comedy.
“Do I smoke ? Ido ! I sit on the balcony and
smoke in the evening. It soothes me. If I were
a man I’d love my pipe. If I have a worry on the
mind I take to the cigarette.
“Diet ? Eat anything you want or fancy. I’m
so healthy I can eat anything, and I do. If nature
craves for sweets, I say take sweets. I eat any-
thing and everytliing.
“I never study my parts. I learn my words.
I never think how I shall pose or act. But I’m
not Miss Vaughan on the stage. I lose myself in
the character. That is the only way to act pro
perly. The moment you become self-conscious on
the stage, you’re done. If you begin to think of
your hands—forget you have them. Do the same
by your feet. Otherwise you’ll feel you have fifty.
It’s very difficult to be natural on (' e stage. The
art of concealing art is the great dnriculty of our
profession- I suppose one of the hardest parts is
that of Juliet, where the woman must look young
and yet mjust be thoroughly experienced.
“I believe in exercise. I don’t go in for it
myself, biit I know it’s very good. I believe in
dumb-bells for people who haven’t weak hearts,
f used to vow ; I love it. I love the water; but I
had to. give rowing up because I was getting so
broad in the back. I get on the roof here some-
Jjmes and look out upon the harbour. I do so
longfo get on the water and have a row—l want
to stretch my muscles. By the way, your paper
called me a fat woman. I’d rather be a fat woman
kj ,all ,a scrag. But I’m going to take it off.
W allung ? As a girl I never used to walk much,
although I was a dancer. My feet are fearfully
tender if I walk, although I’ve been treading on
them a good while now.
“ London audiences are very sympathetic and
never laugh at a love-scene. I cannot understand
why Australian audiences laugh at the pathetic in
a love-scene. They certainly don’t know how
heartbreaking it is to artists. It makes me feel
angry enough to throw myself at them, and I
should hurt them if I did.
“Charles Wyndham is the best actor on the
English stage. We have no best actress. We
haven’t one really good actress on the English
there’ll be no actors in England soon,
ihe days of the stock season, which gave actors a
round of characters to play, are over, and an actor
who wants the perfection that comes from real
hard work and varying experience would obtain a
better training with Robert Brough and his com-
pany than he could get in any other school I know
of. But the continual change of programme is
very hard on Mr. and Mrs. Brough, who play the
leading parts—parts that are very long. Brough
mounts the plays here equal to London pro-
duction.
“ I’ve had no holiday from rehearsal yet—when
T do get a week, I’m going to do everything.
During my stay, so far, in Sydney, I’ve never
seen anything but King-st. and William-st. And
now our season here is coming to a close, and,
womanlike, I leave everything to the last minute.
“I never knew what nervousness was until the
last 10 years ; and, even now, I don’t get really
nervous. When I’m waiting at the wings, I get a
little shaky—a little flutter —tjien I hear my cue,
and it’s all over. I didn’t begin my stage-life with
acting ; I began it with dancing. And that is why
I believe I don’t get nervous. With some act-
resses, the nervousness clings to them all their
lives. It fairly gets hold of them, and worries
them in their dressing-rooms, and when they come
out, and stand waiting to go on, they become
actually sick with nervousness—l’ve often seen
my sister, Kate, actually sick on one side of the
stage, and Kellie Barren actually siok on the
other, as they waited for their oues. Nellie
Farren was nearly always ill like that imme-
diately before going on.
“Bernhardt draws in London, but, as a rule,
foreign artists, speaking a foreign language, do
not draw for any length of time. People go to see
them a little while because it is fashionable. But
you will see five men sleeping in one row of the
dress-circle at a foreign entertainment, and when
the curtain falls you will see their five wives nudge
them hard in the ribs, and then the five men wake
up, and applaud. The foreign artist gets more
sincere sympathy from the gods, and that is why
he sings to them —OUT! goes the note, and—
UP ! goes the hand.
“I knew Miss Ida Yeoland, poor girl. It
knocked me over when Mrs. Brough told me
about the two sisters’suicide. Ida was a sweetly
pretty girl and cheery ! —she used to call me Aunt
Susie. She used to say to me, * I don’t know what
I’ll do when you’re gone. Auntie —you buck me up
so.’ She was a very promising girl, too. She
played the parts very well that I saw her in.
“ The profession in London is so overcrowded.
I don’t know how half or quarter of the girls live.
The London managers are to blame, I think, for
the overcrowding. A girl comes along, who is
pretty and filled with anfbition for the stage, and
they tell her, ‘ Yes, we can let you walk on to
begin with, and then you must wait your oppor-
tunity.’ And that’s as far as the girls get. Girls
are more open to temptation on the stage than in
ordinary life; though, of course, that sort of thing
is purely a matter of temperament. If a girl is
inclined that way she will go wrong in a
MISS SUSIE VAUGHAN, acting with MR. LIONEL BROUGH (“Bob"
Brought uncle) in " The Club Baby” at the London Avenue Theatre .
Photo, by Sana, Strand, London.
church—or a coffee-palace. At the same time,
people who condemn girls who go wrong on the
stage, are very seldom in the position to under-
stand how great the girls’ temptations are.
There is much truth in the lines
Fate or force of circumstance
Moulds us all to saints or sinners.
But actors and actresses now occupy a social posi-
tion that was never allowed them previously.
They were called rogues and vagabonds in the old
days, and I suppose they were not far from it.
But now they are sought after and run after by
the best and strictest society. And the extent to
which they are courted is illustrated by the case
of Mary Moore, of Wyndham’s Theatre. A
society lady fell so much in love with her, that
when the lady died she bequeathed sufficient pro-
perty to Miss Moore to make her independent of
her profession, if she chose, all her life.
“Yes, I’m married; I have one son and one
daughter—a pigeon pair.”
S. A. MILLS.
Allerdale Grainger, the newly-appointed
State Agent in London for S.A., moved by some
muddle-headed comment on the Islands Labor
Bill, published by the Daily Graphic, wrote a
short and pointed letter describing Kanaka
labor conditions as “little better than slavery.’
This evoked a long-winded remonstrance from
dreary Q. Agent-General Tozer, and the contro-
versy is the first open discussion in the camp of
the Australian representatives in London.
Curious advt. in a recent S.M. Herald :
~\XT ANTED, EMPLOYMENT as Caretaker, place of
' r trust, by one for 40 years in N.S.W., used carp,
tools for living'; father was first surveyor employed
by Sir T. Mitchell ; mother, with her parents came to
Sydney, 1813, by Rev. S. Marsden, for mission work
in Maoriland ; her father was sent as forerunner by
Gov. Macquarie, March, 1814, with the Mission Brig
Active to Kororareka Bay to clear the way for three
families, who landed from the Active with Mr.
Marsden on the following Christmas Day.

Bulletin a “first-family,” it would get advertiser

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sing has instructed Lieut.-Gen. Sir lan Hamil-
i? n "°l I < ? onve J r 8 d.esire to Lord Ketchener that the War
should be brought to a close before June.”— Cable.
Rex tremendae majestatia
Sends abroad his message thus :
“Ketchener, that stubborn State is
Cause of much concern to Us.
“ Gloomy fears invade our presence,
Shapes obscene with gestures rude j
Spawn of that vile recrudescence,
Whioh ’fore God must be subdued.
“Spread, therefore, thy desolation ;
Clinch thy hold, and clinch it soon.
tribulation—
Get it over before June.”
Rex tremendae majestatis
Willeth that, the world around,
All mankind should revel gratis
While his royal*brow is crowned.
And that Boer note, ghastly, mordant,
Whispering the world about,
Now, as evermore discordant,
Cannot fail to put him out.
Boers are evil-counselled, surely,
If they fail to hear and heed.
Even now they live but poorly j
Women wail and warriors bleed.
Death o’er veldt and kopje flitting,
Feasts to fulness day by day.
Death within the death-camps sitting
Surfeits of his awful prey.
Yet audacious, contumacious,
Fights<the stubborn burgher still,
Mocking at the prospect gracious
Following suzerain’s good-will.
Fortune lost, andfrlands estreated,
Home and household doomed or wrecked,
Will not own himself defeated,
Nor forego his self-respect.
May we hope this latest message
From a monarch on the raek
Thrilling with most mournful presage
Will avail to win him back ?
Though a continent hath travailed
In a strife by gold-bugs bred^
Will the knot remain unravelled
When the King’s own word is said ?
1 hough the Boer may dare damnation,
Dance with death to horror’s tune,
Will he mar the Coronation
By resisting up to June ?
Burglars and Bottles.
Thb man who has once driven a burglar out of his
house with a pistol is likely to keep the weapon
handy by for use in the future.
On a similar principle Mrs. Elizabeth Langmaid
is never without a bottle of Mother Seigel’s Syrup
where she can lay hands on it any day.
About four years ago she was taken bad with
what was called “ a complication of complaints.’’
The doctors said she had an abscess on one of her
lungs, and also indigestion and heart troubles.
And, seeing how she looked and felt, we should
have believed him without a moment’s hesitation.
“You can get an idea,” she says, “how bad I
was when I tell you I lay helpless in bed nearly
nine months.
(That does give us the idea and no mistake.
Save for the hope of recovery—which seldom
3uite perishes in the mind—l would as lief be
ead, and so have the trouble over and done with).
“Finally,” Mrs. Langmaid goes on to say,
“when I got out of bed, all of me that could
waste away was gone. I was just a skeleton
covered by a skin. In truth thoy wrapped me in
wadding—for appearance and for such comfort
and warmth as the protection might give me.
“ Whatever my complaint was I always had a
dreadful pain in my sides and under the shoulder-
blades; but the medicines I took had no more
effect on it than so much sweetened water would
have had.
“ While in this miserable condition I remem-
bered how different friends of mine had spoken of
the virtues of Seigel’s Sprup for many kinds of
ailments that nothing else seemed able to help.
“Anyway I was sure it would be no mistake to
try it,, and. so I got a bottle from Campbell & Co.’s
store in this town. Up to that time I always had
a great feeling of weariness and drowsiness after
eating, and could not shake it off.
“ But, to my delight, I soon discovered that a
dose of the Syrup dispelled this almost imme-
diately, and by the time I had finished the first
bottle I was greatly improved.
“ As you would suppose, I persevered in taking
the remedy until, by degrees, I got strong again.
Gradually, too, I picked up my lost flesh, and
recovered my former good health.
“Ever since then I keep a bottle of Seigel’s
Syrup in the house, and take a dose whenever I
feel out of sorts in any way.
“You may publish this if you like, and I shall
always be glad to hear of Seigel’s Syrup doing for
others what it did forme.”—Elizabeth Langmaid,
Market-street, Muswellbrook, N.S.W., Sept.
26th, 1899.
