POLITICAL
POINTS
“Home Rule for N.S.W. !” That is to
say, Local Government.
The Govt, proposal that well-to-do rela-
tives of N.S.W. old-age pensioners be compelled
to reimburse the State suggests that the old-age
pension is a charity and not a right; and so
destroys the cardinal principle of the scheme.
Every man who votes for the Bill in N.S.W.
Assembly is an enemy to old-age pension.
Westralia’s new and temporary Premier,
Morgans, has put up something like a record. He
formed a Ministry of six, including himself, and
three of them immediately lost their seats when
they went to their constituents for re-election.
It is but a few months since these same men
werer returned at the general election, so it was
probably being found in Morgans’ company that
killed them.
N.S.W. Assembly, last week, had a sitting
of 30 hours at a stretch. Fourteen hours were de-
voted. to additional estimates (revenue account) of
a few thousand pounds. Then nearly £3,000,000
of loan estimates went through in less than half
the time, and the House turned again to additions
to the additional estimates (revenue account). It
looks as if Parliament didn’t care a rap how loan
monfiy is spent.
W.A. Worker reprints a year-old speech
of the new Westralian Premier Morgans (delivered
in London):
The position I took up upon it was this, that I acted as
the mouthpiece of the Chamber of Mines in the House.
When I tell you, Mr. Chairman, that I brought forward
on that Bill 27 amendments, only three of which I was
able to carry, I think it will be quite easy for gentlemen
in London to realise that at least I did my best to carry
out the wishes of the Chamber of Mines, and also the
wishes of the London capitalists with regard to this Bill.
The only private member in the House who has ever stood
up and defended the rights of the London capitalists is
myself!
THE SPIRIT OF THE AGE.
REV. MR. STUFFENKIDD (who rather fancies himself
in making Scripture lessons entertaining to the
children): “Now, boys, what would you have
done if you had been in Balaam’s place when
the ass spake? ”
SMALL BOR (on front seat): “ Sold him, to the circus!”
(Here you might have heard an elephant drop.)
The indignant consternation with which
Sydney D.T. greets the discovery that the Federal
Labor party knows a thing or two in practical
politics is very amusing. That paper confidently
relied upon the Labor Senators to create trouble
in the Senate over the Aliens Immigration
Restriction Bill, but they wouldn’t. With an eye
on the safety-of the Kanaka Bill, they determined
to take what they could get instead of demanding
the impossible. What angers the D.T. particu-
larly is the artful way in which the Labor Sena-
tors “saved their face” by throwing the responsi-
bility of negativing the absolute exclusion clausa
on the Freetraders. What is “ splendid tactics ”
in Reid or M'Millan is, from a D.T. point of
view, “disreputable manoeuvring” in a Labor
member
They had a financial debate in N.S.W.
Assembly last week on a Bill brought down by
the Treasurer to repudiate a deficit-repayment
agreement, on the ground that N.S.W., despite
the increased tariff revenue, couldn’t spare the
money. Extracts from various financial experts
who spoke
The Howling Dervishes of the Opposition. . . It would
be a hard job to batter your brains out. . . You are
nastiness itself. . . Bananas ! . . I wish, Mr. Speaker,
you would keep this king of the cannibal islands in order.
. . . You’re the wild man from Borneo ! . . . Order. . .
laughter . . . yells . . . order ... I withdraw . . .
order. . . It is absolutely false . . . order . . . loud
laughter.
After these and similar cogent arguments the bill
passed its second reading by 65 to 31.
N.S. Wales M’s.P., whatever they may
say, are solidly opposed to any reduction in their
numbers, and Premier See (who hasn’t yet got his
title) bows his head. The question is to be sub-
mitted in some form or other at the general elec-
tion a couple of years ahead, but as all sides were
agreed at the last contest on the desirableness of
instant reduction, the Government’s course is a
most cheeky one.
Alleged by one politician that, owing to
the inability of the O’Sullivan to say “No” to
any request for a railway, there exists a private
compact between N.S.W. Govt, and its Upper
House supporters to reject any Bill that doesn’t
really meet with the Ministerial approval. The
M’sL.C. can view the storm calmly from a safe
spot, whilst O’Sullivan and co. pose as martyrs
of Upper House obstruction.
Printed again by a London paper that—
The sensation of the next British general election will
be the adoption as part of the Govt, programme of a
scheme of modified Protection—the long-talked-of
Imperial Customs Union, which will be formulated by
Mr. Joseph Chamberlain.
Seriously, can Prime Minister Barton be well
spared next year to attend that Coronation, and a
little private conference with screwmaker Judas?
“ I wish my own dogs would not bite me ! ”
was the retort of Labor-member Carpenter, in
S.A. Assembly, when a colleague interjected
during his speech. The Party is very angry
now at being spoken of as “ Carpenter’s dogs, ”
and he has had to suffer much criticism in con-
sequence. He is the tub-thumper of whom
Premier Jenkins remarked: “The hon. member
is too suspicious to be politically honest, and too
pious to be good. ”
The foreign importers’ party has two
deafening dog-howls in its mouth just now. One
is that the Kingston tariff is “prohibitory ” —will
stop all imports—and will therefore produce no
revenue; consequently, it should be lowered, as
lower duties, by not checking imports, will yield
more revenue. The other is that Kingston’s tariff
promises to yield £11,000,000 or £12,000,000 a
year, which .is an absurd sum to take from the
people; consequently it should be reduced so as
to yield less revenue.

Wales stands to-day with a clothing industry on
the soundest possible footing without the assist-
ance of any Protection whatever.” And this is
Coghlan’s “ Statistical Register ” on the woollen
and tweed mills of N.S.W. by way of illustration :

Treasurer Waddell’s new Audit legislation
is expected to terminate Auditor-General Rennie’s
connection with N.S.W. official life.
Mr. Rennie’s innings was long.
He came of a scholastic
stock, and took
naturally to a se-
dentary and re-
tired life. He
was clerk in the
Audit Office be-
fore Constitu-
tional Govt, was
granted, and has
been working
about there for
nearly 60 years.
He had some
stiff battles to
fight with Trea-
surers who took
irregular views of
financial obliga-
tions, and a few
years ago was
almost goaded out
of office. Were it
not for the terms of
the Audit Act, he
would have been com-
pelled to resign. Mr.
Rennie has always main-
tained his independence of
Treasurers, even when they
were Premiers. He is now well past
80, but his head is still clear, and he is ready
as ever to tell a Treasurer to keep his place.
Ketchener’s reply to criticisms of his con-
centration camps is a lovely bit of brutal cynicism.
Cabled—
The British columns now leave the Boer women and
children on their ruined farms, disregarding all appeals
to take them to the camps.
It was complained that, at the camps, an enormous
percentage of women and children died— i.e., were
killed by the system—so, in future, the helpless
are (after being deprived of the shelter of their
homes) to be left in a desolated land, where they
must almost certainly perish.
Brisbane Courier describes the “ White
Australia ” idea as a crime against Nature, which
will surely bring about its own punishment. Yes,
Serhaps ! But Nature didn’t put the Chinaman,
ap, Hindoo or,Kanaka here, and what right has
the Courier tojrnow more than Nature as to the
right place for these people ? And it didn’t put
the Courier here dither, or the white man, or the
British authority; and it didn’t- send the Briton
to rule India or Burmah or Egypt or South Africa.
Isn’t it a trifle late to begin growing anxious about
the intentions of Nature ?
The -most soulful plaint about the Tariff
which The Bulletin has yet met is the wail of a
Melbourne bookseller of intensely moral ten-
dencies that the unregenerate Ministry proposes
to exact 25 per cent, on imported scriptural texts,
Sunday-school cards, illuminated emblems an l
other such “ elevating ” goods. He reckons that
unless every home can get its imported motto :
“A soft answer tumeth away wrath,” “In life
we are in death,” at duty-free rates, the Common-
wealth will start on the high road to the imported
Pit where the imported Worm dieth not.
Premier Jenkins, of S.A., made no seri-
ous attempt to establish a State-owned electric-
tramway system in Adelaide. Nor did he make
any attempt to consolidate the eight or ten wild-
cat municipalities into a Greater Adelaide with
one municipal council, so that there would have
been a fair chance of establishing a municipal
system. But by setting apart a night for the
Snow Bill, and keeping a quorum together, he
was mainly responsible for putting the private
monopoly scheme through, and for tin's S.A." will
remember its Jenkins in days to come.
The two voices of Sydney D.T. on the
tariff:—
. Dec. 3 : “ The actual experience of the tariff for the
months of October and November indicates that it would,
if passed in its present form, yield a million and a half
more than the Govt, estimated for the current year, and
between two and three millions more than the estimate
for what Sir George Turner calls a normal year. These
are miscalculations worthy of the financial capacity of
this Ministry of lawyers.”
Dec. 14 (after changes which the D.T. generally de-
scribed as likely to bring in more revenue): “ There is
no possibility of the tariff yielding what had been ex-
pected from it.”
Mr. Arthur Griffith, M.P., hon. secretary
of N.S.W. Labor Party, writes :
I want to contradict your statement (14/12/’01) that the
N.S.W. Labor Party “ was disposed to agree to the Leg.
Council’s amendment in clause 36 of the Arbitration Bill
till Sydney Labor Council demanded them to make a
stand.” From the beginning that party was unanimous
in its determination to destroy the Bill rather than adopt
the amendment, and told the Govt. so.
And yet the statements to the contrary were so
numerous and so detailed. “ The party was
unanimous from the first.” Then what was all
the caucussing about ?
Griffith, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), accuses the See
Ministry of hiring four expensive barristers, at
£5OO a week, to represent it m the Rocks’ Resump-
tion cases. But this is, possibly, unavoidable ;
the way the barrister does things, it is necessary
to hire four of him to make reasonably sure of one
being there. A much more important matter is
this: When the awards are made, they will carry
interest from the date of resumption. Are the
rents which the Government has collected to be
used to pay that interest, or does the See Minis-
try propose to pay it out of loan moneys and use
the back rents as ordinary revenue ? If it does,
it deserves to be kicked out of office, and out of
the House, and into permanent oblivion.

Gr. H. Reid, in one of his moments of •
Nationalism :
While we talk of the necessity of founding these systems
of defence, let us never forget that war is a horror ; let us
never forget that, with all the glitter and glory of heroism
on the field, all these are black, dark chapters in the pro-
gress of humanity. Let our Australian spirit expand ■
itself, not in dreams of military adventure in other lands,
but in qualifying itself to put round continent,
a rampart of triple steel; that is a legitimate ambition
for a man or a country. Do not let the statesmen of
England think, because we are ready to send our men to
Africa, that Australians are infested with any lust of -
military enterprise. We are not prepared to enter ‘hose •
lists.

certain, if he were given a copy of the monstroui

Sydney Daily Tklkgraph prof esses amaze-
ment at the idea of the Senate agreeing to news-
paper postage without so much as a division. It
points out that as N.S.W., Tasmania, and S.A.
carry papers free, the question as to whether the-
other half of Australia is to impose postage on ,
the Commonwealth is a serious one. Yet the
D.T. sees nothing incongruous in demanding that
the Freetrade half (or less) of N.S.W. should be-
allowed to force its fiscal policy on the five Pro-
tectionist States ! The unanimity with which the
Senate welcomed newspaper postage is a facer to
the Sydney metropolitan press.
After a bout of all-night sittings the
N.S.W. Govt, succeeded in getting its Kstimates-
in-chief through the Assembly with the loss of'
about half-a-dozen items, aggregating some-
thing under £2000. When the Loan Esti-
mates came on, the Government calmly re-sub-
mitted the items which the House had booted out.
a few weeks before, and passed the lot without,
division. Reid, in his palmiest days, never had. 5
the House in such complete subjection as the See-
Criek-O’Sullivan combination. When the pen-
dulum swings back their downfall will probably
be more complete than his —and it must swing •
back some day.
In the division on Treasurer Waddell’s
Bill to drop the new annual vote —which has never •
yet been paid and only exists on paper —to diminish
the N.S.W. deficit from £250,000 to £100,000, and to .
postpone it for a year even at that, only .31 mem-
bers voted No. The great majority of these were
the old Freetrade party, which would just as.
emphatically have supported the motion had Reid
proposed it in his time, and who voted ag-ainst it
simply because the See Govt, party voted for it.
Of the Laborit.es, two only—Griffith and Holman
—opposed the act of repudiation, and of the-
Independent party, who hollered for Reform six
months ago, not one went against the Govt.
Sydney D.T., after sc.oling-on “ the young-
blood ” of N.S.W. Opposition to blither by the
hour in stonewalling the tariff, now savagely
turns on them thus : —
Mr. Sydney Smith, Mr. Conroy, and Mr. J. Cook, of’

the seven months that their “ A few remarks, Mr. Chair-
man,” represented'three and four.daya and nights’ talking-
without intermission. The whole of the ideas contained
in this avalanche of verbiage might be.-compressed into a ,
speech of a quarter of an hour. Mrs. Caudle was hardly-
as incessant or as aggravating.
Hard lines to be bitten by your own mongrel.
Poor Smith, poor Conroy, poor Cook! They
must feel like returned, unpaid cohentingenters.

“ &•" writes : Many readers will re-
member the Mulgrave Central Mill fiasco, near
Cairns (Q.), and the pro-aliens’ emphatio assertion
that this episode proved that the climate was too
hot for white men, consequently the employment
of colored labor was absolutely necessary—and so
read this quote from an account
x® Mossman Central Mill Co., whose estate is
further _ north than the Mulgrave. It will be
found in the November issue of the Mackay
buGAR J ournal, page 89, the official organ of the
Q. sugar-industry. After bemoaning the fact that
white laborers were migratory during the first
crushing at Mossman, the historian—who is the
paid secretary of the co.— remarks •
The migratory nature referred to may have been due
principally to the want of proper accommodation for the
staff and workmen (they lived in tents and in neighboring
v ’j ') until t . he crushi ng' season was well advanced.
Yet under the trying circumstances alluded to the man-
agement did not allow either Kanakas or Japanese to
work in the mill, nor have their services been called into
requisition at any time in the Mossman central mill.
Happily there has been no repetition of similar trouble,
the regular conduct and general efficiency of the mm
comparing very favorably with other centres of the
industry.
This proves what the “White Australia” party
has said all along—that white laborers’ reliability
is in proportion to the treatment they receive.
Doctors Differed Again,
... BUT ...
VITADATIO
CTXZR, IE ZD-
Read what Mr. Davis writes.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Perth, June 6,1900.
Dear Sir,—l have been invalided for about 2 years and
3 months. The doctors treated me for Inflammation of
the Lungs, Bronchitis, and Pneumonia, but failed to cure
me. I used to suffer with very severe pains through my
back and chest, and had a very nasty cough. I was in
the Perth Hospital three weeks, but the doctor told me
he could do no more for me, and that I was as well as he
could make me. I was laid aside for 12 weeks at
Fremantle. The landlady of the Club Hotel advised me
to try VITADATIO, which I have done, and after taking
four bottles lam now in splendid health. I will be
pleased to answer any inquiries that any sufferer may
require in the hope that my humble testimony may be
the means of leading others to health.
Yours respectfully,
(Signed) FREDERICK DAVIS, Bayswater.
Witness : W. J. Fist, Perth.
For further particulars,
S, A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

“Rough-rider” on “Sydney shingle-
shorts ”:
Sydney is more cursed with these than any other city I
know of. A notable example accosted me, the other day,
as I was enjoying a pipe in one of our parks. He was
about 30 years old—usually they’re a lot more advanced
in years. After a long rigmarole about the inconsisten-
cies of the various churches, he handed me a printed
digest of his views. The document was headed “MI
CREDE.” “Oh!" I said, “you believe, then, in the
phonetic system of spelling ? ” My interlocutor looked
strangely puzzled. “ They are,” he answered, in husky:
tones, “the outway of much thought. Six weeks did
those 17 clauses you see take to draw up.” I nodded due
appreciation—“ That there Nieene affair couldn’t well have
taken more.” Looking at this six weeks’result, I read :
“Clause I. Those who see the Great ‘I Am’ insole’s
abode in pitt till End of all things are wrong.” Mildly, if
not reproachfully, I pointed to the errors in spelling. He
only said : “ I wanted a religion, and I sought it with the
Roman Catholics, what did I find ? Incomprehensibility,
Transubstantiation, and such rot .” Here I pulled
him up, for a policeman, possibly of the R.C.
faith, had his eye on us. and returned to the
“ Crede.” Beyond the funny spelling, I saw
little to reward me till clause XVII. gave me sudden
pause. “ Rats is not cats, neither is raysons figgs, still in
chayos there is safety.” “ Yes,” said the phonetic
apostle ; “ I thought that would a bit stagger you like.
My meanin’isthat them like things require incorporation
like in the general complexity of things. D’ye see what
I mean?” I said I did, and he went on: “I’m gettin’
people to assist financial like to havin’ these pamphlets
distributed. I sent one to Archbishop Smith.” “Did
you get an answer?” “No,” he returned, cheerfully.
Then all matter being determined on as bein’ in a state of
flux, and affairs, generally speakin’, commutin’ theirselves
into absorption of the negative, why then we has universal
domination of the unquenchable.” I rose and departed
in great haste. A few days after I re-encountered my
friend in the position of lift-man to a set of chambers.
Some day, when he is thinking out some additions to his
creed he will let that lift go, and an eminent solicitor or
two will be killed.

He was an Australian country blacksmith,
named, say Smith, and he invented and patented a grub-
ber better than any then in use. Now, in the capital of the
province was a Arm which also manufactured grubbers, and
when the partners got to hear of Smith (through failure
to get orders in his district), they laid their Scotch heads
together; and, when Scotchmen put their heads together,
somebody’s got to suffer by it. Just when Smith had got
his little factory in good swing, the firm invited him to visit
the city to see if they could come to a friendly arrangement.
He went gladly, hoping to have his patent brought out and
to get a good royalty. The firm cordially welcomed him,
and, after liquid refreshment, the elder partner said :
“We have come to the conclusion that we can make grub-
bers at about half the cost that you can, through having
better workmen and better machines. Now, supposing-
we give you £2 for every grubber we make of your pat-
tern, will you give us the right to do so, and agree not to
make any more yourself ? You can’t possibly make more
than that on them as it is, and, moreover, we will pro-
bably sell three times as many as you would sell. We
could easily make 10 to 15 machines a day if there is a
demand for them.” Smith willingly signed an agreement
so apparently favorable to himself, and went home to
wait for the royalties to come along. He is still waiting.
The city firm simply never made a single grubber of his
pattern—but they often drink his health in silence and
Johnywalker. , * *
Dear Bulletin. — Re law and liquorers. I once
killed a man with bad liquor quicker than electrocution
could do it. “My corpse” was the victim of competition.
People will have cheap drink, and as the law allows
poisonous stuff to be sold, merchants must sell, and
cellarmen (I'm one) will fake it up. With such success,
too, that the Govt. Analyst (at the inquest) described my
blend as “a cheap but wholesome liquor.” If spirit-of-
wine, seven months old, reduced, faked (mystery) and
sold as “ Fine Old Cognac” was “wholesome,” then the
verdict was correct. I have known a publican to put
into 10 gallons of the stuff that caused the funeral a
double handful of tobacco. The boss seized the stock ;it
was returned to the store ; and in checking the quanti-
ties I made the discovery. From spirit-of-wine I have
manufactured “ Fine Old Cognac,” “ Jamaica Rum,” and
“ Treble Refined Geneva Hollands.” And the firm sold
thousands of gals, of each. If you want half-measures
for this condition of things, I will supply them. But,
with much profanity, I say—away with half-measures. I
have myself used good liquor all my life ; and, speaking
from an altitude of 6ft. lin., and a solidity of 13st., I
appeal to the members of the Federal Govt, to take an
interest in the breed of men. I ask them to put their
feet in cleats and keep pulling until the Fed. Govt, con-
trols the Destiny of Liquor. A white-faced Australia,
supplied with good liquor, need fear no foe.— Lush
Artist.
• * , * »
Morrissey, Vic. Minister of Agriculture, seems
disposed to talk sense about the local tobacco-growing
industry, which is bemoaning the loss of its 3d. per lb.
export 'bonus. He wants to know why Govt, should
encourage the export of Yic. tobacco when its market
price at the other end of the world is much less than the
Melb. manufacturers pay for imported leaf. At present,
122 hogsheads of stripped leaf are “awaiting sale” in
London, a previous purchaser having refused to take any
more of it at 4Jd. per lb. And a bonus of 3d. per lb. was
paid to the shippers of this absurdly inferior article !

Charming bit of German-English from a circular
recently received by a Sydney resident from a Berlin
entomological dealer“ Moreover you will kindly
observe that I am also ready to buy against cash Butter-
flies and great Moths of your country, provided their
price and quality be convenient, and, if you have the
intention to sell Butterflies and great Moths against cash
payment, I request you to tell me the price of your
Butterflies and Moths per 1000 pieces—viz., in Reichs-
mark, Francs, Dollars, or Sterling. When the prices
offered by you are favorable I have no doubt that we
shall arrive to the most animated and agreeable connec-
tions.”
* • * *
Of all Maoriland industries, bacon-curing will
be hardest hit by the Federal tariff. Within the last two
years hundreds of thousands sterling have been sunk in
expensive plants for hog-curing. The Australian market
was looked on as the big one, S’Africa having proved a
bad frost, and Seddon’s new pets, the Islanders, not
using much pickled pig. The tariff of 3d. a lb. will, of
course, kill the Commonwealth trade. Bacon, unfor-
tunately, is a product the price of which can’t be raised :
as soon as it moves up, people begin to replace it with
fresh meat. The duty should give an enormous fillip to
hog-raising in Australia. What a business could be done
by big capitalists understanding the game, who would
take up northern alluvial lands, cultivate maize, sweet
potatoes, pumpkins, etc., on a big scale,, and feed the
produce to pigs bred wholesale.

“Mamoosa”: Recently bought a pub. in a

The Sydney publicans have approached the
brewers and wine-and-spirit merchants with a proposal
that grocers, clubs, &c., should be supplied by the
hotel-keepers, and not by the brewers or merchants.
Should the licensed victuallers succeed bogus clubs will
be killed.

A civil servant whom sickness and other mis-
fortunes had knocked from pillar to post of late years,
paid off a Sydney money-lender t’other day. Shylock
lent originally—about 60 months ago—a nominal £lOO
(the cash was really £80). The borrower has paid back
£5OO, with several guineas and two-guineas as fines for
unpunctuality and other crimes.
• « * •
In view of the expected glut in the N.S.W.
fruit-market, it has been suggested that fruit-growers
should resort to “pulping,” and thus convert into a
vendible article produce which would otherwise mean a
dead loss. “No other producing industry,” it is pointed
out, “ insists on selling the produce raw or not at all, and
no one but easy-going Eastralians would go on doing it
year after year.”
* * * *
Re the assertion that N.S.W. jam-manufac-
turers largely use pumpkin and turnip in their wares, an
analysis of samples from different factories has shown
that such is not the case, though it was found that in
some instances apple had been used as a bulk-producer.
Instructions have now been issued that where there is an
admixture of apple the jam shall be so labelled.
* # * *
Na'ive statement in Sydney D.T. on behalf of
N.S.W. strawberry-growers : “ We shall never be able to
export them raw, except perhaps to Queensland after the
northern crop is done, but any surplus could be absorbed
for jam, being better material for strawberry jam than
any kind of tomatoes or pumpkins.”
* * * *
From a trade journal, which speaks with
authority:—“ Unfortunately most of our factories in
N.S.W. are undermanned and worked by beggarly-paid
hands, in consequence of which the work is rushed
through, resulting in mottled or improperly worked
butter, untidy boxes, short weight, &c., all of which
defects tend to depreciate our butter on the London
market.” It looks as if even the great natural industry
of butter-making hadn’t been exactly flourishing under
Freetrade in N.S.W.

Editor Bulletin.—The answer to “Fridel’s”
query (B. 12/10/’01) re pawnbrokers and grocers seems to
me simple enough. The pawnbroker doesn’t lend you
84s. and charge you 645. interest per annum. He lends
you 84s. on an article pledged as security, and generally
worth 3, 4 or 5 times 845., at (say) 5. 6d. a month or Is. 3d.
a week ; and, on failure to pay at most two or three weeks’
or one month’s interest, forfeits the security. He gener-
ally deducts the first instalment of the interest. By this
means he multiplies his apparent 80 per cent, by any-
thing from 12 to 500 according to circumstances. He
cannot make bad debts if he knows his business, whereas
the rn-ocer even if a smart business man, often does,
because he’has to give credit for a longer or short« time
which the pawnbroker never do e s.-FAR West.
pawnbrokers-there are some-don t charge the rates nor
carry on the tricks which Far West mentions.-Ed. a. j

There’s going to be a big condensed-milk indus-
try in Australia shortly, and it behoves the authorities
to see from the start that the factories turn out a whole-
some article, with a 1 the cream in, and no salicylic aci(l,
or saccharine. Much of the condensed milk imported to
Australia is only fit to be fed to Boer babies in concen-
tration camps “ from motives of humanity. There is no
reason why Australia should not meet its own demand,
and also make a colossal export of condensed milk.

issue a Union label to factories paying fair rates. This
would give sympathisers a chance to discriminate against
the sweater.
* • • •
Some few weeks ago, down South, four young
fellows found themselves stone-broke, so, having procured
a couple of tin-lined cases, a few yards of ditto piping,
etc., they hied them to the hack of a bush pub., and
there, in a gully, dug a hole and fixed up their fake then
went to town and informed the authorities that they
believed they had discovered a “ still.” The police were
sent out and watched the place for a week, finally carting
the gear into the Custom House bonded store, where it
lies to-day, while the ingenious discoverers are still living
on the £5O they got for finding and informing.
* * * *
The English Court of Appeal recently framed a
rule that every deponent who, in an affidavit, states
“ his information and belief ” on a point, must give the
source thereof; otherwise the solicitor responsible for the
affidavit must bear the cost of it without any right of
recourse against his client. This regulation is aimed at
the increasing practice of swelling up affidavits with
gossip for which no authority' is given.
• * * *
The Sydney Tailoresses’ Union soon got to
work in demanding an eight-hour day', but only about
half the rate of wages paid the male clothing-makers ' So
long as women can be got to work at half male rates they
will be used to displace men; the women’s political
rights advocates should bracket with their political
demands the concession of equal industrial rights and
wages. Why a woman should not be paid by results for
piecework—as well as a man is paid is hard to under-
stand.
* • * •
“X. 0.”: Some Sydney trade-unions are not
too good at putting their own principles into practice.
In many cases they expect an hon. sec. to do all the work
of the Union practically “ on the nod.” Writer knows
one Union with over 1000 members which won’t pay a
secretary ; and another, with an income of about £2OOO a
year, pays its secretary £l5O a year. What a contrast to
the Bush Unions, which pay their officials £4 and £5 per
week!
* * * *
“ Cove ” : Two years have passed since I washed
my hands of the ownership of a furnished house, to let.
The thing is pleasurable to begin with : What a fool you
are to be petting’ 5 or 6 per cent, for your money when
you can make 15, or more, this way. But soon the
skeleton creeps out of the cupboard. The Fates gave me
all the nice people first, and then came the awful run of
the others. Occupants began to hook it, without paying,
and leaving the key in the front door. There are agents
who live mostly by trucking non-payees from one house
to another. Another class of shifty gents are those who
are constantly having fresh nieces. But this leads me
too much towards the shuddery quagmires into which
the furnished house drifts. Soon I got afraid there would
be a baby buried in the yard, and decided against being
landlord of a furnished house in future.
* * * *
A well-known Sydney jewellery time-payjnent
man, when engaging a new canvasser, always gets his (the
canvasser’s) photo, taken, for police information in case of
default.
* * * •
Complainant in a recent Sydney case was
entrusted by his employer with a fiver to change at a
butcher’s shop close by. ' He met a casual acquaintance,
who had a weird yarn of wanting to pay the same butcher
£3. He couldn’t pay the money in person, as his creditor
had threatened to' punch his head when he got a
chance ; he didn’t want to give him a chance, but
he did want to pay what he owed. So the victim
took the £3, in a purse (it was explained that he might
drop the sovs. without a purse), and for fear that he
might mix up the £3 inside and the £5 he was to change,
his friend offered to mind the bank note. Of course,
there was no £3 or no debt. Neither was there any
friend outside nor any £5. The ingenious prisoner got
six months’ hard. The ingenuous prosecutor—well, the
law at present gives that sort of problem up.

GROSVENOR STREF'
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The first of the test-matches between Maclaren’s
team and All Australia commenced at Sydney Cricket
Ground last Friday with a good wicket and weather to
match. Maclaren won the toss and opened the batting
with Hayward, while Jones and Noble conducted the
attack. The Mac. only scored one off the first over, but
slogged Noble for a four and a two in the second, and
settled down to assault and batter the bowling in robust
style. Jones gave place to Howell with the leather, and
Noble seemed to have discovered a kind of ball the
English captain with the Scotch name didn’t exactly like.
The scoring eased off somewhat till the Mac. gave a
fairly easy catch into slips, which Howell failed to con-
firm. By tiffin-time the Englishmen had put up 85 for no
wickets. After lunch M’Leod and Jones resumed theattack
and were slogged round for 139, when Noble gave place
to Laver, and, later, Jones was succeeded by Trumble.
At 154 Hayward slogged Trumble high on the off, and
Hill caught him amid thunders of applause. Then the
luck seemed to change. Tyldesley came out, but he had
only scored one when he was caught by Hill off Trumble,
and was succeeded by Quaife. Maclaren went out leg-
before-wicket, after 3j hours of first-class cricket. Jessop
came on, and the field spread-out apprehensively.
Jessop commenced cautiously enough, but soon broke
loose, and in 15 minutes had hit up five 4’s, sneaked four
singles and been clean bowled by M’Leod. Jones and
Quaife were disposed of for 9 and 21 respectively,
and the stumps were drawn with six English wickets
down for 272. On Saturday morning there seemed to
be a general opinion that the Englishmen were as good as
beaten already, and the five men still alive were spoken
of disrespectfully as “ the tail.” However, when the tail
got to work it proved to be long and hard. The
Australians fielded wretchedly. They clawed at the ball
with both hands and their teeth. They tried to
fall down on top of it, but it got away to the boundary
again and again. The one brilliant episode was when
Trumble, in the slips, sprang up into the air and caught
Blythe (10th wicket), with one hand, off Laver’s ball.
The batting of the Englishmen was artistic: they took
no risks, they gave no chances, but batted on serenely,
and waited for the opportunities to come along. When
they did come, the Britishers carefully placed the ball
where there was no Australian to stop it. When Blythe
was caught, the English total stood at 464.
When the Australians went to the stumps the
awfulness of their fielding became apparent by contrast.
Our men slogged the leather more than the Englishmen;
but it came up against fielding that was like a brick wall.
Gregory and Trumper opened the Australian innings, and
stole runs in a most brazen manner, but, with such deadly
throwing in, it was too dangerous, and they grew more
reserved. In the first five minutes Trumper was caught,
and the score stood one wicket down for three. The Aus-
tralian batsmen settled down, and made heroic efforts to
overtake the long start of the Englishmen, but their efforts
were neutralised by that terrible fielding and lightning-like
throwing in. Twice Hill was all but run out, and the great
Gregory was caught while scoring well in the face of big
difficulties. He and Hill had put up 86 between them.
When Noble joined hfm, Hill played somewhat more
cautiously, and it was necessary. At a few minutes to
six, when the score was only three off the century, Noble
stepped out to smite Braund clean out of the ground. It
was an innocent-looking ball till it struck, when it shot
in. Noble swiped—missed, and before he could step back
to his crease was stumped, for two. Howell came out,
and in the few remaining minutes the Australians suc-
ceeded in carrying the figure to 103. It was not till
Monday that the full tide of disaster fell on them.
Then it came like a tidal wave. The batsmen
were obviously nervous. Blythe and Barnes were bowl-
ing, and Blythe’s infinite variety seemed to get on the
Austral nerves. Hill suddenly cut Barnes to the bound-
ary, but the next ball he chopped right into his wicket,
and the scoring-board showed 4 down, for 112. Then
Howell was caught in the slips by Braund off Blythe.
M‘Leod struggled ineffectually with a ball from Barnes
which took his wicket, and Kelly was bowled by Blythe—
all for 112. Four wickets had fallen without a run being
added to the score. Darling pulled himself together and
made an effort to stem the tide of falling wickets and
shift the curse of 112, but Laver was in difficulties with
the bowling, and Darling could do very little with Blythe’s
curios, backed up with the wall-like fielding, and the
Australians struggled along hopelessly in the face of an
impending donkey-licking. An hour’s play only yielded
34 runs. Australia all out for 168.
Australia (296 behind) followed on, with Darling
and Trumper at the wickets. Darling put all his timber
behind a leg ball which was skimming towards the boun-
dary ■ when Jessop, with a tremendous spring upward,
caught it with one hand. Hill stepped out to drive
Braund, and was bowled for a duck. The English
fielding seemed to be even better than before.
Trumper was caught out at 52 by Lilley at the
wicket off Blythe. Noble went exactly the same way.
M‘Leod was bowled for oby Blythe. Mac. had scored a
brace of fine ducks in the match, one from Blythe and one
from Barnes. Then the sun got in the Englishmen’s eyes,
and they began to miss things. Kelly should have been
out, but was missed by Hayward. Gregory was missed-
in the slips by Braund, but Barnes caught Kelly at point
off Blythe. Gregory and Trumble looked like making a
stand) Trumble slogging Blythe for three fours, two cuts
and a drive. He came to grief playing forward to Barnes,
and was caught by Lilley. Laver joined Gregory and was
stumped for nothing. Howell came out and made some
fours, and Gregory blundered a ball into Maclaren’s hands
at mid-on. Jones joined Howell and the only fragment
of a stand was made. Howell played the game, making
31 runs in 13 minutes, including six fours. Then Jones
skied one, the other Jones caught it on the boundary,
and the Australian second innings closed for 172. The
great match was over, and Maclaren’s allegedly second-
rate team had left Australia away back in the dust behind
the chariot to-the extent of an innings and 124 runs.
* » * «
“J.”: For front the average N.S.W. cricket
barracker wins the average. “ Dick ” Teece, interviewed
immediately after the test-match, suggested, as improve-
ments for the next go, that Jones, Laver and Darling
should be left out. What was the matter with the team,
according to Mr. Teece, was that there were too many
beastly Victorians and South Australians messing about
in it. What is wanted is clearly more men from N.S.W.
Therefore he suggests that we should leave out Laver who
got two wickets, Darling who made the only stand of the
day on Monday, and Jones, off whom Maclaren was missed
at 46 ; and not Howell, the man who missed Maclaren and
only got one wicket; Noble, who failed with both bat and
ball; or Trumper, who showed flukily each time. Unless
the next team is selected under the guidance of clearer-
headed councils than these, Blythe, Braund and co. will
have another romp. y
The one redeeming feature of the Australians’
play Was the. trouble last wicket of the second innings
gave. Bilowell banged away freely and fearlessly, and in
13min. compiled 31, in which there, were six fours.
Sheer, fatuous conceit knocked out Australia.
The local heroes had been boomed by the press until they
believed they had nothing to do but wait till their turn
came at-the wickets and then paste the cover off the
“ feeble” English bowling. Hence slackness in the field,
loose bowling and general want of training-and keenness.
Then Maclaren produced his dark horse, Blythe, and with
him mowed down the blossom of Australian batting
before it came to decent budding-point. The Australians
were confident of getting at least 700 runs on the wicket
provided. But the English fielding, and the three B.’s,
decided otherwise.
A forecast of the next team : Trumper, Darling,
Hill, Noble, Gregory (S.yd.), Jones, M'Beth, Poidevin,
Clark, Kelly, M’Leod, with Trumble as 12th man. Noble
must be retained in spite of failure in one try. Jones had
rotten luck in the match ; Darling cannot be dispensed
with ; M’Leod is the only bowler who came off. Howell
with his one wicket and the general similarity to Noble
and M‘Leod, must stand down. Jones (if he will practise
honestly) will do as well as ever, and, anyhow, you must
have a fast bowler. The three new men suggested are all
from N.S.W. Hack might do better than Poidevin, but
he hasn’t scored off Braund as L.O.S. has. With some-
thing like a return to batting* form, and a little more
swiftness in the field, this troupe should make Maclaren’s
little lot sit up.
* • * *
One clear reason of Australia’s deplorable posi-
tion at the end of test-match No. 1 is the constitutional
incapacity of the Australian to run. To love one’s native
country is a very pure thing, but to prove that love by
the display of a limpet-like attachment to the particular
spot one happens to be standing on in the cricket-field is
to display misplaced emotion. Gregory mo'es about
with some freedom, and Trumper can, on occasions, run
very fast, but the English have a dozen fielders—men like
Jones and Quaife—who are off the mark before the ball is
properly hit, and whose speed is apparently beyond the
possibilities of men burdened with our national tired
feeling.
* * * •
Scutum ” : The ’Stralians made a holy show
of themselves in the first test-match. Most of them
shaped at Braund like schoolboys facing a break bowler
for the first time. Some of them seemed to think that
Braund had something concealed under the pitch which
caught the ball as it touched ground and shied
it into the wicket at an extraordinary angle. But
after much careful searching, and many prods
with the bat, they failed to discover anything
abnormal. Kelly made desperate efforts to knock the
cover off the ball and smash the crowd in the
shilling stand, but his strength was wasted. As a whole
they performed execrably. Gregory stood out above the
rest, but even he never seemed at home. However
praise is due to Barnes (6 for 139), Braund (7 for 101)
Blythe (7 for 56), who bowled splendidly throughout’
Maclaren showed excellent judgment in the placing of his
field, altering their positions for each opposing batsman
as well as to suit his bowlers. His method seems to be to
study the various strokes of each batsman and bis
fallings, and set the field accordingly ; on the other band,
the ’Stralians simply peg away and trust to luck. The
Englishmen likewise made use of their very own umpire
on Saturday : they loaded him up with sweaters and bats
till he looked like an old clo’ man. No doubt Callaway
smiled. To present writer there seem to be marvellous
possibilities in store for the bowler -who can vary his
style of bowling. Blythe sent down a variety in the test,
but he, in common with nine out of ten howlers, makes
a mark from which to start his run. Still, on several
occasions, the ball seemed to leave his hand ere he
actually reached the crease. By varying the style of
bowling is meant changing the method of delivery, i.e.,
the distance of run and the manner of bringing the arm
over. Some years ago, Ironclad 0.C., of Newcastle,
had a bowler who was both-handed. He used to vary bis
bowling by delivering one over from his right hand and
the next from his left, and was fairly successful. But such
instances are rare. In S.M. Herald’s account of the Eng-
lishmen’s first innings, it was stated that M‘ Leod was lucky
in getting Maclaren and Jessop with full tosses. In this
scribe’s humble opinion, bowlers do not make sufficient
use of the full toss. I don’t mean a slow one on the leg,
but a fairly fast one aimed at the top of the wicket
• * * *
Bar Jones, every Australian bowler sent against
the Englishmen in their first innings got one or more
wickets. Jones should have had Maclaren on his list but
for Howell’s mulling. McLeod did best, securing 4 for
84. Trumble’s first showing was when the score topped
100—he accounted for Hayward. “ Points ” Laver and
Noble and “ cover ” Gregory stood out.
• * * *
Jessop’3 first innings lasted 16min., during
which he hit five fours and four ones.
* * * «
Initial innings (Friday last week) of Englishmen
supplied a record for first wicket in test matches played
here. Maclaren and Hayward notched 154 before latter
was caught for 62. Previous best was Ulyett’s and
Barlow’s 122 at Sydney in ’B2. Maclaren and Wain wright
accumulated 111 in ’B9, and Flowers and Read and Brown
and Ward 102 each in ’B5 and ’94 as named. ’Ome record
belongs to Hayward and Jackson—lBs in fifth match of
’99. It’s rather remarkable that Hayward should share
credit in both cases.
* * * *
Clem. Hill grasps the handle of the bat well
down the shoulder, which, though it interferes with free
wrist-play, gives him great command over it. Noble
holds the weapon well up the handle. Hence the differ-
ence in the result when the men jumped out to meet
Braund’s inducers to leave the crease.
• * « *
Oppressed by his run of bad luck in tossing,
Maclaren proposed to the Australians that choice of
innings should alternate in the first four test-matches, a
fresh toss only taking place for the last one. After
deliberation, the local team decided to insist on tossing
anew each match, and the English chief must have
chuckled when he won the first spin of the coin. The
visiting captain’s proposal seems a fair one, but would
certainly arouse opposition. The only other way sug-
gested to minimise the great advantage of winning the
toss is the method that has been adopted in Melb. Pen-
nant matches. There, the side going in first only bats
halt the day, then the fielding side takes 'the wickets,
and so on alternately. This system has not been too
successful. The fact remains that if cricket is reduced to
a science, and luck eliminated from it, the game will lose
considerable charm. Which can also be said of golf,
yachting, and most other pastimes.
* * * *
Opening-day of first England-Australia test-
match (Sydney)broughtls,ooo onlookers. Visitors dropped
M’Gahey, Garnett, and Robson. Maclaren’s score of 116
brought his runs in test matches to over 1000. His
aggregate at moment of writing is 1049, and average 37-46.
Others with four-figure feats in test matches are Grace,
Shrewsbury, Giffen, Darling(ll39), “ Sid.” Gregory (1086),
and Bannerman. The attendance on Saturday was esti-
mated as verging on 40,000, and the sight was remarkable.
Figures are believed to constitute a world’s record. The
crowd on the occasion of one of Ivo Bligh’s team’s matches
is said to have been as big, but facilities for getting near
accuracy were not so reliable.
* * * »
Ed. Bulletin.—A few thoughts suggested by
the test-match. First, a new captain —someone who
knows something of the placing, of Australian fieldsmen
for English batsmen. In this respect Maclaren easily out-
generalled Darling. Secondly, much is heard of the need
of a goad stonewaller to stop a rot. A stonewaller at one
end usually makes the man at the other end cautious
when wickets are falling. Give us, rather, a batsman of
the Graham type, who would give some confidence to the
others. And why not Graham himself ? Again, the slow
bowler must come, and the man who will probably give
place to him is Howell. Bill is not now the field he used
once to be, and his dropping of an easy catch in the slips,
when Maclaren was 43, probably meant 150 to the Eng-
lishmen. Jones bowled faster and fielded better than in
the S.A. match, but he had no luck. Clarke is men-
tioned as a cert, for the next match, but the slow bowler
who will probably have a place in the Australian XI
before the end of the season is—George Giffen. —G. F.
Barrie.
* • * *
If the public’s debilitated faith in bike-racing,
as a sport, can survive the result of this year’s Austral
“struggle,” it must be indeed immortal. There was no
secrecy about the things that might be expected to
happen in the big Stakes. Even the daily press, which
usually professes a complete ignorance of scandals apper-
taining to the malodorous Austral, and does its best to keep
the game alive for the sake of the managerial ad. —even
the daily press, prior to the event, made open reference to
this year’s conspiracy'. True, the allegation of fraudulent
intent on the part of some riders (unnamed, of course)
was coupled with a promise that the committee of the
M.B.C. would do its duty to the public. The case was
put in a way calculated to excite interest and swell the
gate receipts. All the same, it was an astounding fact
that the press pleaded guilty to being not quite blind and
deaf, and destitute of the sense of smell.
The Age said on the morning of the Austral :
“ Developments in the betting have given rise to many'
extraordinary rumors as to intrigues, in which several of
those remaining in the contest are alleged to have been
concerned, but there is good reason for believing that the
committee of the Melbourne Bicycle Club will be found
quite prepared to take drastic action in the event of any
reasonable ground for such action being shown by
the running. The competitors will be very dili-
gently watched. Additional time-keepers and um-
pires have been appointed,” &c., &c. The extra-
ordinary rumors—not that any rumors of roguery
can be out of the common in connection with any bicy'cle
race—radiated from the fact that Bill. Martin, whose
recent “ form” didn’t quite justify the betting, was said
to be a “ moral,” and had been backed down to 2 to 1,
6to 4, and evens. And the explanation of the Billmoral
was as follows. A certain person had backed Bill at long
odds to win so much money that his agents could afford
to “ talk reason ” to a sufficient number of Austral
compel itors in various parts of the handicap to clear
the way for a victorious assertion of Bill’s trustworthy
skill, strength and pluck. Everything that money' could
do for the champion had been done by Bill’s backer, who
took it for granted that William would do the rest and
make an honest effort to win. A comparatively slow
pace was the first essential to a bringing off of the
coup. Therefore the stewards, had they been deter-
mined to declare a “no race” in the event of it
not seeming to be run on its merits (rule 73) would have
concentrated the attention of all their extra timekeepers
and umpires on the front mark men. They knew that
scratch-man Bill was the person for whom the event was
said to have been “ fixed,” and that it couldn’t be so fixed
if the front-mark men did their level best to retain their
handicap advantage among themselves, and show their
wheels to the back-markers from start to finish.
* * • *
The Bill Martin Stakes, as it turned out accord-
ing to prediction, was run in lOsec. slower time than last
year’s Austral. Also, this final heat was slow by compari-
son with the heats that the limit men had run on the
previous Saturday, and Bill Martin won in 4.29 3-ssec.
Heaven knows what the time might have been, but for
Lewis’s decent attempt to win on his own. Everything
had happened as per prediction, but inasmuch as good old
Bill, prediction or otherwise, was the best of ail winners
from a showman’s point of view, the stewards saw
nothing suspicious, except in Frank Beauchamp’s sprint.
They disqualified him for 12 months. He was one of the
few men whom rumor had declared to be honest triers.
» * * •
Whisperings anent final of the Austral, and the
look of the thing should give Vic. legislator Wynne’s bill
for suppressing betting at sports meetings a big shove.
Maximum penalty for an offence is £2O. Persistence in
betting when ordered to stop will bring about arrest, and
persons entering a sports ground after being legally re-
moved incur a penalty of £5O and gaol for three months.
It isn’t likely that Mo will come again. Last year knocked
him limp, and this time he has been completely' flattened
out. Three Melbourne Moseses had a five-thou. book,
which was all snapped up atop of many less amounts
from the smaller fry. Said that Plugger Bill scooped a
fortune and won’t race again. Prize-money was : Winner,
£4OO ; second, £75 ; and third, £25.
* * » •
Little Tartar got away with Q.T.C. Sandgate
Handicap on Saturday from a field of 13, embracing
Derby and Cup winners. A season or two since Little
Tartar was in Warwick Farm (N.S.W.) stable with a great
reputation, hut did no good and went back to Q. to land
a few prizes and wagers. Bred at Dryaaba (Clarence
River) by late Mr. H. Barnes, L.T. is half-brother, by the
Musket horse Tartar, to the speedy Nardoo, who could
beat the best of ’em at pony meetings. Bigger surprise
still was the squelching of Boreas 11. in the Stanley Plate
w.f.a. race, lm. 3f. Starting at odds on in a field of three
he went under to the Derby winner, Narelle, with Brise-
de-Nuits second.
* * * *
Result of Bananaland Cup was remindful of
last Melbourne Cup, in that the winner (Drake), like
Revenue, hadn’t previously won a race for two years, and
yet was much fancied by his stable. He is a fine chestnut
that takes the eye well, and was bred in Q. central district
by Mr. Hatfield. His sire, The Drake, still does stud duty,
though 27 years old. His dam, Decoy, is by Golds-
borough from Sunbeam, by The Barb from Sweetheart
(imp.), by Red Heart. Time (3'3l£) is a record for the
race.
* * • •
Rosehill meet Saturday last caused much weep-
ing and wailing in the ranks of Mo. who, despite the fact
that betting was good on most races, lost to a man.
Finishes, on the whole, were soft things for the winners,
though big fields lined up. Only close go of the day was
Pelissier’s December Handicap. Outsider Barley Bree
rounded the home turn two lengths away, but com-
mencing the last furlong Pelissier got level, and a neck and
neck tussle saw Pelissier pass the post a head to the good.
Three first and three second favorites won the six events.
* * • *
Manner of Baccarat’s shaping at Moorefield a
week or two since brought her heaps of support for
Rosehill Nursery Handicap. She won easily, and in such
a way as to suggest that when she is after it again, and
the company isn’t absolutely first-class, she’ll be worth
an investment. Baccarat is by the Merton stallion
Dalmeny from All Fours.
» * • «
Neither bruiser Turner nor bruiser Youngshowed
better than ordinary form at Sydney Golden Gate A.O.
Saturday night, and Turner won a scrambling thing that
gave everybody the blues. How his people sum him up
as having a hope against Melbourne “ Micko ” Walsh, and
why “ Micko ” doesn’t walk from Melb. to take on the
soft snap is a puzzle. Young and Turner were brought
together because the Waterloo (Sydney) iron-encased lad
Lenton had fought a draw with each, but “ form ” in the
pug. biz has rarely panned out right.
Athletic Union supremacies of the continent
will be contested at Auckland (M.L.) this Friday.
Neither N.S.W. nor Q. have anything like their best
teams. Victoria is most fortunate of all visiting sections,
with 10 reps., and among them the cream of the State.
N.S.W. has six, and Q. four. Absence of Rowley, English,
M’Alister and others weakens the Loan State ; while the
Bananalanders’ prospects would have been considerably
enhanced by the presence of Hawkes. Summed up, it
doesn’t look as if Victoria, N.S.W., or Q. has a hope
against M.L., though combined they might make it a
close thing.

That wonderful human motor known as Plugger
Bill Martin did what the public, who backed him 6 to 4
on, fully expected of him, viz., won the Austral Wheel Race,
from scratch, on his Red Bird. The winning of the
Austral Wheel Race was Bill’s great ambition. Last year
A. 0. Forbes, also on a Red Bird, won the Austral in
world’s record time.*

This cablegram was printed last week :
There are now no British troops north of the Delajroa
Bay line.
If that is true it amounts to the astounding fact
that the Boers have recovered temporary posses-
sion of more than half the Transvaal. “ What is
truth?” said Pilate. Pilate had evidently been
reading the cablegrams, and had dropped the
paper and taken off his helmet to let the cool
breeze from the Dead Sea blow on his staring head
when he said it.
William M'Millan, importer :
I advocate the fixing- of a duty which will not have the
effect of reducing imports.
No doubt. The rising of local industries which
would reduce imports may be good for Australia,
but it isn’t good for the importer. Again, spe.ik-
ing of Sydney :
It is very curious to note that the trade in a State with
a free port is honeycombed by thousands of agents for
manufacturers. The place is swamped with consign-
ments.

Kaiser William, talking to his German
contingenters before they sailed for China, told
them to repay sundry “ outrages ” to the Father-
land Flag with 100 per cent., and give the Chows
a horribly bad time. And now two Berlin
journalists have been gaoled for publishing yarns
to the effect that the German troops were
murderous when they got the chance.
A woman-canvasser raises, in Sydney E.
News, a horrified squeal over the fact that, in some
Government offices, employees are, during hot
weather, occasionally discoverable in their shirt-
sleeves. The Bulletin had not thought that
anything so sensible was allowed in Government
offices, and hopes that no official notice will be
taken of the cackle of the kind of meddlesome hen
that necessitates the term “lydy” as a variant
upon “lady.” A lady doesn’t anxiously quest
around for shocks to her propriety.
Freetrader Cameron, of Tasmania, brought
a South Australian and an imported plough-
share into the House of Reps, t’other day, and
became quite violent, in a small Tasmanian
way, about the alleged inferiority of the local
article. Cameron’s superior imported share was
an American one, produced under a stupendous
Protective tariff, so the apparent inference is that
when Australia, has as much Protection as the
U.S., and has it as long,, it may do equally good
work. Anyhow, the spectacle of a Freetrader
holding forth, as a proof of the advantages of
Freetrade, about the excellence of an implement
made under about the heaviest Protective tariff
on earth has its humorous side.
Wondrous sample of the importing party’s
logic from report of House of Reps, debate :
Mr. Willis (N.S.W.), argued that no protection of the
implement industry was necessary, as was evidenced by
the fact that South Australia had been able to send
ploughshares to N.S. Wales, Queensland, and Tasmania,
and successfully compete with all foreign makers.
Which may be summed up thus
(1) South Australia, under Protection, could produce
ploughshares at such a low price that it could compete
with all foreign makers, even in the‘'N.S.W. free market.
Therefore, Protection didn’t raise the price as alleged.
This proves that Protection should be abolished and that
Foreign-trade is the only i sound policy.
(2) If South Australia, hnder Protection, couldn’t pro-
duce ploughshares as cheap as the imported article that
would also prove that Protection should be abolished and
that Foreign-trade is the only sound policy.
(3) N.S. W. under its Foreign-trade policy didn’t pro-
duce ploughshares to any extent. That also proves
Foreign-trade to be the only sound policy.
The people of Holland, who have been
deriving a lot of mild satisfaction from their local
libels on King Edward since his poor old mother
ceased to be a cartoon subject, have now got their
own royal fish to fry. After nine months of
matrimony, the German prince-consort of Queen
Wilhelmina, who is said to be an absurdly
arrogant young potentate, asserted his semi-
divine right to come home drunk to dinner, and
was nagged by his royal missus until lie told her
to go to —we dunno the Dutch equivalent for it.
Whereupon the lodger—otherwise Her Majesty’s
aide-de-camp—took the lady’s part and a duel
ensued, with bad results to the lodger. After
which another “courtier” was kicked in the
bread-basket for interfering between man and
wife. The Dutch court case ought to be well
worth watching. Which way are the sympathies
of the fiery 20-year-old Queen going “to jump?
Does she pity the lodger who took up arms on her
account, or feel inclined to worship the German
husband who wasn’t too drunk to make him sorry
he spoke ? At latest accounts she was sticking to
her old man : a queen is nevertheless a woman.
Apropos the butter-box controversy, the
manager of Tweed River (N.S.W.) Creamery
Co. asserts that he has used local pine for the last
three years with every success.
Broken Hill just now has two men very
badly “set.” These men, on a recent holiday,
when 800 or 900 of the Hill’s hard-drinkers were
picnicking at Stephen’s Creek, lay low for a couple
of sly-grog-sellers. One man went into a shanty,
pretended sudden illness, and asked for brandy—
which was given him, and paid for. Result,
prosecution of the shanty-man and a £5O fine.
The shanty-keepers have now a special bottle
ready for the next man who takes ill on their
premises.
An interesting perjury case was lately
tried before the Ruthyn (Wales) assizes. The
confessed perjuress was a Manchester girl,
who seemingly came along to manage the
refreshment dept, at a fine-arts museum in a
Welsh town of unpronounceable name. At first
her success was great. She knew a thing or two
more than the ordinary girl associated with
museums. Pretty soon she was engaged to the
local schoolmaster, and became “an active church
worker,” and the curate spoke of her in a general
way as “ our dear sister.” After a while the local
matrons drew unfavorable conclusions from the
appearance—the “figure ” —of Miss Redfern, late
and she was asked to retire from
the fine-arts refreshment bar. So she appealed to
theschool master, telling him that he must be the
father of her future child, but the schoolmaster
would have nothing more to do with the siren.
Then Miss Redfern threw herself and her sha-a-me
on the sympathy of the curate, and the curate
gave her enough money to get her through her
trouble. Therefore the grateful girl transferred
her allegations from the tough schoolmaster to the
gentle shepherd, and ultimately obtained an
affiliation order against him. For years the
curate was a wandering outcast, and the school-
master walked in the shadow of respectable sus-
picion. Then a bundle of letters came to light,
letters written by the barmaid to a gardener em-
ployed at the museum gardens. Result : a full
confession by both parties of how the gardener used
to interview Miss Redfern in her room at night by
means of a rope ladder. His name was Madoc Jones
and her child was his child, and he had wanted
to marry her, but she “considered him beneath
her,” she said. The schoolmaster, being a person
of higher standing, Miss Redfern hoped to bounce
or wheedle him into matrimony, whereupon he
advised her to put the baby down to the curate if
she wanted a good ‘ ‘ mark ” for blackmail. Per-
haps the pedagogue had been jealous of her active
interest in church work, or more likely this part
of her confession was a lie invented to damage her
former sweetheart. The two things quite
beyond question in this sweet story are
the rope-ladder liaison (on one occasion a
head gardener removed the ladder, and Don Juan
had to let himself down by a sheet), and the girl’s
profound sense of social superiority to the man
who catered for her passion. What a satire on the
orthodox magisterial injunction to marry the girl,
and make her respectable ! The holy contract of
matrimony, as the candid Miss Redfern sees it, is
an arrangement by which a woman may lose caste
if she enters into it with the lover on whom she
bestowed her favors.

Slowbart Mercury (same editor for 40
years) persists in holding the Labor party respon-
sible for the high tariff and uses dreadful language
—the mild Hobart kind of dreadful, that is. The
high tariff is so low that Tasmania seems likely to
go bankrupt under it unless a lot more direct
taxation is imposed, and a large section of the
Labor party is trying to cut it down further and
make Tasmania more bankrupt still. But a man
who has edited a dreadful paper like the Mercury
for 40 years can’t be expected to notice a little
thing like that.

"Hoehtah!"-A Song of Woman's Eyes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oall night they say to me j
Hooshtah !
J Wheresoever I may flee:
Hooshtah !
“Man ! kneel down upon the sand
Till we bind you with our band
Hooshtah!
Camel-like, I hear them cry :
Hooshtah /
In the valleys or on high :
Hooshtah ! Hooshtah /
’Mid the roses or the gums,
Lo, the order gently comes —
Hooshtah /
And I kneel me gravely down—
Hooshtah! Hooshtah /
By the forest or the town —
Hooshtah /
By the sea’s imperial way
I must kneel me and obey
Hooshtah !
Down in meadowlands of sleep—
Hooshtah!
Doth the order slyly creep—
Hooshtah !
Sweet young eyes for ever call:
“On your knees, O Mammal, fall! ”
Hooshtah I Hooshtah l
And they load me with soft sighs—
Hooshtah !
With dear glances of their eyes :
Hooshtah 1
Bales of bliss they place on me,
As they chatter by the sea—
Hooshtah ! Hooshtah /
It is pleasant labouring—
Hooshtah !
Where the eyes for ever sing
Hooshtah ! Hooshtah l
May I be a camel aye !
Gladly hearing sweet eyes say
Hooshtah l
Life’s a desert for us all—
Hooshtah l
Till the dear eyes softly call
Hooshtah 1
But the desert doom grows dim,
When there rings o’er Silence’ rim,
Hooshtah ! Hooshtah !
Far and near, O hear them cry,
Hooshtah !
Darling eyes half wet, half dry,
Hooshtah !
Call me, sweet eyes, when you will,
I’ll kneel for you though you kill !
Hooshtah !
V.
GRANT HERVEY.
“ Robert Brothers ” writes to The Bulle-
tin of Maoriland women in politics :
Theoretically, women should be enfranchised. All the
argument favors giving her the vote. The woman of
Maoriland have had the franchise of Maoriland a long
time now. When I travelled M.L. and talked with many
hundreds of women, I wanted to see how much in-
terest they would take in the affairs of a country, and
I tried to draw them out ; but, after all my efforts, I
could only get them to look tired and change the sub-
ject at the first opportunity.
The municipal elections were on in Greymouth when
I was there, and every woman over 21 went to the
poll, and enjoyed going. I was talking to one of those
red-faced bushwomen, with an arm on her like a leg
of mutton. She had a husband and seven or eight
daughters, and she told me that they were all going
to- vote for So-and-So. So-and-So was not a nice-look-
ing man. (It is' all bosh about the women
voting for nice-looking men.) But he meant to put
down the drink traffic. And that was why my
friend Biddy was going to vote for him, and make all the
family vote for him. And now I have travelled all Maori-
land, and talked to women in every town, I know that
prohibition is about the only question that interests the
woman-voter.
Yet the average Maorilander is a very sober fellow. It
seems marvellous that so big an outcry should be made
for such a little cause. The M.L. woman has done part
of her business, and that part very badly. After so many
years of temperance legislation the temperance man
heaves a sigh, and admits that it has proved futile. In
days gone by there were many hotels and much drinking
done. I mean much in his eyes ; not in mine accustomed
to the heavier drinking in Australia. The only change
now is that more drinkers go into one bar; and those
men who are lucky enough to have licenses are reaping
big harvests. Prohibition, therefore, has only made
hotel-keeping a better investment, and hotel properties
more valuable. That is one result brought about by the
women in politics.
But they have done more than that. There were men
who had made sufficiently respectable livings out of
hotelkeeping who lost their licenses, and many of these
set up sly grog-selling. Now, sly grog-shops do not sup-
ply pure liquors Then some greater attraction is wanted
than drink, so attractive girls with loose morals are sup-
plied. And, in the third place, only those frequent sly
grog-shops who are likely to be most harmed—young
fellows rising out of their teens, fellows who would not go
into a public-house for the sake of a stimulant, but who
patronise the sly grog-shop because of the distinction it
gives them in their own estimation as men about town,
and the excitement supplied by the loose girls. And that
is the other and worse result of M.L. women in politics.
They have made sly grog-selling pay.
Somehow the average woman doesn’t seem built for
politics. Up at Tuggerah, N.S.W., I once heard a fisher-
man say that he would vote for Protection because then
he wouldn’t have his baskets stolen. Well, the end and
aim of the Maoriland woman seems to be to give her vote
solely to keep her old man from going into the public-
house on his way home on pay-day.
Apropos the above. Theoretically men
should be enfranchised. There is absolutely no
reasonable ai’gument against it. The men of
N.S.W. have had the vote a long time now.
When the writer travelled round at the last
election he talked with a score or two of men to
see how much interest they would take in the af-
fairs of their own country. Many told him they
would vote for old Reid because he wasn’t a
bad sort, and “could sling his slack.” Several
others voted for another candidate “because
he’s a member of our cricket club.” All
the same, writer doesn’t see that that is
any reason why men should not be allowed
to vote. Mr. Brothers’ idea seems to be that
because Prohibition is the chief subject the
collective Maoriland woman is interested in, she
should be deprived of a vote. Will Mr. Brothers
say how many subjects one should be interested
in to qualify for a vote? Also, how many subjects
is Mr. Brothers interested in ?


Premier Barton does not inquire suffi-
ciently into departmental matters, but trusts to
his official “ remembrancer,” with the result that
some of his answers to questions in Parliament
are far from accurate. Supporters of the Govern-
ment are always complaining of these little errors.
Cabled that Roosevelt derides the idea of
U.S. interference in S’Africa. We should smile !
England stands by while Roosevelt and co. grab
the Philippines to perfect their tobacco-monopoly ;
and Roosevelt, like a good business man, returns
the compliment.
What miserable piffle is thought worthy
of the cable nowadays? Just been wired to the
Australian dailies that English cricket-captain
Maclaren “ has been appointed assistant-treasurer
to the Hampshire County C.C.” If it had even
been full treasurer, now !
Obit: In Maoriland, that well-known
Sydney identity, Abraham Friedman, of Castle-
reagh-st. However tough Abraham was as a
lender, he had little personal luck as a speculator ;
he failed to lay to his book. The cigars he gave
to his clients were always of irreproachable
quality.
A collision, which might easily have proved
a terrible disaster, occurred in Port Jackson on
Saturday night last between the harbor-ferry
steamer Lady Hampden and the Colonial Sugar
Co.’s s.s. Fiona. The former, which was much
damaged, had 300 persons aboard. According to
all accounts a large number of the young men
passengers looked carefully to their own safety,
and rushed a relieving steamer for first places at
the expense of the women.
Sydney D.T., trying hard to take its
newspaper postage with dignity, swears only at
the “glaring anomaly” that if 500 newspapers
‘ ‘ weighing eight ounces each are posted separately
to different addresses the stamp charges would be
just the same as if the whole 500 were sent in a
bulk parcel to the one address.” How carefully
it failed to discover the anomaly of allowing 500
paper to go free separately when they were
allowed to go likewise in bulk !
A correspondent to a N.S.W. country
paper alleges—
Before the local P.M. a laborer was fined £2 for using
indecent language ; another laborer, also charged with
same offence, was fined £5 ; then the Mayor of the town
was summoned for insulting language. He was dis-
charged (although he admitted using words equally as
offensive as those the laborers were charged with), the
P.M. expressing a wish that the defendant being Mayor
the newspapers should suppress the proceedings.
The Department of Justice should assuredly
investigate the incident.

Mr. Eyre early began to interest himself in exploration,
and in 1836 conducted an expedition across the Aus-
tralian continent from Sydney to Swan River, W.A.
Eyre’s journey round the Great Bight occurred in
1840-41. No one crossed from settlement in
N.S.W. to settlement in W.A. in 1836.
“ Melb. Man” : Concerning the statement
that The Bulletin, in spite of being “ forbidden,”
gets to S’Africa to a greater extent than any
■other Australasian paper, the evidence of traders
in this city is conclusive. The S’African circula-
tion of The Bulletin is shown by the number ox
inquiries after special goods advertised in its
columns. For instance, Baden Powell (of all
people) lately wrote to the Daylesford Mineral
Water Co. asking further particulars of their
aerated water machine, and a letter from another
officah at the front disclosed the fact that Barnum
P. had written on information received from the
outlawed publication, which is red from cover to
cover.
The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Co., Limited, reports Wheat : Some important trans-
actions during the week have taken place, the shippers
being the principal operators, and as a consequence of
their eagerness to secure prime grain the market for such
descriptions has for the time being materially improved,
and for the best Western samples as high as 2s. 10id. has
been reached. Chickwheat, too, commands relatively
high rates, and is worth to 2s. Bd. per bushel. Flour con-
tinues on a comparatively low rate, and the millers cannot
afford at present rates of wheat to operate, except for im-
mediate requirements.*

The Slaughter of the Children.
During October and November there were 5933
deaths among the Boer prisoners confined in
Ketehener’s concentration camps, and 4904 of the
victims were children. For the two previous
months (August and September) 2474 children
died, so the figures are mounting up. At the
present rate of extirpation, the whole of the children
of the Boer race will be hilled off in much less than
two years. The servile Jingo papers still describe
the process of confining women and children in
places where the average life of the confinee
is about twenty-four months, with the alter-
native of burning them out and turning them
adrift without food and shelter in the desert,
as pampering an ungrateful enemy and consigning
her to a palatial existence of unaccustomed luxury,
and so on. But the facts are difficult to explain
away, and the Jingo paper has a hard row
to hoe. The most common explanation now is
to the effect that the chief cause of the awful
mortality is the insanitary habits of the Boer
women and their inefficiency as child-nurses,
which is hardly a convincing lie, all things
taken into account. When the Boer womaTn
is in her own home, and away from the surround-
ings now provided for her by the kindly
Ketchener, she is likely to be just as ignorant
and insanitary as at any other time. Yet the
Transvaal is a country with a low death-rate, and
the Boer child dies off less rapidly than most other
children. In Ketchener’s concentration camps,
where the Boer woman surely can’t have ac-
quired any extra ignorance through association
with the Britisher, the Boer child, among alleged
surroundings of unaccustomed luxury, dies several
dozen times faster than the average. And there
is always the lingering suspicion that if an
examination of the concentration camps would
convince any ordinary person that the hideous
mortality is not Ketchener’s doing, and that
the natural and incurable degradation of the
Boer is wholly responsible, these camps would
be open to a certain amount of inspec-
tion, the Red Cross nurses would not have
been ejected from them, and Miss Emily
Hobhouse, the English lady whom Ketchener, m
an absent-minded moment, admitted to a view of
the inner working of the machine, wouldn t have
been so furiously driven off the premises.
Ketchener and Chamberlain have tried themselves
in secret tribunal over the matter, and found
themselves not guilty, but that species of trial is
apt to be inconclusive.
At Poverty Point.
Melba sent good wishes and regrets that she could not
be present, to Amy Castles the night ot her London
concert. . . .
After all, London press comments seem to -criticise
Amy Castles not as an artist, but as a student.
Albert Whelan, Australian mimic (whose success
abroad, by the way, The Bulletin somewhat enthusi-
astically prophesied) gets a whole bill to himself at
London Empire, with his name in green letters as long as
a page of Hansard, and the rest of the show in purple.
This is indeed fame.
Sydney last week saw two theatrical managers (Denham
and Fuller) hiring expensive legal assistance, and de-
manding blood and hair and damages, over a question of
the right to “The Kelly Gang a stage-howl that
nobody of any literary instinct would care to have found
in his dust-barrel. Long extracts from the dreadful
thing were read to a dazed Judge, who probably never
magined before that the world contained such literature.
A truly extraordinary likeness was the resemblance of
Clarence Harvey (of Josephine Stanton co.), as Beppo, in
“Fra Diavolo,” to John Cole, as “the tramp, in the
panto, of “ The Babes in the Wood,” produced here a few
veare ago. Face, figure, voice, action, were wonderfully
alike ■ yet nothing is more certain than that neither is a
copy ’of the other. It might be worth some manager s
while to try them as the “ two Dromios.”
Several vears ago, Dan Barry’s dramatic co. showed for
one night'at Wellington, N.S.W., and, after the second
act, Dan appeared before the curtain to return thanks.
He told his audience that he had travelled thousands of
miles in his time, and had never been so impressed with a
place as he had been with Wellington (cheers). The
scenery was beautiful, climate superb, and the people
pictures of health and happiness. Next night the co.
appeared at Davisville, eight miles distant, and Dan
again toed the footlights with much the same pitch. A
man in the audience, who had attended the performance
the night previous, interjected, “Oh! you told them
that at Wellington last night ” ; whereupon returned the
ever-ready Dan —“ Yes j but I hadn t seen Davisville
then 1 ”
Percy Grainger, who in ’O4, as a 12-year-old boy, cap-
tured Melbourne concert-goers with his golden curls and
correct fingering, was, at latest, in London, where he made
his debut at a recital in Steinway Hall. His playing gets
most encouraging notices from the leading critics. Six
vears at Frankfort, under Kwast, have transformed him
into a slim youth with the stereotyped yellow mane
(straight) and soulful expression necessary to a successful
pianist.
The hairy Australian millionaire played by Walter
Baker in “ Hearts are Trumps” is a very English concep-
tion of the article. Millionaire Baker is certainly sup-
posed to have emigrated from England, and made his
fortune in a Tysonian way, but that doesn t quite justify
his Lancashire-Yarkshire dialect and his gloomy refer-
ences to “graves wi’out headstones, and bones wiout
craves.” Australian experience “way oop in the dry
North” doesn’t incline a capitalist to tragic oracular
remarks. ,
