Plain Bill Holloway has retired from the stage, his fare-
well being given in S’Africa. He had been 36 years on
the boards, and thought that his life work was done. His
daughter, Juliet Sydney, also retires, bn the ground of
ill-health. .
A certain dramatic co., pretty well-known in Australia,
has, according to M.L. papers, had a wild time in Wanga-
nui district. As an excited crowd of creditors was under-
‘stood to be hanging about the railway-station, the princi-
pals drove to Aramoho Junction, where the scouts
reported that more enraged creditors were waiting. So
the principals went on to Fordell, and there struck a
third commando of people, who dealt out blue paper in
terrific stvle. Then the train came in, and the elder pro-
prietor was almost rent in several parts. His co., which
was on board the train, seized his head and shoulders
his creditors, who were on the platform, grabbed his feet
and pants What gave way is not related, but the ? nd of
ft was that theco. on the train hauled the fragments of
their man on board, and steamed away in triumph.

Strong-man Sampson, who was in Sydney a couple
years back, was recently, at Hamburg, severely mauled
by the Polar bears with which he had been performing.
He had, per programme, wrestled with and thrown one of
the brutes, when the other six escaped from the cage and
fell fiercely upon the man and their comrade, one of them
stripping the flesh and breaking the bones of Sampson’s
forearm. The small Hercules, however, added yet
another to his extraordinary list of quick recoveries.
When in Sydney, he burst a gastric bloodvessel, and, later
on, in St. Petersburg, his “ Roman Column ” gave way
and one of his thumbs was torn out. A similar accident
in Paris caused the dislocation of Sampson’s right knee;
and again, in Buenos Ayres, the platform, upon which he
was supporting 12 women, came down and crushed him
nearly to death. He then invested in £3OOO worth of
Polar bear, with the result above set forth.


THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
A London P. M. lately fined a man £2 and
costs for keeping dogs that barked at night time.
The magistrate laid it down that if the barking of
the dogs annoyed two or more persons defendant
was liable for creating a public nuisance.
From the new paper started by Frank
Harris (late editor of the Fortnightly Review) :
The Duke of Cornwall and York, strange to say, though
born and bred here, speaks with as German an acoent as
do his mother and his aunt, Princess Christian. The
King himself burrs his r’s very oddly, and the Queen has
a most pronounced accent, which a Danish lady tells me
is German-English, and not Danish-English. Her father
and mother, the King and Queen of Denmark, were both
of German stock, and spoke Danish always in strong
German, to the vexation of the people, who loved them
well, and had no other fault to find with a very charming
couple. Queen Alexandra is so beautiful, and her praises
are sung so often, that it may be forgiven to one to hint
that her voice alone is not perfection. A little harsh and
guttural, it hardly seems to belong to her. I hear, in this
connection, that a young girl, engaged in embroidering
the pall for the late Queen’s coffin, remarked, after she
had been interviewed by every English and foreign
royalty at Osborne, “ After all, the Kaiser is the only one
who can really speak English.”
It is seriously proposed in U.S.A. to
change the name of the Philippines to the
M‘Kinley Islands. The awful prospect of being
known as “M‘Kinleyinos” is perhaps responsible
for the recrudescence of revolt among thePilipinos,

A SLUGGISH LIVER
Newcastle Proof for
Newcastle Readers.
A Lesson Worth Learning.
The Case of
Mr. D. LEACH.
(BY A NBWGASTLE REPORTER.)
The work of the press is usually of in-
finite public benefit in its immediate re-
sults, as it paves the way for all those bene-
ficent reforms to which all progressive
people are aspiring. Complete evidence was
lately obtained by a reporter from Mr.
Daniel Leach, of Downie-street, Smedmore,
Newcastle, to justify the above opinion, but
to those to whom the subject is unfamiliar a
perusal of the words of Mr. Leach is re-
spectfully commended.
“ When I left Ratcliffe,” said Mr. Leach,
“ which is a small town about seven miles
from Manchester, I came straight to New-
castle and have been working there ever
since, which embraces a period of a dozen
years. Three years prior to that I was in a
very bad state of health, my internal organs
being s> much upset after an accident in
which my shoulder bone was broken that I
could not get my food to agreed with me
I suffered from a sluggish liver, it was evi-
dent, and the drowsiness which came over
me after meals was distressing ”
What sort of an appetite did you have ? ”
en pared the reporter.
A very fickle one. Some days I felt
hungry and so i e days I had no appetite at
all. When I had eaten freely my stomach
became bloated out, and I felt considerable
pain as if the food was fermenting. If I ate
but very little the same unpleasantness was
experienced, so it was quite plain that in-
digestion was one of my ailments. I became
so melancholy that I lost all interest and
pleasure in life, and the awful depression in
my spirits was even worse when I got up to
work in the mornings, as my loss of energy
was so great that I felt thoroughly incap-
able of lasting the days out. Every evening
I returned home feeling very weary and
fagged out, there not being a diain of
vitality left in my system; but the curious
part of it was that I could not sleep when I
went to bed. My brain was never at rest,
as I started thinking directly my head
rouched the pillow, and as the hours
dragged slowly along my misery increased,
for when the clock pointed to the hour to
arise my limbs were aching intensely, and I
was almost too exhausted to get up. The
only variety I h id was by occasionally fall-
ing asleep for a little time, and then the
most unhappy dreams came to my mind and
woke me abruptly. My tongue was as dry
is a piece of vood, and I also had a foul
taste in the mouth. A fearful pain in my
chest came after I had eaten a little food,
and those pains went right through to the
back and hurt me betw’een the shoulders.
Sometimes there was a sinking sensation in
my stomach as if I were on the point of
starvation, but when food was placed before
mel sickened at the sight of it. I lingered on
like this from w- ek to week, suffering acutely
from aches acrosß my loins, and anxious
fears obtruded on my mind btM.use of my
heart palpitating. This gen. rally happened
when I had been exerting myself by walk-
ing a little distance, but at other times my
heart started beating and thumping with-
out any apparent cause. A strange feeling
came to my eyes, as if they were swollen,
and some days my sight was so bad that
everything I looked at appeared to be
clo ked in a mist. You must know that 1
was quite a young man when all this trouble
c me, and it was a serious thing for me to
be wasting away like I was, and with my
nervous system so terribly affected that I
was set all of a shake by the slightest noise,
besides continually fearing that something
of a dreadful nature was going to take place.
The irregular condition of my bowels was
very injurious, but what was I to do?
Nothing gave me permanent relief from con-
stipation, and the same remark applies to
all my ailments. As I had to work for my
living my daily loss of strength was making
my future look very black, and anybody
who has suffered like I have can understand
what a matter for great rejoicing it was
when I found a medicine to give me re-
lief.”
“ Was it prescribed for you ? * asked the
newspaper man.
“ No. I was very fortunate in saving
doctor’s expenses, as the medicine I took
was simply through reading some of the ex-
traordinary cures which had been effected
by using Clements Tonic. What had cured
others might possibly help me, I thought,
so I sent for some Clements Tonic and began
to take it. When I had drunk two bottles
my appetite had so improved that I could
eat nearly as well as ever, and the pains
were not half so bad as they had been. I
was quite satisfied with the advance I had
already made, and did not lose any time in
providing myself with more Clements
Tonic, which steadily imparted vigor to my
weakened system, and as I got the full
benefit of the food I could now relish I need
hardly tell you that I was soon strong
enough to work all day without feeling the
least distress. To promote digestion I found
that Clements Tonic could not be equalled,
as there were none of those inconveniences
resulting from flatulence after I had been
taking that medicine a while, and my chest
was quite free from the oppression which
had almost smothered me. Bodily pains
were gradually extinguished by Clements
Tonic, and I was exceedingly glad to find
that my heart palpitations had ceased to
exist, while as a nerve-toning medicine I am
sure I was taking the best it was possible to
obtain, for my nervousness disappeared
completely, and my system was invigorated
throughout. It was a remarkable restora-
tion to health which I experienced through
using Clements Tonic, which gave me per-
fect freedom from all my ailments, and as I
continued to use that medicine till my
health was sound in every way it is only
fair to give all the credit to Clements 1 onic.
“ Have you had any illness since then ? ”
“ A few years ago I had four of my ribs
broken, and my system was broken down
again for a while. Indeed, I got intensely
weak and miserable, but I knew what to
fall back upon by my first experience, and
with the use of a few bottles of Clements
Tonic I was made strong and vigorous as
before. Working in the mines is very ex-
hausting labor at the best of times, but in
summer it is particularly hard on the con-
stitution, and when I feel that the strain is
commencing to tell on my strength I get a
bottle or two of Clements Tonic to keep my-
self in proper working order, and the result
is that I am always healthy and happy.”
“ You have adopted a wise plan, Mr.
Leach.”
“ I know that, and others would be
better off if they followed my example; but
when they have read these remarks, which
you can publish in any way, they can judge
for themselves.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Daniel Leach, of Downie-street, Smedmore,
near Newcastle, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and sin-
cerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of four folios, and
consecutively numbered from one to four, and that
ii contains and is a true and faithful account of nay
illness and cure by Clement. Tonic, and also contains
my full permission to publish in any way m)
statements—which I give voluntarily, without re-
ceiving any payment; and I make this solemn de-
claration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Smedmore, this
twenty-third day of October, one thousand nine
hundred and one, before me,
PETER BENNETT, J.P.

WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people,
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large sums of money in studying that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact and disseminate the knowledge universally, We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of y r our medical advisers, our method of treatment
may be the fact stili remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received from
grateful patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
wav the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
iealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to run riot in your system, you
are guilty of culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.

h
HE PIANOLA is an instrument by means of which anyone
can play the Piano.
This is accomplished by simply rolling the Pianola in
front of any piano, allowing the felt-covered fingers to rest
upon the keys of the piano, then inserting a roll of perforated paper
into the sockets, and operating the pedals. Playing-directions appear
upon the roll as the music unwinds.
Thus the Pianola provides a substitute for the human fingers and
a correct technique for any piece.
The artistic effects and vivifying personality are given to the ren-
dition by the player, who controls the expression. It is entirely subject
to his will. He may vary the touch from the most delicate pianissimo
to the “ lion’s touch of Rubinstein.”
This human element is the distinctive feature of the Pianola, and
removes it from the ranks of purely mechanic al musical instruments. It
taxes the credulity of all who have rot heard it, but is substantiated by
the fact that the Pianola
is the only Piano = Player
endorsed by musicians
of note.

A Surprise-Party.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bob waa a born leader. Everybody said so. The
fact waa apparent by the way he had always
managed our games of football and cricket, our
boating-parties, our camps at Middle Harbor, and
our midsummer dances at Fern Bay. So we
decided to find some fitting means of recognising
his past services. Jim McGrath ran the move-
ment, and devised a scheme that gave general
satisfaction. We were to give Bob a gigantic
surprise-party—a real surprise-party, not one of
those where the person surprised was found to
have previously removed the sewing-machine,
gipsy-tables, photographs and vases from the
front room, leaving only a few chairs and that old
family relic, the piano.
The party turned-up. There were about thirty
of us, including the ladies. Jim, our captain,
was in high spirits and declared the night a tip-
top one for a dance. No one disputed it.
Promptly at eight o’clock he gave the command,
and we moved en masse down street toward Bob’s
place. Imagine our chagrin upon reaching the
house to find a big “To Let” notice in the
window !
There was no doubt about the place having once
been Bob’s residence —quite ten of the party were
ready to swear to it. Several more swore to give
vent to their feelings; but Jim, I must say, acted
manfully throughout.
“ We won’t be done-in ! ” he declared, and ran
next door to make inquiries. He returned in a
few seconds, beaming. “31 Thomson-street,” he
said, and started off.
We followed, not quite so happy, perhaps, for
Thomson-street was some distance away, and our
baskets were heavy. However, the street was
reached at last, and having located the number,
Jim called a halt.
“’Old on,” said he ; “let’s stand back a bit and
go in by twos and threes, like a procession. ’E’ll
never know when it’s goin’ t’end.”
We hailed his plan with delight, and I went
with him to the door. He was very excited, as
was evident from the way he tugged at the bell.
“Well,” said an old man, opening the door
suddenly; “d’ye want to pull the house down ?”
I felt inclined to say that the house looked
rather like the kind that would fall naturally, but
checked myself.
“Not exactly,” returned Jim. “We want to
know if Bob Walton’s at ’ome.”
“Eh?”—the old man looked very Cross at us.
I concluded he must be either Bob’s grandfather
or a lodger. ,
“We wanter know if Bob’s at ’ome,” Jim re-
peated.
“Eh? I’m a bit deaf; you’ll have to speak
louder.”
He placed his hand behind his ear and leaned
forward.
“We—want—to know—if Bob’s at ’ome,”
yelled Jim.
“ Bob ! Oh, is that it ?—Bob ! ” The old man’s
face softened. “ Oh, no, he’s not in just now.”
“ That’s a pity ! ” observed Jim.
“Eh?”
“ I say—that’s—a—pi—ty.”
“Yes, yes. I —ah—understand.”
“ Will ’e be long ? ” shouted Jim.
“Dunno,” replied the old man. “Went out
with his mother some time ago. I don’t suppose
he’ll be long.”
I looked at Jim and pitied his bad luck. Jim
was apoplectic. “ We’ll wait if you like,” he
bawled in the old man’s ear. But the old man
didn’t ask us in. “Just as you like,” was his
answer.
Jim became desperate. “ See ’ere, ”he shouted,
“ we’re some o’ Bob’s partickler friends, an’ we’re
come to give ’im a little treat: so you might as
well let’s in.”
“ I see,” the old man ejaculated—we turned
and saw the other 28 standing at the gate; they
had become impatient—“lt’s a bit of a surprise-
party, eh ?”
We admitted the fact and were invited inside.
I’ll never forget the look on the old man’s face as
he watched us trying to stow ourselves in the
front room. Fortunately, nothing worse than
folding doors divided us from the dining-room,
and we crowded in there also. We then sugges-
ted removing some of the furniture. The old man
admitted the wisdom of the idea, and we set to
work with a will. The girls removed the photo-
graphs, vases, etc., while we shifted the heavier
things, Jim superintending. Nothing was left in
the big double room but the piano. Before this
our chief pianist seated himself and struck some
startling chords, then launched forth into a waltz,
which soon had the effect of filling the room with
a seething, whirling, twirling mass. It was
grand ! We almost forgot that the object of our
entertainment was missing. Even when we did
think of him we consoled ourselves with the re-
flection that the party would be a genuine surprise
to him. Jim pulled us up at 11.30.
“Time to git supper ready,” said he; “Bob’s
bound to be ’ome soon.”
We carried in the tables belonging to the dining-
room and a couple of others from the kitchen;
also chairs innumerable, not to mention boxes and
shelves. The baskets were unpacked, plates and
dishes piled with cakes or fruit, and for the benefit
of a few teetotallers the old man made coffee. He
seemed to have entered into the spirit of the
thing. I was about to go outside for a smoke and
wait for Bob, when above the laughter and
chatter I heard distinctly a woman’s voice. It
came from somewhere near the front door.
“ Good Heavens ! ” it exclaimed. “ Are we in
the right house ? ”
Now, could there have been a more perfect
surprise-party? Here was a woman made to
doubt if she were in her own house. I heard the
old man’s step in the hall.
“Some o’ Bob’s friends,” he was explaining.
“ Bob’s friends ! ” echoed the lady. “ Did you
toll them to come, Bob ? ”
“ No.” I thought by the sound of his voice
(hat Bob had a cold.
“A bit of a surprise-party,” explained the old
man.
“I should think it was!” said the lady—a
remark that made me feel proud of Jim.
Another moment and she stood in the room, a
young man—a friend of Bob’s, doubtless—stand-
ii g bs3ide her.
“ Here's a pretty mess ! ” she cried. “ Who are
they all?”
Jim came forward and the company settled
itself to listen.
“ Who are they, Bob ? ” she again demanded.
To my surprise, the young man beside her
made answer: “ Can’t say as I know any of ’em.”
“Hasn’t Bob come yet?” Jim inquired.
“Eh? Can’t you see him? Here he is!” the
lady exclaimed.
‘ ‘ No, no ! ” said Jim. “ I mean Bob Walton,”
and I fancied his voice trembled.
" Walton, Walton I'* th© lady rej jeated,
Never heard o’th© name. Our name’s Walker.
Eh 1 W-a-l-k-e-rl”
For a few seconds the silence was awful. Then
our collective name became Walker.
* * * *
We have not yet publioly recognised Bob’s
valuable servioes.
TELSIHC.
The Queen-Bee.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
**Yer gathering ’em in,” said,Larry; “but yer
can’t cop me. I’m the queen-bee, Tommy ! I’m the
queen-bee ! ”
Larry was the chief of a noted gang of horse and
cattle stealers on the Southern line, not far from
the Victorian border, in the late seventies.
Tommy was the sergeant of police in charge of
the neighboring police-station. I was Tommy’s
understrapper, and during the previous year, by a
close attention to the gang’s haunts between
sunset and sunrise, we had succeeded in partly
breaking it up. Larry, however, we could never
catch, and for a merry year or two he had been
responsible for the successful theft of several fine
drafts of horses, and their convevance from
N.S.W. to Queensland.
Larry’s remarks related to the capture (which
we had just effected) of one of the gang, whom
we had caught red-handed operating on an ad-
jacent cookie’s fat steer. We brought him in, and
later on he was duly sentenced to the nearest gaol
for two years.
A month later Larry rode into the barrack-
yard.
“Where’s Tommy?” he asked, excitedly. “I
want a warrant for Dick Danty. He’s mizzled
with my grey mare, and the must be well
on to Mimosa by now.”
Dick Danty was Larry’s first-lieutenant, the
smartest horse-thief of the gang, and as hard to
snap as Larry himself.
I brought Tommy out. Larry laid his infor-
mation, the warrant was issued, and half-an-hour
later we were on Danty’s trail. A two-days’ ride
gave us plenty of time to discuss ways and means
for bringing Larry ultimately to book. It was a
sore point with us, for he took no pains to conceal
his contempt for our efforts, and more than once
he had made us squirm in public by giving a
detailed description of our movements on previous
nights, when, instead of being, as we supposed,
the watchers, we actually turned out to be the
watched.
Four days later we had Danty safely in the
lock-up, and the following day he was committed
to take his trial at the next Circuit Court.
* * * *
“ I had a queer dream last night,” said Tommy
to me one morning about three weeks before the
court was due. “ I dreamt that Larry shook that
grey mare outside, himself. We’ll turn up the
Police Gazette after breakfast.
Accordingly, breakfast over, we worried through
the Gazettes, where, sure enough, we discovered
the identical mare’s description. She was missing
from near S some years previously. A few
inquiries elicited that Larry had been away from
home about that time, and a wire through to the
S man, giving a clear description of the
mare, satisfied us that we had the “ queen-bee ” at
last. We arranged for the secret presence in Y
of the S man and a couple of witnesses, when
the court came on.
* * * *
Court-day. Dick Danty had just been sen-
tenced for stealing the mare from Larry, and
Larry was anxious to regain possession of his
beast, which we had, of course, held till the case
came off.
“Now, Larry,” said Tommy, “does that mare
really belong to you ? ”
“ I’ve sworn to that six times already this morn-
ing,” replied Larry. “ What makes you ask ? ”
“ Beoause I’m going to charge you with stealing
her from ,at S , three years ago, and I
reckon I’ve got the ‘queen-bee’ this trip,” rejoined
Tommy. And we landed Larry into a handy cell.
That afternoon he was taken before the P.M.,
and committed for trial at the court then sitting.’
By noon next day Larry had been tried and sen-
tenced to his 12 months, and I took him up to the
local gaol. Dick Danty was just passing the
inner gate, and had evidently heard the news.
“ What’s he got ? ” he asked.
“Same as yourself, Dick,” I replied, “12
months.”
“ Good for you, old man I I’ll do my stretch on
my blanky head.”
N.S.W.
A.E.W.
Ballarat Chinese have decided to ship all
their paupers, 200 in number, back to China—a
good resolution which other Mongolian colonies
in Australia with a desire for popularity might
imitate.
How Gen. Weyler, the Spanish gentle-
man who set the concentration-camp example in
modern warfare, must chuckle! When it was
Weyler’s work, the British and American press
unanimously denounced the system as barbarous
and unutterably horrible, and the U.S.A. was so
shocked that it went to war on its account. Now
both Britain and U.S.A. have their concentration-
camps—in S’Africa and the Phillipines respec-
tively. As to U.S. official sympathy with Britain
in the S’African war, the fact, of course, is that
both nations being out on a thinly-disguised
mission of plunder neither can afford to criticise 1
the other, as both, in point of motive and methods,
are tarred with the same brush.
If the pomps and vanities of British
militarism are recognised as a fair mark for
ridicule in every country where the haw ! haw !
language of the officah is spoken, what should
German newspapers say about the inane arrogance
of militarism revealed in the recent Blaskowitz
scandal? Lieut. Blaskowitz and Lieut. Hildebrant
were returning to barracks from a supper party,
and the latter, being the less drunk of the pair’
was assisting his gallant comrade to walk straight’.
Therefore Blaskowitz, after the manner of proud
inebriates, smacked his companion on the nose.
It is such an awful thing for one German swash-
buckler to get smitten by another that the colonel
of the regiment summoned a Court of Honor, and
the oourt ordered the two lieutenants to fight a
duel. Practically the colonel and co. were minis-
tering to their own humorless sense of personal
greatness when they ordered the other fellow’s
silly “insult” to be wiped out with blue military
blood. Blaskowitz got killed in the duel, hence
the scandal. The death of either combatant is
calculated to bring duelling into disrepute.

Acting in a gentle manner, they do not (as many other

and eventually leave the system weaker than ever. They,
on the contrary, strengthen and build up the whole
digestive system, purify the blood and tone up the nerves.
A course of BILE BEANS will increase your vitality and
enable you to take work and recreation alike with pleasure.

EDWARD EYRE,
A Kero of Australian Exploration.
Thk death of Edward John Eyre, at Walreddon
Manor, Dorset (Eng.), was announced by cable, the
other day. But he had been dead for many years
to Australia —Australia so little mindful of the
heroic exploits of its own soil —and that he was
still alive in 1901 was only discovered by Mr. J. H.
Maiden, of Sydney, on his recent visit to Europe.
Mr. Maiden had a veneration for the lion-hearted
explorer, and, visiting England, inquired as to his
burial place, so that he might visit the tomb. He i
THE LATE EDWARD JOHN EYRE (aged 8 6).
was much astonished to discover that the ex-
plorer was still, at the age of 86, alive and hearty,
in his retirement. A correspondence passed be-
ween them, and at Mr. Maiden’s request Mr. Eyre
had, after some demur, the photograph, printed
herewith, taken. Some of the astonishing vigor
of the man, shown in his exploring feats,
had evidently survived extreme old age, as the
handwriting—a photographic reproduction of
which is printed here—shows little signs of feeble-
ness. The dominant note of Eyre’s character was
courage. He had the courage of his opinions, the
courage to go through with his resolves to the
very jaws of death. He was of the breed of men
who do things, not talk things ; and in a strenuous
career, whilst there may have been many mistakes,
there was never an instance of mean timidity.
His greatest exploit was overlanding from Ade-
laide to Perth.
When this feat was first mooted in 1840, Eyre
was at once suggested as the leader of the
expedition. He had already shown skill and
intrepidity as a bushman, and he knew the
country away to the north of Port Augusta and to
the west of Port Lincoln. Fate seemed to point
to him as the man to open up an overland route
from Adelaide to Perth. The scheme suggested
King George’s Sound as the point of emergence.
Eyre, whilst not considering the plan practicable,
undertook to lead the expedition and to bear one-
third of the expense. In due course a party was
fitted out; it consisted of Eyre himself, E. B.
Scott (an ex-superintendent of Yatala labor
prison), four other whites, and two native boys ;
and took 13 horses, 40 sheep, and three months’
stores. A further supply was to be sent to the
head of Spencer’s Gulf, by sea. The party set
forth on June 18, 1840.
Eyre’s plan of campaign was simplicity itself —-
he decided to follow up the Flinders Range until
it led him to the centre of the continent, and then
strike a bee-line for the Westralian coast. The
outsetting was marked by glowing enthusiasm.
Up to the head of Spencer’s Gulf the country
passed through was delightful. On July 3 a camp
was made at Mount Brown to wait for the supply
vessel, the Waterwitoh. Two days later she
arrived, and, while the stores were being landed,
Eyre made a reconnoitring trip northward, as the
result of which he had to admit that an attempt
to proceed northward by hugging the ranges was
impossible. This preliminary trip took 15 days.
On his return to the depot at Mount Arden,
Eyre sent back the Waterwitoh, and led
his party over barren, stony plains and
dry water-channels, into the arid wildernesses,
hoping to conquer by another route, trending to
the north-west. This also failed, and at Mount
Hopeless the explorer was forced to turn back.
He next determined on a westerly route, and on
Nov. 3, 1840, the expedition concentrated its
forces at Streaky Bay, the base for the next cam-
paign against the desert. After fruitless efforts
to round the Bight there, Eyre decided to reduce
his staff. He sent back to civilisation all but the
overseer, Scott, one other white man, and two
native boys; and he wrote to the Governor asking
for more assistance in the form of provisions and
forage. He waited for these at the depot for
about six weeks, and then made another attempt
to head the Bight, with success this time, reaching
a point 50 miles beyond it. The country was
still desolate, hopeless, sterile, ridged with sand,
flecked here and there with scrub, waterless for
stages of over 60 miles. At the head of the
Bight, Eyre met with some natives, who guided
him to grass and water; but they told him that
no more water would be found for over 100 miles.
The hinterland from the coast was flat and open,
with low, prickly bushes, solanaceous plants, and
patches of gum scrub and grassed spaces.
Eyre knit his brows and clenched his teeth.
Turn back he would not ! His heart would break
in the hour of defeat. But travel onward he
dared not with so large a party. He resolved to
reduce his mouths yet again. Scott and another
of his men were sent back to the settlements.
Baxter (his overseer) and three native black boys
only were retained.
On the 23rd February the reduced party set
forth on a terrible journey. As they were leaving,
1 a cutter brought letters from the Governor urging
Eyre to return. The dogged hero of the desert
point-blank refused, and set forth on his stony
pilgrimage. On 7th March, 1841, Eyre reached
the head of the Bight with a party almost blinded
with sand and tortured with clouds of huge flies.
The water at which they camped was the last of
which they had knowledge. Then Eyre set forth
again, “ travelling very slowly,” with one of the
native boys (Wylie) and the sheep. Baxter, the
overseer, was directed to follow on with the pack-
horses.
On the third day, at noon, Eyre was some 110
miles from his last water, and the sheep could not
move forward an inch. Perforce, he left them
behind, and, with them, a note to Baxter to hurry
on with the horses, whilst he (Eyre) pressed ahead
in search of water. After terrible suffering,
Eyre and his black boy reached the edge of the
cliffs on the 11th of March—but of water there
was none. The baffled and thirst-tortured ex-
plorer pushed on for seven miles in despair,
until a break in the rocks, with sand-drift between,
gave water in plenty. The journey through the
desert had lasted five days, and they had traversed
135 miles. At this oasis the party stayed six days.
Resuming the journey, Eyre, after two days,
struck a grassy patch, but no water could be
found. The stock could not last out another long
stage. The stores were therefore buried, and the
overseer was sent back some 40 miles for
water, while Eyre stayed and guarded the
sheep (now reduced to three). The total quantity
of flour in camp was only 1421b5. When
the overseer returned with fresh water everything
possible was dispensed with, and a sheep was
killed for food. The horses began to fall down
from exhaustion as the journey was pursued ; they
had had no water for five days, and those which
could manage to struggle along followed the men
like dogs. Baxter, the overseer, broke down
utterly, and implored to be allowed to return to
Fowler’s Bay. Eyre was stony to his beseeching.
He determined to conquer, or die in his tracks.
On the 30th of March, and 160 miles from
their last water, Eyre struck the saving fluid at
a depth of five feet in some sandhills.
The horses plucked up spirit, a sheep was killed
for food, and the leader shot a wallaby. Baxter
was sent back from this place for some of the
buried stores. He was absent for 10 days. King
George’s Sound was about 650 miles distant, and
there were provisions only sufficient to last three
weeks, with care. Baxter continued to plead for
permission to return. His chief sternly refused,
though himself fish-poisoned and feeble. A horse
was slaughtered for food, and all the party
sickened from eating of the carcase. Two of the
black boys, however, stole a large quantity of the
meat, and their rations were reduced as a punish-
ment. They deserted the camp. Then the last
sheep was killed, and the dreary tramp resumed.
On the 26th April everything, except pro-
visions, arms, ammunition, and the clothes worn
by the party, was discarded. On the first day 15
miles, on the second day 19 miles were gained.
In the evening the horses were hobbled and turned
out to feed. They strayed away, and between 10
and 11 o’clock Eyre went out to look for them.
Coming back he heard the report of a gun, and
reaching camp found the faithful Baxter shot
through the chest. The murderers were the two
black boys who had deserted. Baxter expired in
Eyre’s arms. All the weapons and most of the
stores had disappeared. The next morning
Baxter’s body was wrapped in a blanket and
covered with leaves—all that was possible, since
the earth was as iron. The provisions left in the
camp by the thieves amounted to about 401 b. of
flour, four gallons of water, and a little tea and
sugar.
Still not daunted, Eyre plunged forward on his
tramo of some 600 miles, moving at the rate of
about 10 miles a day, with frequent rests to hus-
band his strength. The two disaffected blacks
followed in his track, trying to entice Wylie
away. Then they, too, faded into the blur of the
rear, probably perishing in the hungry desert.
The horses had had no water for five days ; 150
miles of rocky tableland had been traversed
without a single drink. On the 2nd May a native
well was found and the horses heartened some-
what. Eight days’ rest, and a horse was
slaughtered for food and his flesh dried —then
once more on the track ! From this point the
country improved, and water was easily obtained ;
but Eyre and Wylie were deadly sick from the
effects of eating diseased horse meat. They could
do no better than crawl forward. One day a
native dog carried off some fourteen pounds of
their rotten food, and the outlook was black
indeed. But a day later grassed country was
struck, and Eyre shot a kangaroo. On the 19th
May only four miles were made, but water and
grass were found, so Eyre and his black boy
rested and refreshed for six days, feeding on kan-
garoos, fish and crabs.
Once again the sturdy-hearted tramps resumed
their treadmill path to victory. On May 26 water
was obtainable, but of game there was none. By-
and-by the travellers were reduced to a few
spoonfuls of flour and water. Some reeds
and roots were greedily chewed. Eyre and
Wylie now indeed lived upon anything that
crept or grew. After an aeon of hideous
suffering, the poor wretches made the sea, and
there, to their joy unutterable, rode at anchor a
barque. It was a French whaler, the Mississippi,
commanded by Captain Rossiter—after whom
Eyre gratefully designated the bay. A boat put
off and took the wayfarers on board. There for
some time they rested. With new clothes and
fresh provisions, they once more faced the desert,
and in 12 days reached Cape Barren, 150 miles
from Rossiter Bay. On 7th July they reached
Albany, and Eyre had won.
The biggest cargo ever taken from Aus-
tralia to England is that of the Suevic on her
current voyage. It includes 21,518 bales of wool
and 74,000 frozen sheep.
What the mercantile marine is coming to.
Capt. Salter, of the barque Ancenis, lately arrived
in Hobson’s Bay, reported that on leaving New
York he found that of the “ able seamen” supplied
him by the boarding-master, eight were capable
men, but of the others one had never seen a ship
before, another was an artist who had no
knowledge of the duties, the third was a machinist,
the fourth an actor, the fifth and sixth were
soldiers, and last of all came a cowboy.
